Prophethood
beginner
5 min
Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets: The Objection of Jesus' Descent Answered
The author answers an objection about Jesus to prove Muhammad's sealing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Jesus' future descent does not contradict Muhammad's finality as the Seal of the Prophets.

Core Idea
Jesus will descend as a follower of Muhammad's law, not as a new prophet.

One common objection to Muhammad's finality is that Jesus is still alive and will return. The author anticipates this and provides a clear answer using a prophetic tradition about Moses. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it reinforces Muhammad's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objection about Jesus being alive and descending. Understand why someone might think this challenges Muhammad's sealing.
The objection assumes that a later prophet would supersede the previous one.
2
Examine the answer
The author concedes Jesus is alive but states he will follow Muhammad's sharia. Note the use of the hadith about Moses to reinforce the point.
The key is that Jesus will not bring a new law.
3
Understand the definition of 'Seal'
The passage defines 'Seal of the Prophets' as no prophet sent after him. Reflect on how the answer fits this definition.
The definition is explicit: لا يبعث نبي بعده.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This objection and answer is part of the proof that Muhammad is the best prophet. The answer preserves the finality while acknowledging Jesus' unique role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمحمد (عليه السلام والاتباع لشريعته لما فيهما من البشارة ببعثته، والإنباء عن الاهتداء بمتابعته فإن قيل : أليس عيسى عليه السلام) حياً بعد نبينا، رفع إلى السماء، وسينزل إلى الدنيا؟ قلنا بلى ولكنه على شريعة نبينا، لا يسعه إلا اتباعه على ما قال (عليه السلام) في حق موسى أنه لو كان حياً لما وسعه إلا اتباعي، فيصح أنه خاتم الأنبياء، بمعنى أنه لا يبعث نبي بعده، وأجمع المسلمون على أن أفضل الأنبياء محمد، لأن أمته خير الأمم لقوله تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلناسِ ) (۱) ﴿وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَاكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطَا (٢). وتفضيل الأمة من حيث إنها أمة تفضيل للرسول الذي هم أمته، ولأنه مبعوث إلى الثقلين، وخاتم الأنبياء والرسل ومعجزته الظاهرة الباهرة باقية على وجه الزمان، وشريعته ناسخة لجميع الأديان، وشهادته قائمة في القيامة على كافة البشر، الى غير ذلك من خصائص لا تعد ولا تحصى. وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَفعَ بعضهم دَرَجَاتِ ﴾ (۳) . إشارة إلى ذلك، والأحاديث الصحاح في هذا المعنى كثيرة، حتى قال (عليه السلام : أنا أكرم الأولين والآخرين على الله تعالى ولا فخر. كما قال (عليه السلام): لا تخيروني على موسى، وما ينبغي لعبد أن يقول : إني خير من يونس بن متى تواضع منه .
With Muhammad (peace be upon him) and following his law, because in them is the announcement of his mission and the indication of guidance through following him. If it is said: Is not Jesus (peace be upon him) alive after our Prophet, raised to heaven, and will descend to the world? We say: Yes, but he is upon the law of our Prophet; he cannot but follow him, as he (peace be upon him) said regarding Moses: if he were alive, he would have no choice but to follow me. Thus it is correct that he is the Seal of the Prophets, meaning that no prophet will be sent after him. And the Muslims have unanimously agreed that the best of the prophets is Muhammad, because his community is the best of communities, as Allah says: "You are the best community brought forth for mankind" (3:110) and "Thus We have made you a middle community" (2:143). And the preference of the community, inasmuch as it is a community, is a preference for the Messenger whose community they are, and because he is sent to the two weighty ones (jinn and humans), and the Seal of the Prophets and Messengers, and his manifest, dazzling miracle remains throughout time, and his law abrogates all religions, and his testimony stands on the Day of Resurrection against all human beings, and other countless particularities. And His statement: "And We raised some of them in degrees" (2:253) refers to that. And the authentic hadiths on this meaning are many, until he (peace be upon him) said: "I am the most honored of the first and the last before Allah Almighty, and I do not boast." And he (peace be upon him) said: "Do not give me superiority over Moses, and it is not fitting for a servant to say: I am better than Jonah son of Matta" — out of humility from him.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection structure affirming that Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets and the best of them. It begins by establishing that following Muhammad’s law is tied to the glad tidings of his mission. The author anticipates an objection: Is not Jesus still alive, raised to heaven, and will descend later? The answer is yes, but Jesus will descend as a follower of Muhammad’s sharia, not as a new prophet. The author supports this with a prophetic statement about Moses — that if Moses were alive, he would have to follow Muhammad. This proves that Muhammad is the Seal, meaning no prophet comes after him. The passage then cites the consensus of Muslims that Muhammad is the best prophet, because his community is the best, as per Quranic verses. The preference of the community implies preference of the messenger. Additional proofs include that he was sent to both jinn and humans, his miracle (the Quran) remains, his law abrogates all others, and his testimony on Judgment Day covers all. Finally, it mentions authentic hadiths where the Prophet humbly acknowledges his status without boasting, and even forbids preferring him over Moses or Jonah, which is itself a sign of his humility and virtue.

Key Terms
خاتم الأنبياء
شريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Jesus' return means he supercedes Muhammad or that prophethood continues after Muhammad.
Correction: The author clarifies that Jesus returns as a follower of Muhammad's law, not as a new prophet, preserving Muhammad's finality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Jesus’ future descent not contradict Muhammad being the Seal of the Prophets?
A) Because Jesus will not descend at all.
B) Because Jesus will descend as a follower of Muhammad’s law, not as a new prophet.
C) Because Jesus is not a prophet.
D) Because Muhammad will also descend again.

Key Takeaway
Jesus will return as a follower of Muhammad's sharia, so he does not break Muhammad's sealing of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that even a prophet like Jesus will follow Muhammad's law, does your daily life reflect the finality of Islam? In what area do you still act as if other guidance is needed?
?
What would change in your heart if you absolutely knew that no new prophet or revelation will ever come—and that everything you need for guidance is already in the Quran and Sunnah?
?
Do you ever secretly wish for a new prophet to solve modern problems? How does this lesson challenge that desire and call you to submit fully to Muhammad's final message?

Prophethood

The Best of Prophets: Muhammad's Superiority and Its Proofs
The author cites consensus and multiple proofs for Muhammad's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus and multiple proofs that establish Muhammad as the best of all prophets.

Core Idea
The best community implies the best messenger.

Having established Muhammad's finality, the author now turns to his superiority. Citing Quranic verses, hadiths, and logical arguments, the passage shows why Muslims unanimously hold Muhammad as the greatest prophet. This lesson unpacks those evidences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of consensus
The passage starts with 'وأجمع المسلمون' — the consensus of Muslims. Note that this is a strong source of authority in Islamic theology.
Consensus (ijma) is considered a proof.
2
Analyze the first proof: best community
The author argues that because Muhammad's community is the best (Quran 3:110, 2:143), the messenger is also the best. Understand this logical connection.
The community's virtue reflects on the messenger.
3
Examine the additional proofs
The passage lists several other proofs: sent to jinn and humans, seal of prophets, eternal miracle, abrogating law, testimony on Judgment Day. Recognize each as a unique distinction.
These are specific merits not shared by other prophets.
4
Reflect on the hadiths about humility
The author includes hadiths where the Prophet humbly says not to prefer him over Moses or Jonah. Understand that this humility itself is a sign of his greatness.
True greatness often comes with humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمحمد (عليه السلام والاتباع لشريعته لما فيهما من البشارة ببعثته، والإنباء عن الاهتداء بمتابعته فإن قيل : أليس عيسى عليه السلام) حياً بعد نبينا، رفع إلى السماء، وسينزل إلى الدنيا؟ قلنا بلى ولكنه على شريعة نبينا، لا يسعه إلا اتباعه على ما قال (عليه السلام) في حق موسى أنه لو كان حياً لما وسعه إلا اتباعي، فيصح أنه خاتم الأنبياء، بمعنى أنه لا يبعث نبي بعده، وأجمع المسلمون على أن أفضل الأنبياء محمد، لأن أمته خير الأمم لقوله تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلناسِ ) (۱) ﴿وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَاكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطَا (٢). وتفضيل الأمة من حيث إنها أمة تفضيل للرسول الذي هم أمته، ولأنه مبعوث إلى الثقلين، وخاتم الأنبياء والرسل ومعجزته الظاهرة الباهرة باقية على وجه الزمان، وشريعته ناسخة لجميع الأديان، وشهادته قائمة في القيامة على كافة البشر، الى غير ذلك من خصائص لا تعد ولا تحصى. وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَفعَ بعضهم دَرَجَاتِ ﴾ (۳) . إشارة إلى ذلك، والأحاديث الصحاح في هذا المعنى كثيرة، حتى قال (عليه السلام : أنا أكرم الأولين والآخرين على الله تعالى ولا فخر. كما قال (عليه السلام): لا تخيروني على موسى، وما ينبغي لعبد أن يقول : إني خير من يونس بن متى تواضع منه .
With Muhammad (peace be upon him) and following his law, because in them is the announcement of his mission and the indication of guidance through following him. If it is said: Is not Jesus (peace be upon him) alive after our Prophet, raised to heaven, and will descend to the world? We say: Yes, but he is upon the law of our Prophet; he cannot but follow him, as he (peace be upon him) said regarding Moses: if he were alive, he would have no choice but to follow me. Thus it is correct that he is the Seal of the Prophets, meaning that no prophet will be sent after him. And the Muslims have unanimously agreed that the best of the prophets is Muhammad, because his community is the best of communities, as Allah says: "You are the best community brought forth for mankind" (3:110) and "Thus We have made you a middle community" (2:143). And the preference of the community, inasmuch as it is a community, is a preference for the Messenger whose community they are, and because he is sent to the two weighty ones (jinn and humans), and the Seal of the Prophets and Messengers, and his manifest, dazzling miracle remains throughout time, and his law abrogates all religions, and his testimony stands on the Day of Resurrection against all human beings, and other countless particularities. And His statement: "And We raised some of them in degrees" (2:253) refers to that. And the authentic hadiths on this meaning are many, until he (peace be upon him) said: "I am the most honored of the first and the last before Allah Almighty, and I do not boast." And he (peace be upon him) said: "Do not give me superiority over Moses, and it is not fitting for a servant to say: I am better than Jonah son of Matta" — out of humility from him.

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection structure affirming that Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets and the best of them. It begins by establishing that following Muhammad’s law is tied to the glad tidings of his mission. The author anticipates an objection: Is not Jesus still alive, raised to heaven, and will descend later? Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Prophet's command not to prefer him over Moses means he is not superior.
Correction: The author explains that this command was out of humility, not denial of superiority. Muhammad is still the best, but he taught modesty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first reason given for Muhammad being the best prophet?
A) Because he was sent to both jinn and humans.
B) Because his community is the best community.
C) Because his miracle remains eternal.
D) Because he is the Seal of the Prophets.

Key Takeaway
Muhammad is the best prophet because his community is the best, and he has unique distinctions like being sent to all beings, having an eternal miracle, and abrogating all previous laws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to follow the best of prophets, are you actually living in a way that honors his superiority? What specific area of your life falls short of the example he set?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) showed profound humility even while being the most honored before Allah. What pride or arrogance in your heart do you need to let go of today to truly follow his path?
?
If the Prophet will testify on the Day of Resurrection against all people, what would his testimony be about you? What personal change must you make now so that his testimony is in your favor?

Best Prophet After Muhammad: Scholarly Opinions
Enumeration of opinions on the best prophet after Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly opinions on which prophet is best after Muhammad.

Core Idea
Scholars differ on the best prophet after Muhammad, each citing a unique virtue.

This passage lists five opinions among scholars regarding the most excellent prophet after Muhammad. Each opinion highlights a different prophet's distinguishing characteristic, from Adam's fatherhood to Jesus' unique station as Spirit of Allah. The author presents these views as a prelude to discussing the Christian claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage listing opinions on the best prophet after Muhammad. Note the structure: each opinion begins with 'قيل' (it was said) and gives a reason.
The reasons are based on distinctive virtues.
2
Identify each prophet and reason
List the five prophets mentioned and the specific reason given for each being considered best.
3
Reflect on the diversity
Consider why scholars might differ: each virtue is important, but which is greatest? This sets up the Christian claim for Jesus.
This is a common topic in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في الأفضل بعده، فقيل : آدم ، لكونه أبا البشر. وقيل: نوح لطول عبادته ومجاهدته . وقيل : إبراهيم لزيادة توكله واطمئنانه . وقيل : موسى، لكونه كليم الله ونجيه . وقيل : عيسى، لكونه روح الله وصفيه . وفضله النصارى على الكل بأنه كلمة ألقاها إلى مريم وروح منه ، طاهر مقدس ، لم يخلق من نطفة، وقد ولدته سيدة نساء العالمين المطهرة عن الأدناس، وتربى في حجر الأنبياء والأولياء، وتكلم في المهد بعبودية نفسه، وربوبية الله، لم يخل زماناً من التوحيد والشرائع، ولم يلتفت إلى زخارف الدنيا، ولم يستمتع بلذاتها، ولم يدخر قوت يوم، ولم يسع في هلاك نفس أو سبيها ، أو استرقاقها، ولا فى أخذ مال ولا ولد، ولا إيذاء لأحد، معجزاته من إحياء الموتى، وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص أبهر المعجزات وأشهرها، ثم في السماء، ومن زمرة الأحياء، ونبوته مما اتفق عليها ذوو الآراء، واعترف بها خاتم الأنبياء .
And they differed concerning who is best after him. It was said: Adam, because he is the father of mankind. And it was said: Noah, due to the length of his worship and his struggle. And it was said: Abraham, due to his increased trust and tranquility. And it was said: Moses, because he is the interlocutor of Allah and His intimate. And it was said: Jesus, because he is the Spirit of Allah and His chosen one. And the Christians gave him superiority over all — that he is a word cast to Mary and a spirit from Him, pure and holy, not created from a sperm-drop; the chief of the women of the worlds, purified from impurities, gave birth to him; he was raised in the lap of prophets and saints; he spoke in the cradle about his own servitude and the lordship of Allah; no time was devoid of monotheism and laws; he did not turn to the adornments of this world, nor did he enjoy its pleasures; he did not store up food for a day; he did not seek the destruction of any soul, its captivity, or enslavement; nor did he take wealth or children, nor harm anyone; his miracles — reviving the dead, healing the blind and leprous — are the most astounding and famous of miracles; then he is in heaven and among the living; and his prophethood is agreed upon by those of opinion, and the Seal of the Prophets acknowledged it.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of scholarly opinions on which prophet is best after Muhammad, followed by the Christian argument for Jesus’ superiority. The author lists five opinions: Adam (as father of humanity), Noah (long worship and struggle), Abraham (trust and tranquility), Moses (interlocutor of Allah), and Jesus (Spirit of Allah and chosen). Then the Christians’ detailed argument is presented: Jesus is the word and spirit from Allah, born without a father from the purest woman, raised among prophets, spoke in the cradle, always observed monotheism and law, rejected worldly pleasures, never harmed anyone, performed the greatest miracles including raising the dead, and is now alive in heaven. Even the Seal of the Prophets acknowledged his prophethood. The author presents this as a preamble to a refutation.

Key Terms
أفضل الأنبياء
آدم
نوح
إبراهيم
موسى
عيسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Abraham considered the best prophet after Muhammad by some?
A) Because he is the father of mankind
B) Due to his increased trust and tranquility
C) Because he is the interlocutor of Allah
D) Due to his long worship and struggle

Key Takeaway
Scholars offer different reasons for which prophet is best after Muhammad, each highlighting a unique virtue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars gave reasons for each prophet's superiority. Look at your own life: which of these virtues—worship, struggle, trust, intimacy with Allah, or being chosen—do you lack most? What is one concrete step you will take today to cultivate that virtue?
?
If you were asked to defend one prophet as best after Muhammad based on this passage, which reason would you choose and why does that reason expose a deficiency in your own relationship with Allah?
?
The passage reports multiple opinions without favoring any. How does your own ego react when you encounter differing Islamic opinions? Do you seek truth humbly or cling to your own preference? What is one area where you need to submit to scholarly disagreement?

The Christian Argument for Jesus’ Superiority
Detailed Christian argument for Jesus' superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Christian argument for Jesus’ superiority over all prophets as presented in this text.

Core Idea
Christians argue Jesus is superior because of his miraculous birth, sinless life, and unparalleled miracles.

This passage presents the Christian case for Jesus’ superiority over all other prophets. It lists numerous qualities: his origin as the Word and Spirit of God, his virgin birth, his sinless life, his rejection of worldly pleasures, his great miracles, and his continued existence in heaven. The author includes this detailed argument before refuting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Christian argument
Read the Arabic passage describing why Christians consider Jesus superior. Notice the extensive list of virtues.
This is a detailed theological argument.
2
Identify the key claims
List the main points: Jesus is God's Word and Spirit, born without a father from Mary, raised among prophets, spoke in the cradle, lived ascetically, performed great miracles, and is now alive in heaven.
The passage emphasizes his purity and lack of worldly attachment.
3
Understand the rhetorical purpose
Recognize that the author is presenting the Christians' view as a precursor to a refutation. The argument is not endorsed but described.
This sets up the author's response.
4
Reflect on the final point
The passage ends by stating that even the Seal of the Prophets (Muhammad) acknowledged Jesus' prophethood, implying consensus.
This strengthens the Christian argument from an Islamic perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في الأفضل بعده، فقيل : آدم ، لكونه أبا البشر. وقيل: نوح لطول عبادته ومجاهدته . وقيل : إبراهيم لزيادة توكله واطمئنانه . وقيل : موسى، لكونه كليم الله ونجيه . وقيل : عيسى، لكونه روح الله وصفيه . وفضله النصارى على الكل بأنه كلمة ألقاها إلى مريم وروح منه ، طاهر مقدس ، لم يخلق من نطفة، وقد ولدته سيدة نساء العالمين المطهرة عن الأدناس، وتربى في حجر الأنبياء والأولياء، وتكلم في المهد بعبودية نفسه، وربوبية الله، لم يخل زماناً من التوحيد والشرائع، ولم يلتفت إلى زخارف الدنيا، ولم يستمتع بلذاتها، ولم يدخر قوت يوم، ولم يسع في هلاك نفس أو سبيها ، أو استرقاقها، ولا فى أخذ مال ولا ولد، ولا إيذاء لأحد، معجزاته من إحياء الموتى، وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص أبهر المعجزات وأشهرها، ثم في السماء، ومن زمرة الأحياء، ونبوته مما اتفق عليها ذوو الآراء، واعترف بها خاتم الأنبياء .
And they differed concerning who is best after him. It was said: Adam, because he is the father of mankind. And it was said: Noah, due to the length of his worship and his struggle. And it was said: Abraham, due to his increased trust and tranquility. And it was said: Moses, because he is the interlocutor of Allah and His intimate. And it was said: Jesus, because he is the Spirit of Allah and His chosen one. And the Christians gave him superiority over all — that he is a word cast to Mary and a spirit from Him, pure and holy, not created from a sperm-drop; the chief of the women of the worlds, purified from impurities, gave birth to him; he was raised in the lap of prophets and saints; he spoke in the cradle about his own servitude and the lordship of Allah; no time was devoid of monotheism and laws; he did not turn to the adornments of this world, nor did he enjoy its pleasures; he did not store up food for a day; he did not seek the destruction of any soul, its captivity, or enslavement; nor did he take wealth or children, nor harm anyone; his miracles — reviving the dead, healing the blind and leprous — are the most astounding and famous of miracles; then he is in heaven and among the living; and his prophethood is agreed upon by those of opinion, and the Seal of the Prophets acknowledged it.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of scholarly opinions on which prophet is best after Muhammad, followed by the Christian argument for Jesus’ superiority. The author lists five opinions: Adam (as father of humanity), Noah (long worship and struggle), Abraham (trust and tranquility), Moses (interlocutor of Allah), and Jesus (Spirit of Allah and chosen). Then the Christians’ detailed argument is presented: Jesus is the word and spirit from Allah, born without a father from the purest woman, raised among prophets, spoke in the cradle, always observed monotheism and law, rejected worldly pleasures, never harmed anyone, performed the greatest miracles including raising the dead, and is now alive in heaven. Even the Seal of the Prophets acknowledged his prophethood. The author presents this as a preamble to a refutation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is endorsing the Christian argument as correct.
Correction: The author is presenting the Christian view as a prelude to a refutation, not as his own belief.

Checkpoint
According to the Christian argument in this passage, which of the following is NOT a reason for Jesus' superiority?
A) He is the Word of God
B) He performed miracles
C) He was born in a normal manner
D) He is alive in heaven

Key Takeaway
The Christians’ argument for Jesus’ superiority lists his unique origins, sinless life, great miracles, and acknowledgment by Muhammad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Christian arguments for Jesus' superiority, do you feel any subtle admiration for their perspective? How does the fact that Jesus himself declared his servitude in the cradle correct any tendency to elevate a prophet beyond his status?
?
If someone today used the same arguments (miracles, pure birth, sinless life) to claim that Jesus is superior to all prophets, how would you respond using the exact words from this passage about Jesus’ own testimony?
?
Do you ever, even unintentionally, attribute a status to a human being that belongs only to Allah? What specific quality of Jesus mentioned in this passage (e.g., 'he did not store up food for a day') challenges the modern love of material accumulation in your own life?

Inverting Christian Arguments for Muhammad's Virtue
Author lists three inverted proofs for Muhammad’s virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author turns the Christians’ own praises of Jesus into proofs of Muhammad’s superior virtue.

Core Idea
What seems to favor Jesus actually proves Muhammad’s greater virtue.

The author answers the Christian argument by taking the very points they use to praise Jesus and showing that they apply even more strongly to Prophet Muhammad. In this passage, he gives three examples: birth from polytheists, devotion to jihad, and balancing worldly order with spiritual absorption. This approach reframes the debate entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get the rhythm of the argument. Notice the three examples starting with 'ka'.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the three inverted arguments
The author lists three points: birth from polytheists, jihad against polytheists, and managing world affairs while absorbed in devotion. Compare each to Jesus’ situation.
3
Understand the rhetorical strategy
The author does not deny the Christian points but shows they are even truer for Muhammad. This is a powerful refutation technique.
This is a common kalam tactic: turning the opponent’s evidence against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن البعض من ذلك حجة لنا وشاهد بفضل نبينا، كالولادة من المشركين والمشركات والتربي في حجرهم، مع المواظبة على التوحيد والطاعات، وكالإقبال على الجهاد وقمع المشركين وقهر أعداء الدين، وكالقيام بمصالح نظام العالم مع الاستغراق في التوجه إلى جناب القدس . وأما معجزاته، فإنما اشتهر تلك الشهرة بإخبار من نبينا وكتابه، مع ذلك فأين من من معجزاته؟ ثم الكون ميتاً في الأرض أنفع للأمة من الكون حياً في السماء، حيث صارت الروضة المقدسة مهبطاً للبركات، ومصعداً للدعوات، وموطناً للاجتماع على الطاعات .. إلى غير ذلك من أنواع الخيرات . . ونبوة محمد ( صلى الله عليه وسلم) مما نطق به العجراء، وشهد به رب الأرض والسماء واتفق عليه من سبقه من الأنبياء، وخصائصه مما لا يضبطه العد والإحصاء وقد أشرقت الأرض بنورها إشراق الشمس في كبد السماء. فصياح الخصماء نباح الكلاب في الليلة القمراء
And the answer: That some of that is an argument for us and a witness to the virtue of our Prophet — such as birth from polytheist men and women and being raised in their laps while adhering to monotheism and acts of obedience; and such as engaging in jihad, suppressing polytheists, and overcoming the enemies of religion; and such as undertaking the interests of the order of the world while being absorbed in turning to the Holy Presence. As for his miracles, they only became that famous through the report of our Prophet and his Book; nevertheless, where are they compared to his miracles? Then being dead in the earth is more beneficial to the community than being alive in heaven, because the sacred garden (Rawda) became a place of descent for blessings, an ascent for supplications, and a meeting place for acts of obedience... and other kinds of good things. And the prophethood of Muhammad (peace and blessings be upon him) is what the barren rock spoke of, and the Lord of the heavens and earth witnessed, and the prophets before him agreed upon. His particularities cannot be counted or enumerated. The earth has shone with his light as the sun shines in the midst of the sky. The clamor of opponents is like the barking of dogs on a moonlit night.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian argument for Jesus’ superiority. The author responds by flipping the Christians’ own points into proofs for Muhammad’s virtue. For example, Jesus was born from a pure virgin mother, but Muhammad was born from polytheists yet remained steadfast in monotheism — that is greater. Jesus did not engage in worldly struggle, but Muhammad combined spiritual devotion with managing the world and jihad. As for miracles, Jesus’ miracles gained fame only through Muhammad’s report and the Quran. Moreover, Muhammad’s death and burial in Medina is more beneficial for the community than Jesus being alive in heaven, because the Prophet’s grave (Rawda) is a source of blessings, a place for prayers and gatherings. The passage then asserts that Muhammad’s prophethood is affirmed by the Quran, Allah, and earlier prophets, and his virtues are beyond count. The final simile dismisses opponents’ objections as meaningless noise.

Key Terms
الجواب
الولادة
المواظبة
الجهاد
الاستغراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christians' praise of Jesus is unanswerable and proves his superiority.
Correction: Their own points can be inverted to show Muhammad's greater virtue, as demonstrated by the examples.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the inverted proofs for Muhammad?
A) Birth from polytheists while adhering to monotheism
B) Engaging in jihad and suppressing polytheists
C) Performing miracles of healing the blind and leper
D) Managing world affairs while absorbed in devotion

Key Takeaway
The author turns Christians’ own arguments into proofs for Muhammad’s superior virtue using three inverted examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You were born into a family that may not share your faith or values. Are you, like Muhammad, 'adhering to monotheism and acts of obedience' despite that environment? What specific compromises have you made that you need to abandon today?
?
Muhammad managed the affairs of the world while being fully absorbed in turning to Allah. Which worldly responsibility do you use as an excuse to neglect your spiritual devotion? Write down one concrete change you will make this week to balance both.
?
If you were in a situation where your faith required active struggle (jihad of any kind), would you step up? Or do you prefer passive comfort? Look at the example of the Prophet: he engaged in jihad and overcame enemies of the religion. What enemy of truth in your own life are you avoiding confronting?

Miracles and the Superior Benefit of Muhammad's Death
Author argues for the superiority of Muhammad's miracles and the benefit of his death.

Muhammad’s death brought greater blessings to his community than Jesus’ life.

After the inverted arguments, the author turns to miracles and the unique benefit of the Prophet’s burial place. He argues that Jesus’ miracles gained fame only through Muhammad’s report, and that the Prophet's grave in Medina (the Rawda) is a source of immense blessings and community bonding.

The Reality and Proof of the Prophet's Ascension
The author establishes the Mi'raj as bodily and wakeful, and discusses the chain of transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three levels of proof for the Prophet's Ascension (Mi'raj) and why a merely spiritual interpretation is refuted by the intensity of Quraysh denial.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj is bodily and wakeful; the strength of disbelievers' denial proves it.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a graded classification of the evidence for the Mi'raj. The author begins with the most certain proof—the Quran—for the journey to the Aqsa Mosque, then moves to widespread reports for the ascent to the heavens, and finally to single-transmitter reports for the higher realms. He then addresses potential objections and offers a powerful argument from the reaction of the Quraysh polytheists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own classification come through.
Notice the three distinct 'then' (ثم) transitions.
2
Identify the three tiers of evidence
The author divides the Mi'raj into stages: to Aqsa Mosque (by Quran), to heaven (by widespread report), and beyond (by single report). Understand how each level of proof corresponds to a part of the journey.
The consensus of the second generation supports the bodily, wakeful nature.
3
Examine the objections and the final argument
Consider the reports from Aisha and Muawiya that might suggest a non-physical event. Then see how the author uses the disbelievers' strong denial to argue for physical reality.
This is a key theological move: if it were a dream, why would they deny it so vehemently?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال خاتمة (معراج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ) إلى المسجد الأقصى ثابت بالكتاب (۱) وهو في اليقظة وبالجسد بإجماع القرن الثاني، ثم إلى السماء بالخير المستفيض، ثم إلى الجنة أو إلى العرش أو إلى طرف العالم بخبر الواحد، وما روي عن عائشة (رضي عنها أنها قالت: والله ما فقدت جسد رسول الله ) . وعن معاوية أنها كانت رؤيا صالحة، لا يعارض ما ذكرنا على أنه لو ادعى المعراج للروح أو في المنام لما نكره الكفار غاية الإنكار ) .
The Conclusion said: The Ascension (Mi‘raj) of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) to the Aqsa Mosque is established by the Book (Quran), and it was in wakefulness and with the body by consensus of the second generation (of Muslims). Then (the ascension) to the heaven is established by widespread report (khabar mustafid). Then (the ascension) to Paradise, or to the Throne, or to the edge of the universe is established by a single-transmitter report (khabar al-wahid). And what is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said: "By Allah, I did not miss the body of the Messenger of Allah" — and from Mu‘awiya that it was a righteous dream — does not contradict what we have mentioned. Moreover, if one claimed that the Ascension was spiritual or in a dream, the disbelievers would not have denied it so vehemently.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal classification of the proofs for the Prophet’s Ascension (Mi‘raj). It begins by stating that the Mi‘raj to the Aqsa Mosque (Isra) is proven by the Quran directly. It was bodily and in wakefulness, based on the consensus of the second generation (Tab‘un or early scholars). The ascension from there to the heavens is proven by widespread transmission (khabar mustafid), which is a level below Quran but still strong. The ascension to Paradise, the Throne, or the edge of the universe is based on single-transmitter reports (khabar al-wahid), which are acceptable in creedal matters. The passage then addresses objections: the reports from Aisha (that she did not miss the Prophet’s body) and Mu‘awiya (that it was a dream) do not contradict the majority view. Finally, an argument is given: if the Mi‘raj were merely spiritual or a dream, the Quraysh polytheists would not have denied it so strongly, because such claims would have been less objectionable. This shows the Mi‘raj must have been a physical, wakeful event.

Key Terms
معراج
المسجد الأقصى
القرآن
إجماع القرن الثاني
الخبر المستفيض
خبر الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mi'raj was only a spiritual experience or a dream.
Correction: The passage argues that if it were spiritual or a dream, the Quraysh would not have denied it so intensely; their strong denial indicates it was a physical, wakeful event.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what level of proof establishes the Mi'raj to the heavens?
A) The Quran itself
B) Consensus of the second generation
C) Widespread report (khabar mustafid)
D) Single-transmitter report (khabar al-wahid)

Key Takeaway
The Mi'raj is a physical, wakeful event with graded proofs: Quran for the journey to Aqsa, widespread reports for the heavens, and single reports for higher realms, and the disbelievers' denial confirms its physical reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The disbelievers denied the Mi'raj so intensely because it was a physical, tangible event. In what area of your life have you been 'denying' a truth that demands bodily action, not just mental agreement? What would it cost you to live as if that truth were physically real?
?
The Prophet's journey was witnessed by his body and soul together. Is your faith merely a spiritual feeling, or does it affect your physical choices—where you go, what you do with your hands? What one physical habit could you change today to make your faith more 'wakeful'?
?
If the Quraysh had only been told of a dream, they would have shrugged. The strength of their rejection proved the event was real. When you face rejection of your faith, do you see it as a sign that you are standing for something real? How does this lesson change your view of opposition?

Defending the Prophet's Virtues: Responding to the Birth Objection
The author responds to an objection about the Prophet's birth among polytheists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the Prophet's virtues against the objection of his upbringing among polytheists.

Core Idea
The Prophet's birth among polytheists highlights his extraordinary devotion.

This passage is a direct response to those who question the Prophet's status due to his birth among polytheists. The author turns this apparent weakness into a sign of his merit, showing how his steadfastness in tawhid despite his environment is proof of his prophethood. We will examine his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical style, especially at the end.
2
Identify the key arguments
The author lists several points: birth among polytheists as a merit, the fame of his miracles, the benefit of his death, and testimony to his prophethood. Identify each one.
3
Analyze the rhetorical conclusion
Pay attention to the final sentence comparing opponents to barking dogs. What effect does this have on the argument?
This dismissal reinforces the author's confidence.
4
Reflect on the transformation
Consider how the author turns a potential criticism into a proof of prophethood. Why is this effective?
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن البعض من ذلك حجة لنا وشاهد بفضل نبينا، كالولادة من المشركين والمشركات والتربي في حجرهم، مع المواظبة على التوحيد والطاعات، وكالإقبال على الجهاد وقمع المشركين وقهر أعداء الدين، وكالقيام بمصالح نظام العالم مع الاستغراق في التوجه إلى جناب القدس . وأما معجزاته، فإنما اشتهر تلك الشهرة بإخبار من نبينا وكتابه، مع ذلك فأين من من معجزاته؟ ثم الكون ميتاً في الأرض أنفع للأمة من الكون حياً في السماء، حيث صارت الروضة المقدسة مهبطاً للبركات، ومصعداً للدعوات، وموطناً للاجتماع على الطاعات .. إلى غير ذلك من أنواع الخيرات . . ونبوة محمد ( صلى الله عليه وسلم) مما نطق به العجراء، وشهد به رب الأرض والسماء واتفق عليه من سبقه من الأنبياء، وخصائصه مما لا يضبطه العد والإحصاء وقد أشرقت الأرض بنورها إشراق الشمس في كبد السماء. فصياح الخصماء نباح الكلاب في الليلة القمراء
The answer: that some of that is an argument for us and a witness to our Prophet's merit, like his birth from polytheist men and women and his upbringing in their care, while persisting in tawhid and acts of obedience, and like his engagement in jihad, suppressing polytheists, and overpowering enemies of the religion, and like managing the interests of the order of the world while being absorbed in turning to the Holy Presence. As for his miracles, they attained that fame only through the report of our Prophet and his Book; nevertheless, where are [his miracles] compared to his miracles? Then being dead in the earth is more beneficial for the umma than being alive in heaven, for the holy garden became a descent for blessings, an ascent for supplications, and a place for gathering in obedience... to other kinds of good things. And the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) is that which the [العجراء?] testified, and the Lord of the earth and heaven witnessed, and the prophets before him agreed upon. And his special characteristics cannot be counted or enumerated; the earth has become bright with its light like the shining of the sun in the middle of the sky. So the yelling of the opponents is the barking of dogs in a moonlit night.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection raised about the Prophet (peace be upon him). The author begins by stating that some of the things mentioned are actually proof of the Prophet's merit. He then lists examples: his birth from polytheists while remaining devoted to tawhid, his jihad and suppression of enemies, his balancing worldly affairs with spiritual devotion. Then he discusses the fame of the Prophet's miracles being due to the Quran and the Prophet's own report. He concludes that the Prophet's death was more beneficial for the umma than his life, because his grave became a source of blessings and a gathering place for obedience. Finally, he dismisses the opponents' objections as mere barking like dogs in a moonlit night.

The Prophet's Night Journey and Ascension: Evidences and Refutations
The author affirms the Mi'raj in wakefulness and with body, supporting it with scriptural and rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple levels of evidence for the Prophet's Mi'raj and how the author counters objections to its bodily, wakeful nature.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj is proven by gradations of evidence, from the Quran to solitary reports.

The Mi'raj (Night Journey and Ascension) of Prophet Muhammad is one of the most debated miracles. In this passage, the author Taftazani affirms it as a bodily journey in wakefulness, supported by the Quran, consensus of early generations, widespread and solitary reports. He also addresses the narrations from Aisha and Muawiya that might suggest a dream or spiritual experience, showing they do not undermine the stronger proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the full translation. Notice the author's systematic presentation of different levels of evidence.
The Arabic uses terms like 'khabar al-mustafīḍ' and 'khabar al-wāḥid' which are technical classifications of hadith.
2
Identify the layers of proof
Note that the author assigns different categories of proof to different parts of the journey: Quran and consensus for the journey to al-Aqsa, widespread reports for the ascent to heaven, and solitary reports for beyond heaven.
This hierarchy shows the strength of evidence for each stage.
3
Understand the rational argument
The author provides a rational proof: bodies are similar, so breaking through the heavens is as possible as on earth; there is no proof of impossibility; and if it were a dream, disbelievers would not have reacted so strongly.
This is a kalam-style argument using possibility and actual occurrence.
4
Examine the refutation of objections
See how the author handles the narrations from Aisha and Muawiya. He argues that even if authentic, they cannot outweigh the mass of evidence from hadiths and consensus.
The author uses the principle of preferable evidence (tarjih).
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author insists on bodily and wakeful Mi'raj: it's a greater miracle and affirms the Prophet's truthfulness in the face of severe rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال خاتمة (معراج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ) إلى المسجد الأقصى ثابت بالكتاب وهو في اليقظة وبالجسد بإجماع القرن الثاني، ثم إلى السماء بالخير المستفيض، ثم إلى الجنة أو إلى العرش أو إلى طرف العالم بخبر الواحد، وما روي عن عائشة (رضي عنها أنها قالت: والله ما فقدت جسد رسول الله ) . وعن معاوية أنها كانت رؤيا صالحة، لا يعارض ما ذكرنا على أنه لو ادعى المعراج للروح أو في المنام لما نكره الكفار غاية الإنكار . قد ثبت معراج النبي بالكتاب والسنة ، وإجماع الأمة . إلا أن الخلاف في أنه في المنام أو في اليقظة؟ وبالروح فقط ، أو بالجسد؟ وإلى المسجد الأقصى فقط أو إلى السماء؟ والحق أنه في اليقظة بالجسد إلى المسجد الأقصى بشهادة الكتاب، وإجماع القرن الثاني ومن بعدهم ، ثم إلى السماء بالأحاديث المشهورة، والمنكر مبتدع ، ثم إلى الجنة أو العرش، أو طرف العالم على اختلاف الآراء بخبر الواحد، وقد اشتهر أنه نعت لقريش المسجد الأقصى على ما هو عليه، وأخبرهم بحال غيرهم، وكان على ما أخبر وبما رأى في السماء من العجائب، وبما شاهد من أحوال الأنبياء على ما هو مذكور في كتب الأحاديث لنا أنه أمر ممكن أخبر به الصادق، ودليل الإمكان أما تماثل الأجسام ، فيجوز الخرق على السماء كالأرض، وعروج الإنسان كغيره، وأما عدم دليل الامتناع وأنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، وأيضاً لو كان دعوى النبي و المعراج في المنام أو بالروح لما أنكره الكفرة غاية الإنكار، ولم يرتد بعض من أسلم تردداً منه في صدق النبي تمسك المخالف بما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها قالت: والله ما فقدت جسد محمد رسول الله ، وعن معاوية إنها كانت رؤيا صالحة، وأنت خبير بأنه على تقدير صحة روايته لا يصلح حجة في مقابلة ما ورد من الأحاديث وأقوال كبار الصحابة ، وإجماع القرون اللاحقة.
The conclusion: The Mi'raj of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to the al-Aqsa Mosque is established by the Book, and it was in wakefulness and with the body by consensus of the second century. Then to the heaven by the widespread [report?] (al-mustafīḍ), then to Paradise or the Throne or the edge of the world by a solitary report. And what was narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said: 'By Allah, I did not lose the body of the Messenger of Allah.' And from Mu'awiya that it was a righteous dream; this does not contradict what we have mentioned, because if he had claimed the Mi'raj for the spirit or in a dream, the disbelievers would not have denied it to the utmost. The Mi'raj of the Prophet is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the umma. However, the difference is whether it was in a dream or in wakefulness, and by the spirit only or by the body, and only to al-Aqsa Mosque or to the heaven. And the truth is that it was in wakefulness with the body to al-Aqsa Mosque by the testimony of the Book and the consensus of the second century and those after them, then to the heaven by the famous hadiths; and the denier is an innovator. Then to Paradise or the Throne or the edge of the world, differing in opinions, by a solitary report. And it is famous that he described to Quraysh the al-Aqsa Mosque as it was, and informed them of the condition of other caravans, and it was as he informed. And from what he saw in the heaven of wonders, and what he witnessed of the conditions of the prophets as mentioned in hadith books, for us it is a possible matter reported by the truthful one. The proof of possibility: because bodies are similar, so breaking through the heaven is permissible like the earth, and the ascension of a human is like others. And also because there is no proof of impossibility, and it does not necessitate from the assumption of its occurrence an impossibility. Also, if the Prophet had claimed the Mi'raj in a dream or by the spirit, the disbelievers would not have denied it to the utmost, and some of those who had converted would not have apostatized due to hesitation about the truthfulness of the Prophet. The opponent clings to what was narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said: 'By Allah, I did not lose the body of Muhammad the Messenger of Allah,' and from Mu'awiya that it was a righteous dream. And you are aware that, assuming its authenticity, it is not a valid argument against what has come from hadiths and statements of the senior Companions and the consensus of later generations.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage deals with the Mi'raj (Night Journey and Ascension) of the Prophet. It first states that the journey to al-Aqsa is established by the Quran, in wakefulness, and with body, by consensus of the second generation (the Tabi'un). Then it mentions that the journey to the heavens is established by widespread reports (al-khabar al-mustafīḍ), and to paradise or the throne by solitary reports (khabar al-wāḥid). It then addresses the narrations of Aisha and Muawiya, arguing they do not contradict the established proofs, because if it were only a dream, the disbelievers would not have strongly objected. Next, it explains the differences of opinion: whether it was in dream or wakefulness, by spirit or body, only to al-Aqsa or further. The truth is that it was in wakefulness with body to al-Aqsa by Quran and consensus, and to the heavens by famous hadiths; the denier is an innovator. The author then provides rational proofs: since bodies are similar, it is possible to break through the heavens as through the earth; there is no proof of impossibility; and if it were a dream, the disbelievers would not have so strongly rejected it, nor would some new Muslims have apostatized due to doubts. Finally, he refutes the objection from Aisha's statement that she did not lose the Prophet's body, arguing that even if authentic, it cannot outweigh the many hadiths and consensus of later generations.

خبر الواحد

خرق السماء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that the Mi'raj was only spiritual or a dream based on Aisha and Muawiya's narrations.
Correction: The author shows that even if those narrations are authentic, they cannot outweigh the stronger evidence from the Quran, consensus, and famous hadiths. The disbelievers' strong reaction also proves it was a physical event.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the truth regarding the Mi'raj?
A) It was in a dream and by spirit only
B) It was in wakefulness with body to al-Aqsa and then to heavens by famous hadiths
C) It was only to al-Aqsa
D) It was only a vision

Key Takeaway
The Mi'raj is a bodily, wakeful event established by multiple proofs, and objections from individual narrations are insufficient to overturn the consensus and mass of evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that the disbelievers' intense denial is evidence the Mi'raj was physical and wakeful. Are there areas of your faith where you unconsciously downplay or spiritualize physical truths of Islam (like prayer, charity, or miracles) to avoid the weight of their reality? What would it mean for your daily life to fully accept that Allah's power intervenes in the physical world just as He did for the Prophet?
?
The author refutes Aisha's narration by saying even if authentic, one report cannot overturn the mass of evidence. Is there a single doubt or weak argument you are holding onto that keeps you from fully embracing a clear truth of the Quran or consensus? Will you submit to the authority of the majority of evidence, even if it contradicts a cherished personal opinion?
?
The Prophet's journey began from the mosque on earth to the heavens — a physical movement. When you stand to pray, do you ever sense that your physical posture (standing, bowing, prostrating) connects you to realities beyond this world, just as the Prophet's physical body connected earth and heavens? What would it take for your prayer to become a true ascension of your heart?

Prophetic Infallibility and Sectarian Differences
The author defines prophetic infallibility and lists differing opinions on its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of prophetic infallibility (isma) and the differing views among Islamic sects regarding its scope.

Core Idea
Prophetic infallibility is defined by the miracle but its scope is disputed among sects.

This passage from al-Maqasid presents the doctrine of prophetic infallibility (isma) and then surveys the positions of various groups, including al-Qadi, the Azariqa, the Mu'tazila, and the Hashwiyya. Understanding these differences is essential for navigating theological debates on prophethood. The passage is a concise yet comprehensive summary of the key points of contention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the structure: it starts with a definition then lists points of disagreement.
The passage uses a very condensed style; each clause introduces a new view.
2
Identify the core definition
Find the phrase that defines infallibility. It says prophets are infallible regarding anything that contradicts the requirement of the miracle (mu'jiza), giving the example of lying in conveying the message.
This definition anchors the subsequent disagreements.
3
Map the three areas of disagreement
The passage lists three specific issues: (1) lying forgetfully (al-Qadi's view), (2) kufr (Azariqa's view), and (3) intentional major sins (three views: Ahl al-Sunna, Mu'tazila, Hashwiyya). List each and note the group associated.
These are not exhaustive but represent major historical positions.
4
Reflect on why these differences matter
Consider how each position affects the understanding of prophetic authority and the nature of revelation. The debate shows that even among Muslims, the precise limits of infallibility were contested.
This connects to broader themes in kalam and usul al-din.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث السادس الأنبياء معصومون عما ينافي مقتضى المعجزة كالكذب في التبليغ. وجوزه القاضي سهواً، وعن الكفر، وجوزه الأزارقة حيث جوزوا الذنب مع القول بأن كل ذنب كفر، وعن تعمد الكبار سمعاً عندنا، وعقلاً عند المعتزلة، وجوزه الحشوية
He said: The sixth section. The prophets are infallible regarding what contradicts the requirement of the miracle, such as lying in conveying (the message). Al-Qadi permitted it (i.e., lying) forgetfully. And regarding kufr, the Azariqa permitted it, for they permit sin while saying that every sin is kufr. And regarding intentional major sins, (it is prohibited) by revelation according to us, and by reason according to the Mu'tazila, and the Hashwiyya permitted it.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is the sixth section on the infallibility of prophets (isma). It defines infallibility as protection from anything that contradicts the requirement of the miracle (mu'jiza), specifically giving the example of lying in conveying revelation. Then it outlines differing views: al-Qadi (likely a reference to a scholar, possibly al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar or another) allowed lying forgetfully. As for kufr (disbelief), the Azariqa (a Kharijite subsect) allowed it because they consider every sin as kufr. Regarding intentional major sins (kaba'ir), the author states that according to us (Ahl al-Sunna) it is prohibited by revelation (sam‘), while according to the Mu'tazila it is prohibited by reason ('aql). Finally, the Hashwiyya (anthropomorphists) allowed it. Thus, the passage summarizes the different positions on the extent of prophetic infallibility.

Key Terms
عصمة
مقتضى المعجزة
الكذب في التبليغ
الأزارقة
الحشوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree on the exact scope of prophetic infallibility.
Correction: This passage shows significant differences among sects, including al-Qadi, Azariqa, Mu'tazila, and Hashwiyya, each with distinct positions on what prophets can or cannot do.

Checkpoint
Which group allowed prophets to commit kufr?
A) Al-Qadi
B) The Azariqa
C) The Mu'tazila
D) The Hashwiyya

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility is affirmed by all, but its scope—particularly regarding forgetful errors, kufr, and major sins—varies among Islamic theological schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I truly believe that the prophets were free from lying even in forgetfulness? How does that conviction shape the way I speak about them and the weight I give to their words?
?
When I encounter theological disagreements among Muslims, do I hastily dismiss them as errors, or do I earnestly seek to understand each position? What pride or prejudice might be hindering my humility before differing views of prophetic infallibility?
?
The passage shows that some sects allowed prophets to commit major sins. In my heart, do I hold the prophets to a standard that is truly above any sin, or have I ever unconsciously lowered that standard to justify my own shortcomings? What would change if I exalted their infallibility more deeply?

The Mi'raj: Established by Revelation and Consensus
The author affirms the Mi'raj and enumerates scholarly disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mi'raj is established through the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus, and what the main points of disagreement among scholars are.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj is a historical reality confirmed by divine revelation and scholarly consensus.

In this passage, the author begins by affirming that the Prophet's Night Journey is firmly established by the three primary sources of Islam. He then lists the key areas of disagreement among early scholars and declares the correct position, supporting it with evidence. This lesson introduces the foundations of belief in the Mi'raj and the scholarly consensus surrounding it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on how the author establishes the Mi'raj and lists the points of contention.
Notice the use of 'كتاب' (Book), 'سنة' (Sunnah), and 'إجماع' (consensus) as sources.
2
Identify the three disagreements
The passage lists three areas of disagreement: dream vs. wakefulness, spirit only vs. body, and extent (only to Masjid al-Aqsa or to heaven). List them and consider each side.
3
Understand the correct view
The author states 'الحق' (the truth) and gives his position. Identify the evidence he cites for each part (Qur'an, consensus of second century, well-known hadiths).
Pay attention to the phrase 'المنكر مبتدع' (the denier is an innovator).
4
Note the role of isolated reports
The author mentions that further destinations (Paradise, Throne, etc.) are based on 'خبر الواحد' (isolated reports) with differing opinions. Understand the distinction in evidentiary strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد ثبت معراج النبي ( ) بالكتاب والسنة ، وإجماع الأمة . إلا أن الخلاف في أنه في المنام أو في اليقظة؟ وبالروح فقط ، أو بالجسد؟ وإلى المسجد الأقصى فقط أو إلى السماء؟ والحق أنه في اليقظة بالجسد إلى المسجد الأقصى بشهادة الكتاب، وإجماع القرن الثاني ومن بعدهم ، ثم إلى السماء بالأحاديث المشهورة، والمنكر مبتدع ، ثم إلى الجنة أو العرش، أو طرف العالم على اختلاف الآراء بخبر الواحد، وقد اشتهر أنه نعت لقريش المسجد الأقصى على ما هو عليه، وأخبرهم بحال غيرهم، وكان على ما أخبر وبما رأى في السماء من العجائب، وبما شاهد من أحوال الأنبياء على ما هو مذكور في كتب الأحاديث لنا أنه أمر ممكن أخبر به الصادق، ودليل الإمكان أما تماثل الأجسام ، فيجوز الخرق على السماء كالأرض، وعروج الإنسان كغيره، وأما عدم دليل الامتناع وأنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، وأيضاً لو كان دعوى النبي ) و المعراج في المنام أو بالروح لما أنكره الكفرة غاية الإنكار، ولم يرتد بعض من أسلم تردداً منه في صدق النبي (عليه السلام) تمسك المخالف بما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها قالت: والله ما فقدت جسد محمد رسول الله ، وعن معاوية إنها كانت رؤيا صالحة، وأنت خبير بأنه على تقدير صحة روايته لا يصلح حجة في مقابلة ما ورد من الأحاديث وأقوال كبار الصحابة ، وإجماع القرون اللاحقة.
The Mi'raj of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) has been established by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the Umma. However, there is disagreement: was it in a dream or wakefulness? By the spirit only, or by the body? Only to the Masjid al-Aqsa, or to heaven? The truth is that it was in wakefulness by the body to the Masjid al-Aqsa, by the testimony of the Book, the consensus of the second century and those after them; then to heaven by well-known narrations; and the denier is an innovator. Then to Paradise, or the Throne, or the end of the world, according to differing opinions based on isolated reports. It is well-known that he described the Masjid al-Aqsa to Quraysh as it was, informed them of the condition of others, and it was as he reported, and concerning what he saw in heaven of wonders, and what he witnessed of the conditions of the prophets, as mentioned in hadith books. For us, it is a possible matter, reported by the truthful one. The proof of possibility: the similarity of bodies allows penetration in the heaven as on earth, and the ascension of a human is like others. Also, there is no proof of impossibility, and no absurdity necessarily follows from supposing its occurrence. Also, if the Prophet claimed that the Mi'raj was in a dream or by the spirit, the disbelievers would not have denied it so vehemently, and some who had converted would not have apostatized due to doubt in his truthfulness. The objector clings to what has been reported from 'A'isha (رضي الله عنها) that she said: 'By Allah, I did not lose the body of Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah,' and from Mu'awiya that it was a true vision. You are aware that even if the report is authentic, it cannot serve as evidence against what has come from hadiths and statements of senior Companions, and the consensus of later centuries.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the reality of the Prophet's Night Journey (Isra and Mi'raj). The author begins by stating that the Mi'raj is established by the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus of the Muslim community. He then lists the points of disagreement: whether it was in sleep or wakefulness, by spirit alone or with the body, and whether it was only to Jerusalem or all the way to heaven. The author then declares the correct view to be that it was in wakefulness with the body, first to the Masjid al-Aqsa (supported by the Qur'an and the consensus of early generations), then to the heavens (supported by well-known hadiths). He brands the denier of this as an innovator. Regarding later destinations (Paradise, Throne, etc.), the author says these are based on isolated reports and involve differences of opinion. The author then provides an argument for the possibility of the Mi'raj: bodies are similar, so piercing the heavens is possible just as piercing the earth; also, the ascension of a human is not inherently impossible. He further argues that if the journey had been only a dream, the Quraysh would not have objected so strongly, and some converts would not have apostatized. He then refutes an objection based on reports from 'A'isha and Mu'awiya that the body was never away or the event was a dream, stating that even if authentic, such reports cannot outweigh the strong textual evidence and consensus.

معراج
إسراء
إجماع
خبر الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mi'raj was only a spiritual experience or a dream.
Correction: The author argues it was bodily and wakeful, supported by Qur'an and consensus.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the points of disagreement listed by the author?
A) Was it in a dream or wakefulness?

C) Did it occur during the day or night?
D) Did it go only to Masjid al-Aqsa or to heaven?

Key Takeaway
The Mi'raj is a divinely confirmed event, and the correct scholarly view is that it was a physical journey in wakefulness to Jerusalem and the heavens.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Rational Proof of the Mi'raj and Refutation of Objections
The author presents rational proofs for the Mi'raj and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments for the possibility of the Mi'raj and how the author refutes objections based on reports from 'A'isha and Mu'awiya.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj is rationally possible, and objections from weak evidence cannot override established proofs.

Having established the revealed basis of the Mi'raj, the author now turns to rational arguments to demonstrate its possibility. He then addresses the strongest objections, including reports from 'A'isha and Mu'awiya, showing that they cannot outweigh the clear evidence of the Qur'an, Sunna, and consensus. This lesson explores the logical reasoning and refutation that solidify belief in the Mi'raj.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for possibility
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'لنا أنه أمر ممكن' and its translation. Identify the three rational proofs: similarity of bodies, lack of impossibility, and the reaction of disbelievers.
The argument begins with a claim of possibility and then provides evidence.
2
Analyze the rational proofs
The author gives three proofs: (1) bodies are similar, so piercing the heavens is like piercing the earth; (2) there is no proof of impossibility; (3) if it were only a dream, the Quraysh would not have denied it so fiercely, and some believers would not have apostatized.
The third proof is an argument from historical reaction.
3
Examine the objection from 'A'isha and Mu'awiya
The objector cites reports that the Prophet's body was never absent ('A'isha) or that it was a true vision (Mu'awiya). Understand these objections.
4
Understand the author's refutation
The author argues that even if those reports are authentic, they cannot outweigh the mass of hadiths, statements of senior Companions, and the consensus of later centuries.
Note the phrase 'لا يصلح حجة في مقابلة' (it does not serve as evidence against).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد ثبت معراج النبي ( ) بالكتاب والسنة ، وإجماع الأمة . إلا أن الخلاف في أنه في المنام أو في اليقظة؟ وبالروح فقط ، أو بالجسد؟ وإلى المسجد الأقصى فقط أو إلى السماء؟ والحق أنه في اليقظة بالجسد إلى المسجد الأقصى بشهادة الكتاب، وإجماع القرن الثاني ومن بعدهم ، ثم إلى السماء بالأحاديث المشهورة، والمنكر مبتدع ، ثم إلى الجنة أو العرش، أو طرف العالم على اختلاف الآراء بخبر الواحد، وقد اشتهر أنه نعت لقريش المسجد الأقصى على ما هو عليه، وأخبرهم بحال غيرهم، وكان على ما أخبر وبما رأى في السماء من العجائب، وبما شاهد من أحوال الأنبياء على ما هو مذكور في كتب الأحاديث لنا أنه أمر ممكن أخبر به الصادق، ودليل الإمكان أما تماثل الأجسام ، فيجوز الخرق على السماء كالأرض، وعروج الإنسان كغيره، وأما عدم دليل الامتناع وأنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، وأيضاً لو كان دعوى النبي ) و المعراج في المنام أو بالروح لما أنكره الكفرة غاية الإنكار، ولم يرتد بعض من أسلم تردداً منه في صدق النبي (عليه السلام) تمسك المخالف بما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها قالت: والله ما فقدت جسد محمد رسول الله ، وعن معاوية إنها كانت رؤيا صالحة، وأنت خبير بأنه على تقدير صحة روايته لا يصلح حجة في مقابلة ما ورد من الأحاديث وأقوال كبار الصحابة ، وإجماع القرون اللاحقة.
The Mi'raj of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) has been established by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the Umma. However, there is disagreement: was it in a dream or wakefulness? By the spirit only, or by the body? Only to the Masjid al-Aqsa, or to heaven? The truth is that it was in wakefulness by the body to the Masjid al-Aqsa, by the testimony of the Book, the consensus of the second century and those after them; then to heaven by well-known narrations; and the denier is an innovator. Then to Paradise, or the Throne, or the end of the world, according to differing opinions based on isolated reports. It is well-known that he described the Masjid al-Aqsa to Quraysh as it was, informed them of the condition of others, and it was as he reported, and concerning what he saw in heaven of wonders, and what he witnessed of the conditions of the prophets, as mentioned in hadith books. For us, it is a possible matter, reported by the truthful one. The proof of possibility: the similarity of bodies allows penetration in the heaven as on earth, and the ascension of a human is like others. Also, there is no proof of impossibility, and no absurdity necessarily follows from supposing its occurrence. Also, if the Prophet claimed that the Mi'raj was in a dream or by the spirit, the disbelievers would not have denied it so vehemently, and some who had converted would not have apostatized due to doubt in his truthfulness. The objector clings to what has been reported from 'A'isha (رضي الله عنها) that she said: 'By Allah, I did not lose the body of Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah,' and from Mu'awiya that it was a true vision. You are aware that even if the report is authentic, it cannot serve as evidence against what has come from hadiths and statements of senior Companions, and the consensus of later centuries.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the reality of the Prophet's Night Journey (Isra and Mi'raj). The author begins by stating that the Mi'raj is established by the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus of the Muslim community. He then lists the points of disagreement: whether it was in sleep or wakefulness, by spirit alone or with the body, and whether it was only to Jerusalem or all the way to heaven. The author then declares the correct view to be that it was in wakefulness with the body, first to the Masjid al-Aqsa (supported by the Qur'an and the consensus of early generations), then to the heavens (supported by well-known hadiths). He brands the denier of this as an innovator. Regarding later destinations (Paradise, Throne, etc.), the author says these are based on isolated reports and involve differences of opinion. The author then provides an argument for the possibility of the Mi'raj: bodies are similar, so piercing the heavens is possible just as piercing the earth; also, the ascension of a human is not inherently impossible. He further argues that if the journey had been only a dream, the Quraysh would not have objected so strongly, and some converts would not have apostatized. He then refutes an objection based on reports from 'A'isha and Mu'awiya that the body was never away or the event was a dream, stating that even if authentic, such reports cannot outweigh the strong textual evidence and consensus.

Key Terms
تماثل الأجسام
خرق السماء
الصادق
خبر الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mi'raj is rationally impossible because bodies cannot ascend through the heavens.
Correction: The author argues that bodies are similar, so if a body can move on earth, it can move in the heavens; there is no inherent impossibility.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the author's proofs for the possibility of the Mi'raj?
A) The similarity of bodies allows piercing the heavens.
B) There is no proof of impossibility.
C) The Quraysh would have accepted a dream claim.
D) The Prophet is the truthful one.

Key Takeaway
The Mi'raj is not only scripturally confirmed but also rationally sound, and objections from isolated reports cannot outweigh the established evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear objections to Islamic beliefs from weak sources, do you weigh them against the overwhelming evidence from the majority of scholars? How can you train yourself to prioritize stronger evidence in your own research?
?
The Quraysh’s strong negative reaction proved the Mi’raj was a physical claim. In your life, do you sometimes dismiss the significance of a claim because it seems extraordinary? How can you apply the principle of not assuming impossibility without proof?
?
If the Prophet’s truthfulness is established beyond doubt, what areas of your faith are you still hesitant to fully accept because they seem beyond your experience? What would change if you truly believed nothing is impossible for Allah?

The Core of Prophetic Infallibility: Lying and Disbelief
Definition of 'isma and two contested categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational principle of prophetic infallibility and the specific positions on lying in conveying and disbelief.

Core Idea
Prophets are immune from lying in conveying revelation; disbelief is impossible.

This passage opens the discussion on prophetic infallibility by stating the core principle: prophets cannot do anything that contradicts the purpose of their miracles, such as lying in delivering the message. It then covers two controversial categories: sins committed out of forgetfulness and the extreme view of the Azariqa on disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification come through.
Notice the structure: principle first, then two specific issues.
2
Identify the core principle
Locate the statement that defines what prophets are immune from. Understand the connection to miracles.
The key phrase is 'ما ينافي مقتضى المعجزة'.
3
Examine the two contested categories
The passage mentions forgetfulness (al-Qadi's view) and disbelief (Azariqa's view). Note how each challenges the principle.
The Azariqa's view is extreme: they equated all sins with disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبياء معصومون عما ينافي مقتضى المعجزة كالكذب في التبليغ. وجوزه القاضي سهواً، وعن الكفر، وجوزه الأزارقة حيث جوزوا الذنب مع القول بأن كل ذنب كفر، وعن تعمد الكبار سمعاً عندنا، وعقلاً عند المعتزلة، وجوزه الحشوية وعن الصغائر المنفرة وكذا تعمد غير المنفرة خلافاً لإمام الحرمين، وأبي هاشم، والمختار عن سهو الكبيرة أيضاً
The prophets are infallible (ma'sumun) regarding that which contradicts the requirement of the miracle (muqtada al-mu'jiza), such as lying in conveying (the message). Al-Qadi permitted it (i.e., occasional sin) out of forgetfulness. Regarding kufr (disbelief), the Azariqa permitted it, since they permitted sin while holding that every sin is kufr. Regarding deliberate major sins (kaba'ir) – according to us (the Ash'aris) it is prohibited by revelation (sam'an), and according to the Mu'tazila it is prohibited by reason ('aqlan). The Hashwiyya permitted it. Regarding minor sins (sagha'ir) that are repulsive (munaffira), and also deliberate non-repulsive minor sins, contrary to Imam al-Haramayn and Abu Hashim. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) also (allows) forgetfulness regarding major sins.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of prophetic infallibility ('isma). It begins by stating that prophets are immune from anything that contradicts the purpose of their miracles, such as lying in delivering the message. It then enumerates the various positions on different types of possible lapses. Al-Qadi (likely Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar or similar) permitted sins out of forgetfulness. The Azariqa (a Kharijite sect) permitted disbelief because they considered all sins as disbelief. For deliberate major sins after prophethood, the Ash'aris hold it to be prohibited by revelation, the Mu'tazila by reason, while the Hashwiyya (anthropomorphists) permitted it. Regarding minor sins, the author notes disagreement: repulsive minor sins and deliberate non-repulsive minor sins are prohibited, contrary to the views of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and Abu Hashim. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) also includes the possibility of forgetfulness regarding major sins, indicating a nuanced position that does not absolutely forbid inadvertent major sins. The author does not provide details of the proofs here; the focus is purely on the taxonomy of sins and the different scholarly positions.

Key Terms

كذب في التبليغ
سهو
كفر
القاضي
الأزارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think prophets can lie in conveying the message; the passage states they are immune.
Correction: The text explicitly says prophets are immune (معصومون) from lying in the conveyance of revelation, which is a core aspect of 'isma.

Checkpoint

A) The Ash'aris
B) The Mu'tazila
C) The Azariqa
D) The Hashwiyya

Key Takeaway
The foundational principle of prophetic infallibility includes immunity from lying in conveying revelation; other sins are subject to scholarly debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage records scholarly disagreement on the limits of 'isma. How do you typically react when you learn that respected scholars disagreed? Does it shake your confidence in Islamic knowledge, or does it deepen your trust in the tradition's robustness? What does your reaction reveal about your own relationship with scholarship?

Major and Minor Sins: Scholarly Disagreements
Enumeration of positions on major and minor sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on whether prophets can commit major or minor sins, and the role of reason vs revelation.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree on major and minor sins; Ash'aris rely on revelation, Mu'tazila on reason.

After establishing the basic principle, the author turns to the more nuanced categories of major and minor sins. He contrasts the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions on major sins, mentions the Hashwiyya's permissive view, and then discusses minor sins and forgetfulness.

Guided Path

2
Compare the reasons for prohibiting major sins
Ash'aris say major sins are prohibited by revelation (سمعاً), while Mu'tazila say by reason (عقلاً). Understand the difference.
The Hashwiyya permitted them, showing a spectrum of views.
3
Analyze the minor sins and forgetfulness
Note that repulsive minor sins are universally prohibited, while non-repulsive minor sins are debated. The preferred view allows forgetfulness in major sins.
Imam al-Haramayn and Abu Hashim held stricter views.
