The Opponent's Objection and the General Response
Presents the opponent's evidence and general interpretative response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend prophetic sinlessness by distinguishing between types of narrations and offering interpretative frameworks for apparent sins.

Core Idea
Solitary reports are rejected; clear texts are interpreted through forgetfulness or pre-prophethood.

When opponents of prophetic sinlessness cite stories of prophets committing sins or seeking forgiveness, they rely on both Qur’anic verses and hadiths. This lesson presents their objection and the first level of response: a general method that sorts narrations by reliability and applies consistent interpretations. The author shows that apparent sins can be explained away without denying the prophets' essential immunity from sin.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
Notice how the opponent uses prophetic stories and Quranic verses mentioning sin and repentance. Key terms: معصية, ذنب, توبة.
2
Examine the general response structure
The answer has two parts: general and detailed. The general response classifies narrations as solitary (مردود) or mutawatir/Quranic (interpreted).
3
Learn the standard interpretations
The author lists: forgetfulness (سهو/نسيان), abandoning the better (ترك الأولى), pre-prophethood (قبل البعثة), etc.
4
Note the methodology
This shows a systematic approach to apparent contradictions—a key skill in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المخالف بما نقل من أقاصيص الأنبياء، وما شهد به كتاب الله من المعصية والذنب إليهم ، ومن توبتهم واستغفارهم وأمثال ذلك . والجواب عنه إما إجمالاً فهو أن ما نقل آحاداً مردود وما نقل متواتراً أو منصوصاً في الكتاب محمول على السهو والنسيان أو ترك الأولى، أو كونه قبل البعثة، أو غير ذلك من المحامل والتأويلات، وإما تفصيلاً فمذكور في التفاسير وفي الكتب المصنفة في هذا الباب أما في قصة آدم (عليه السلام) فأمران . أحدهما ـ ما ورد في التنزيل من أنه عصى وغوى وأزله الشيطان، وخالف النهي عن أكل الشجرة، وبنزع اللباس والإخراج من الجنة ثم تاب الله تعالى عليه و اجتباه . والجواب : أنه كان قبل البعثة كيف ولم تكن له في الجنة أمة أو كان عن نسيان لقوله تعالى : (فَنَسِي وَلَمْ نَجِدْ له عَزْماً ) (٢) . أو كان زلة وسهواً حيث ظن أن المنهي شجرة بعينها ، وقد قرب فرداً آخر من جنسها، وإنما عوتب لترك التيقظ والتنبه لإصابة المراد. وقد يعتذر بأنه وإن كان عمداً ، لكن لم يكن إلا صغيرة، وهذا هو الظاهر، إلا إن فيه تسليماً للمدعي. وثانيهما - قوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ ثُمَّ جَعَلَ مِنْهَا زَوْجَهَا) (۳). إلى قوله : جَعَلا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا أَتَاهُمَا. ولم يقل أحد في حق الأنبياء بالشرك في الألوهية ولو قبل البعثة، فالوجه على
The opponent argued with what is narrated from the stories of the prophets and what the Book of Allah testifies of disobedience and sin concerning them, and of their repentance and seeking forgiveness and the like. The answer to it, either in general, is that what is narrated as solitary reports is rejected, and what is narrated as mutawatir or explicitly in the Book is interpreted as forgetfulness or oversight, or abandoning the better, or being before the mission, or other interpretations and explanations. Or in detail, it is mentioned in the commentaries and in the books composed on this topic. As for the story of Adam (peace be upon him), there are two matters. One of them: what came down in the revelation that he disobeyed and went astray, and Satan caused him to slip, and he violated the prohibition against eating the tree, and the removal of clothing and expulsion from Paradise, then Allah relented toward him and chose him. The answer: that it was before the mission – how so when he did not have a nation in Paradise? – or it was from forgetfulness, as per His statement: 'So he forgot, and We did not find in him determination' (Quran 20:115). Or it was a slip and forgetfulness where he thought the prohibited was a specific tree, but he approached another individual of its kind, and he was only rebuked for lack of caution and attentiveness to hit the target. And it may be excused by saying that even if it was deliberate, it was only a minor sin, and this is the apparent view, except that it concedes to the claimant. The second matter: His statement: 'He is the One who created you from a single soul, then made from it its mate' (Quran 7:189) up to His statement: 'they set up partners with Him in what He gave them' (Quran 7:190). No one has said concerning the prophets that they commit shirk in divinity, even before the mission. So the correct interpretation is ... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the opposition (المخالف) who use the apparent sins and repentance of prophets mentioned in the Quran and in hadith to argue against their absolute sinlessness. The author then gives a two-part response: general and detailed. The general response states that solitary reports are rejected, and that mutawatir reports or explicit Quranic verses must be interpreted as due to forgetfulness, oversight, abandoning the better (ترك الأولى), or being before the prophetic mission, etc. The author then begins the detailed response with the story of Adam (peace be upon him), which has two issues. The first issue is the apparent disobedience of Adam – eating from the tree, being seduced by Satan, etc. The author offers several interpretations: (1) it was before his mission as a prophet; (2) it was due to forgetfulness as indicated by the verse 'So he forgot'; (3) it was a slip because he thought the prohibited tree was a specific one, but he ate from a different tree of the same kind, and his rebuke was for not being careful enough; (4) even if it was deliberate, it was only a minor sin – but the author notes that this last interpretation concedes to the opponent's claim that a prophet can commit a sin. The second issue concerns the verse about Adam and his wife setting up partners with Allah – the author argues that no one claims that prophets committed shirk, so the verse must be interpreted differently. The passage ends abruptly, indicating that the argument continues on the next page.

Correction: Such reports are either unreliable (solitary) or must be interpreted as forgetfulness, pre-prophethood, etc.

The general response filters narrations by reliability and interprets accepted texts through excuses consistent with 'isma.

Detailed Response on Prophet Adam: Interpretations of Apparent Sin
Begins the detailed defense of Adam, offering four interpretations for his apparent sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple scholarly interpretations of Prophet Adam's apparent disobedience in the Quran, all of which preserve his sinlessness.

Core Idea
Adam's sin is explained as pre-prophethood, forgetfulness, or a slip—never a true sin against a prophet.

The author now moves to the detailed response, starting with the story of Adam. The Quran mentions Adam's disobedience, eating from the tree, and subsequent repentance. This lesson examines the first of two matters: the apparent sin. Several interpretations are offered, each showing that Adam did not actually commit a sin that would violate his prophethood. The lesson also begins the second matter regarding shirk, but the passage is incomplete.

Adam's action was before his mission as a prophet (since he had no nation in Paradise).
3

Adam forgot the prohibition, as the Quran says 'فنسي' (so he forgot).

General Objection and Answer on Prophets' Sins
Taftazani presents the objector's argument and gives a general answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general objection against prophetic infallibility and Taftazani's two-tiered response, particularly the distinction between solitary and mass-transmitted reports and how each is handled.

Core Idea
Scriptural evidence against infallibility is interpreted, not denied.

This passage presents a key challenge in prophetic theology: how to reconcile Qur'anic verses and stories that seem to ascribe sin to prophets with the doctrine of infallibility. Taftazani outlines a method to answer such objections by categorizing the type of transmission and applying appropriate interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the argument. Notice that the objector cites stories and verses, and the author gives a two-part answer: summary and detailed.
The detailed answer is only referenced, not given here.
2
Identify the two categories of reports
In the summary answer, the author separates solitary (āḥād) reports from mass-transmitted (mutawātir) or explicitly stated Qur'anic texts. Understand the different treatment: solitary are rejected; mass-transmitted are interpreted.
This is a key distinction in Islamic legal and theological methodology.
3
List the acceptable interpretations
The author lists four interpretations for mass-transmitted texts: forgetfulness, inadvertence, leaving what is better, and occurrence before the prophetic mission. Reflect on how these preserve infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المخالف بما نقل من أقاصيص الأنبياء، وما شهد به كتاب الله من المعصية والذنب إليهم، ومن توبتهم واستغفارهم وأمثال ذلك. والجواب عنه إما إجمالاً فهو أن ما نقل آحاداً مردود وما نقل متواتراً أو منصوصاً في الكتاب محمول على السهو والنسيان أو ترك الأولى، أو كونه قبل البعثة، أو غير ذلك من المحامل والتأويلات، وإما تفصيلاً فمذكور في التفاسير وفي الكتب المصنفة في هذا الباب.
The opponent argued by what is transmitted from the stories of the prophets, and what the Book of God attests of disobedience and sin attributed to them, and of their repentance and seeking forgiveness and the like. The answer to it, either in summary, is that what is transmitted as solitary (āḥād) is rejected, and what is transmitted as mass-transmitted (mutawātir) or explicitly stated in the Book is interpreted as forgetfulness and inadvertence, or leaving what is better, or it being before the mission, or other such interpretations and explanations. And in detail, it is mentioned in the exegeses and in the books authored on this topic.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the general objection against the infallibility of prophets and the author’s two-tiered response. The objector cites stories from the prophets and Qur’anic verses that mention sin, repentance, and forgiveness as proof that prophets can commit sins. The author gives a summary answer (ijmālan) and then a detailed answer (tafṣīlan). In the summary answer, he distinguishes between two types of transmitted reports: solitary reports (āḥād) are simply rejected if they contradict infallibility; mass-transmitted reports (mutawātir) or explicit Qur’anic texts are interpreted through acceptable explanations such as forgetfulness, inadvertence, doing what is less preferable (tark al-ūlā), or occurring before the prophetic mission (qabla al-biʿtha). The detailed answer, he says, is found in the exegeses and specialized books on this subject. The author thus establishes a method for handling apparent evidence against prophetic infallibility without denying the clear statements of scripture.

Key Terms
احتج المخالف
آحاد
متواتر
سهو
نسيان
ترك الأولى
قبل البعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author dismisses all objections as false, denying the validity of the Qur'anic verses.
Correction: The author actually accepts the verses but interprets them through acceptable explanations (forgetfulness, leaving the better, pre-prophethood) to preserve infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to the general answer, how are mass-transmitted reports about prophets' sins treated?
A) They are rejected outright.
B) They are interpreted as forgetfulness, leaving the better, or pre-prophethood.
C) They are accepted as proof of sin.
D) They are considered abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The author preserves prophetic infallibility by categorizing evidence: solitary reports are rejected, while mass-transmitted texts are interpreted through acceptable explanations such as forgetfulness, leaving the better, or pre-prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Qur'anic verse that seems to show a prophet in a negative light, does it ever cause you to doubt their character? After studying this lesson, will you now pause to consider the interpretations (forgetfulness, leaving the better, before mission) before allowing any doubt to settle?
?
The author provides a methodological approach: accept mass-transmitted evidence and interpret it, reject solitary evidence. In your own life, when you encounter a hadith or religious claim that challenges your beliefs, do you have a similar rigorous method? Or do you react emotionally? This lesson calls you to intellectual discipline—will you adopt it?
?
The concept of 'leaving what is better' (tark al-ūlā) is used to defend prophets. Now think about yourself: are there areas in your daily life where you consistently choose what is merely permissible over what is better? How does the high standard of the prophets inspire you to raise your own standards, even in non-obligatory matters?

Adam's Disobedience: Objection and Answers
Taftazani enumerates answers to the objection of Adam's disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend prophetic infallibility of Adam despite Quranic verses describing his disobedience.

Core Idea
Adam's apparent sin is explained by context: before prophethood, forgetfulness, or a minor slip.

The story of Adam in the Quran raises questions about prophetic sinlessness. Taftazani presents an objection from verses describing Adam's actions and offers several scholarly responses that preserve his prophetic status. Let's read the passage and examine each answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and problematic verses
Read the listed actions of Adam: disobedience, going astray, Satan's slip, etc. Note how these challenge prophetic infallibility.
The objection uses Quranic verses directly.
2
Identify the four answers
Find each answer: before prophethood, forgetfulness, a slip/mistake, and minor sin (concession). Notice how the author presents them.
The last answer is presented as a concession to the opponent.
3
Evaluate the strength of each defense
Consider which answers fully preserve sinlessness and which allow minor sins. Reflect on the author's caution about the last answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما في قصة آدم (عليه السلام) فأمران. أحدهما – ما ورد في التنزيل من أنه عصى وغوى وأزله الشيطان، وخالف النهي عن أكل الشجرة، وبنزع اللباس والإخراج من الجنة ثم تاب الله تعالى عليه واجتباه. والجواب: أنه كان قبل البعثة كيف ولم تكن له في الجنة أمة أو كان عن نسيان لقوله تعالى: (فَنَسِي وَلَمْ نَجِدْ له عَزْماً). أو كان زلة وسهواً حيث ظن أن المنهي شجرة بعينها، وقد قرب فرداً آخر من جنسها، وإنما عوتب لترك التيقظ والتنبه لإصابة المراد. وقد يعتذر بأنه وإن كان عمداً، لكن لم يكن إلا صغيرة، وهذا هو الظاهر، إلا إن فيه تسليماً للمدعي. وثانيهما - قوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ ثُمَّ جَعَلَ مِنْهَا زَوْجَهَا﴾ إلى قوله: جَعَلا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا أَتَاهُمَا. ولم يقل أحد في حق الأنبياء بالشرك في الألوهية ولو قبل البعثة، فالوجه على أنه على حذف المضاف أي جعل أولادهما له شركاء، بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَعَالَى اللهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ﴾. أو المراد ما وقع له من الميل إلى طاعة الشيطان، وقبول وسوسته، أو الخطاب لقريش، والنفس الواحدة قضى. ومعنى جعل منها زوجها جعلها من جنسها عربية قرشية. وإشراكهما فيما آتاهما الله تسمية أولادهما بعبد مناف، وعبد العزى وعبد الدار، ونحو ذلك.
As for the story of Adam (peace be upon him), there are two matters. The first: what has come in the revelation that he disobeyed and went astray, and Satan caused him to slip, and he violated the prohibition against eating the tree, and the removal of clothing and expulsion from Paradise, then God relented toward him and chose him. And the answer: that it was before the mission – how could he have had a nation in Paradise? – or it was out of forgetfulness due to His saying: (So he forgot, and We did not find in him determination). Or it was a slip and inadvertence when he thought that the forbidden tree was a specific one, and he had brought near another individual of its kind, and he was only reproached for leaving caution and attentiveness to hit the intended mark. And it may be excused that even if it was intentional, it was only a minor sin, and this is apparent, except that it contains a concession to the opponent. The second: His saying: (He it is who created you from a single soul and then made from her her mate) up to His saying: (they made partners for Him in what He gave them). And no one has said regarding prophets that they commit shirk in divinity, even before the mission, so the interpretation is that it is with ellipsis of the genitive, i.e., He made their children partners for Him, evidenced by His saying: (So exalted is God above what they associate). Or what is meant is what occurred of inclination to obey Satan and accepting his whisper, or the address is to Quraysh, and the single soul is Quraysh. And the meaning of 'made from her her mate' is made her of her kind, Arab, Qurayshite. And 'making partners in what God gave them' is naming their sons Abd Manaf, Abd al-Uzza, Abd al-Dar, and the like.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents two specific objections from the story of Adam and their answers. The first objection is that Adam is described as disobeying, going astray, being slipped by Satan, violating the prohibition, and then repenting. The author gives multiple answers: it was before his prophethood (since he had no nation in Paradise), it was out of forgetfulness (citing verse 20:115), it was a slip where he mistakenly thought the forbidden tree was a specific one and ate another of the same genus, thus blame was for lack of caution, or even if intentional, it was only a minor sin (though this last concedes to the opponent that prophets can commit minor sins). The second objection is the verse about making partners for God. The author argues that no one claims prophets committed shirk, so the partner-making must refer to their children (by ellipsis), or it refers to Adam's inclination to accept Satan's whisper, or the address is to Quraysh (the 'single soul' is Quraysh), and the naming of children after idols is meant.

Key Terms

نسيان
زلة
سهو
صغيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That scholars unanimously insist Adam's sin was only forgetfulness.
Correction: The author presents multiple answers, including the possibility of a minor sin, showing scholarly diversity.

Checkpoint
What is one interpretation given for Adam's disobedience regarding the tree?
A) It was a major sin that he repented from.
B) It was done after he became a prophet.
C) It was a slip due to mistaking the tree, so he was blamed for lack of caution.
D) It was a deliberate act of pride.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility is preserved through contextual and interpretive explanations of Adam's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows multiple explanations, but the core is that Allah still chose Adam after his slip. Do you truly believe that your failures do not disqualify you from Allah's mercy, or do you secretly think some sins are too great? What can you learn from Adam's story to break that barrier?

The Shirk Objection from Adam's Story
Taftazani provides interpretations to excuse Adam from shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse about making partners for God is interpreted to avoid implying shirk by a prophet.

Core Idea
'Making partners' in the verse refers to Adam's children naming them after idols, not to Adam himself.

The second objection from Adam's story involves a verse where it says 'they made partners for Him in what He gave them.' Taftazani explains that no prophet can commit shirk, so the interpretation must involve a genitive ellipsis or refer to Adam's descendants. Let's see the different interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the cited verse
Read the verse from the Quran and the claim that it implicates Adam and Eve in shirk. Understand the difficulty.
The verse uses plural 'they made partners'.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Find each interpretation: genitive ellipsis (children), inclination to Satan, or address to Quraysh. Note the evidence given for each.
The first interpretation is supported by another verse using 'they associate'.
3
Assess which interpretation is strongest
Consider the context of the verse and the author's reasoning. Reflect on why shirk cannot be attributed to prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما في قصة آدم (عليه السلام) فأمران. أحدهما – ما ورد في التنزيل من أنه عصى وغوى وأزله الشيطان، وخالف النهي عن أكل الشجرة، وبنزع اللباس والإخراج من الجنة ثم تاب الله تعالى عليه واجتباه. والجواب: أنه كان قبل البعثة كيف ولم تكن له في الجنة أمة أو كان عن نسيان لقوله تعالى: (فَنَسِي وَلَمْ نَجِدْ له عَزْماً). أو كان زلة وسهواً حيث ظن أن المنهي شجرة بعينها، وقد قرب فرداً آخر من جنسها، وإنما عوتب لترك التيقظ والتنبه لإصابة المراد. وقد يعتذر بأنه وإن كان عمداً، لكن لم يكن إلا صغيرة، وهذا هو الظاهر، إلا إن فيه تسليماً للمدعي. وثانيهما - قوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ ثُمَّ جَعَلَ مِنْهَا زَوْجَهَا﴾ إلى قوله: جَعَلا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا أَتَاهُمَا. ولم يقل أحد في حق الأنبياء بالشرك في الألوهية ولو قبل البعثة، فالوجه على أنه على حذف المضاف أي جعل أولادهما له شركاء، بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَعَالَى اللهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ﴾. أو المراد ما وقع له من الميل إلى طاعة الشيطان، وقبول وسوسته، أو الخطاب لقريش، والنفس الواحدة قضى. ومعنى جعل منها زوجها جعلها من جنسها عربية قرشية. وإشراكهما فيما آتاهما الله تسمية أولادهما بعبد مناف، وعبد العزى وعبد الدار، ونحو ذلك.
As for the story of Adam (peace be upon him), there are two matters. The first: what has come in the revelation that he disobeyed and went astray, and Satan caused him to slip, and he violated the prohibition against eating the tree, and the removal of clothing and expulsion from Paradise, then God relented toward him and chose him. And the answer: that it was before the mission – how could he have had a nation in Paradise? – or it was out of forgetfulness due to His saying: (So he forgot, and We did not find in him determination). Or it was a slip and inadvertence when he thought that the forbidden tree was a specific one, and he had brought near another individual of its kind, and he was only reproached for leaving caution and attentiveness to hit the intended mark. And it may be excused that even if it was intentional, it was only a minor sin, and this is apparent, except that it contains a concession to the opponent. The second: His saying: (He it is who created you from a single soul and then made from her her mate) up to His saying: (they made partners for Him in what He gave them). And no one has said regarding prophets that they commit shirk in divinity, even before the mission, so the interpretation is that it is with ellipsis of the genitive, i.e., He made their children partners for Him, evidenced by His saying: (So exalted is God above what they associate). Or what is meant is what occurred of inclination to obey Satan and accepting his whisper, or the address is to Quraysh, and the single soul is Quraysh. And the meaning of 'made from her her mate' is made her of her kind, Arab, Qurayshite. And 'making partners in what God gave them' is naming their sons Abd Manaf, Abd al-Uzza, Abd al-Dar, and the like.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents two specific objections from the story of Adam and their answers. The first objection is that Adam is described as disobeying, going astray, being slipped by Satan, violating the prohibition, and then repenting. The author gives multiple answers: it was before his prophethood (since he had no nation in Paradise), it was out of forgetfulness (citing verse 20:115), it was a slip where he mistakenly thought the forbidden tree was a specific one and ate another of the same genus, thus blame was for lack of caution, or even if intentional, it was only a minor sin (though this last concedes to the opponent that prophets can commit minor sins). The second objection is the verse about making partners for God. The author argues that no one claims prophets committed shirk, so the partner-making must refer to their children (by ellipsis), or it refers to Adam's inclination to accept Satan's whisper, or the address is to Quraysh (the 'single soul' is Quraysh), and the naming of children after idols is meant.

Key Terms
حذف المضاف
الخطاب لقريش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse in Surah al-A'raf accuses Adam and Eve of shirk.
Correction: The author shows that the verse is interpreted in multiple ways to avoid any implication of shirk by prophets.

Checkpoint
What is the main interpretation given for the verse 'they made partners for Him in what He gave them'?
A) Adam and Eve committed shirk.
B) It refers to their children making partners.
C) It refers to Adam's forgetfulness.
D) It is a hypothetical scenario.

Key Takeaway
The verse does not implicate Adam in shirk; it is interpreted as referring to his descendants or a non-literal slip.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a Quranic verse and taken its apparent meaning without considering scholarly interpretations, even when that meaning seemed to contradict the stature of a prophet? What does that reveal about your patience in seeking knowledge?
?
When you encounter a difficult verse that seems to challenge your understanding of Allah's justice or mercy, do you quickly settle on a surface-level understanding, or do you actively seek deeper scholarly explanations like Taftazani's? What steps can you take to adopt a more careful approach?
?
The Quraysh named their children after idols (Abd Manaf, Abd al-Uzza). Are there any names, titles, or identities you hold dear that might reflect a hidden allegiance or prioritize something above Allah? How would you recognise and abandon such attachments?

Noah's Story: Clarifying God's Reply
Taftazani answers the objection about Noah calling his son family.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani resolves the apparent contradiction in the story of Noah and his son, showing that God's response was not a rebuke but a clarification.

Core Idea
Noah was not rebuked; God clarified what 'family' means in His promise.

When reading the Qur'anic verses about Noah calling his son part of his family, some raise a doubt: Did God rebuke Noah for a false claim? Taftazani explains that this is a misunderstanding. The passage presents several interpretations that show God's words were a clarification, not a denial. Let's examine the objection and the nuanced answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the doubt
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the objection: someone claims God denied Noah's statement. Pay attention to the words used.
The doubt centers on the word 'family' (ahl).
2
Understand the objection
The objector sees God's words 'he is not of your family' as a direct denial of Noah's 'my son is from my family'. This seems to imply Noah was mistaken or lying.
3
Explore the three answers
Taftazani gives three distinct interpretations, each showing that God was not denying but clarifying. Note how each interpretation redefines what 'family' means in the promise.
The three answers are: righteous family, religion, and stepson/stranger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة في حق نوح (عليه السلام) فهو أن قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا نُوحُ إِنَّه لَيْسَ مِنْ أَهْلِكَ ﴾ تكذيب له في قوله: ﴿إِنَّ ابْنِي مِنْ أهلي﴾. والجواب أنه ليس للتكذيب بل للتنبيه على أن المراد بالأهل في الوعد هو الأهل الصالح، أو المعنى أنه ليس من أهل دينك، أو أنه أجنبي منك، وإن أضفته إلى نفسك بأبنائك لما روي من أنه كان ابن امرأته والأجنبي إنما يعد من آل النبي إذا كان له عمل صالح.
As for the doubt regarding Noah (peace be upon him), it is that His saying: (O Noah, he is not of your family) is a denial of his saying: (My son is from my family). And the answer is that it is not for denial but to indicate that what is meant by 'family' in the promise is the righteous family, or the meaning is that he is not of your religion, or that he is a stranger from you, even if you attribute him to yourself as your son, because it is narrated that he was his wife's son, and the stranger is only counted among the family of the prophet if he has righteous deeds.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage addresses a doubt about Prophet Noah. The objector claims that God denied Noah’s statement because Noah called his son part of his family while God said 'he is not of your family'. The author responds with multiple interpretations: it is not a denial but a clarification that 'family' in the promise refers to righteous family members; or it means he is not of your religion; or he is a stranger to you even if you call him your son because he was actually his wife’s son from a previous marriage. A stranger is only considered part of a prophet’s household if he has righteous deeds. Thus, Noah was not rebuked for a lie but taught a nuance.

أجنبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God rebuked Noah for claiming his son was part of his family, implying Noah lied.
Correction: God's response was not a rebuke but a clarification that 'family' in the promise refers to righteous family members, or that the son was not of Noah's religion, or that he was an outsider despite being his stepson.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say God's response to Noah is not a denial?
A) Because Noah was not a prophet then.
B) Because 'family' in the promise means only righteous family.
C) Because the son was a prophet too.
D) Because God always speaks in metaphors.

Key Takeaway
God's words to Noah were a clarification of the term 'family', not a denial of Noah's statement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

What would it take for you to treat them with the same urgency as Noah calling them 'my son' while still recognizing the truth of God's clarification?

Abraham’s Three Apparent Lies Explained
Taftazani responds to three apparent lies of Abraham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani interprets three statements of Abraham (as) that seem like lies, preserving his prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Abraham’s words are not lies but rhetorical devices.

Abraham (as), known as the friend of Allah, is accused of lying in three Qur'anic verses. How can a prophet lie? Taftazani provides subtle yet powerful explanations that transform these apparent falsehoods into sophisticated rhetorical strategies. Let's examine each case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three statements are listed together.
2
Identify the three alleged lies
The passage lists three statements: 'This is my Lord', 'Rather, the big one among them did it', and 'Indeed I am sick'. Find each one in the text.
These are from Surah Al-An'am and Surah Al-Anbiya.
3
Study the first interpretation
The first statement is explained as hypothesis, interrogative, or reasoning before prophethood. Understand why this clears it of falsehood.
The key term here is 'فرض وتقدير' (hypothesis).
4
Study the second and third interpretations
The second is insinuation and mockery (of the idols), the third is illness of worry or fever. See how each fits the context.
These are not lies but figurative or contextual statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة في حق إبراهيم (عليه السلام) فهو أنه كذب في قوله تعالى: هَذَا رَبِّي وبَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبيرهُم وهو إِنِّي سَقِيم. والجواب: أن الأول على سبيل الفرض والتقدير، كما يوضع الحكم الذي يراد إبطاله أو على الاستفهام، أو على أنه كان في مقام النظر والاستدلال. وذلك قبل البعثة. والثاني على التعريض والاستهزاء. والثالث على أن به مرض الهم والحزن من عنادهم، أو الحمى على ما قيل.
As for the doubt regarding Abraham (peace be upon him), it is that he lied in His saying: 'This is my Lord' and 'Rather, the big one among them did it' and 'Indeed I am sick.' And the answer: the first is by way of hypothesis and assumption, as one posits the judgment he wants to refute, or as interrogative, or that he was in a position of reflection and inference. And that was before the mission. The second is by way of insinuation and mockery. And the third is that he had the illness of worry and grief due to their stubbornness, or fever as has been said.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage deals with three objections from the story of Abraham where his words appear to be lies. First, when he said 'This is my Lord' referring to a celestial body; this is interpreted as a hypothetical statement (farḍ taqdīr), like setting up the view to be refuted, or as an interrogative, or as part of his reasoning before prophethood. Second, when he claimed the big idol did the breaking; this is explained as ta‘rīḍ (insinuation) and istihzā’ (mockery) against the idol worshippers. Third, when he said 'I am sick' while he was healthy; this is understood as the sickness of worry and grief due to his people's obstinacy, or that he had a fever. Thus, none of these statements constitute actual lies contrary to infallibility.

Key Terms
فرض وتقدير
استفهام
مقام النظر
تعريض
استهزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abraham deliberately lied and thus prophets can sin by lying.
Correction: The statements are not lies but rhetorical or contextual statements that preserve infallibility.

Checkpoint
How is Abraham's statement 'I am sick' interpreted?
A) He was physically ill with a fever.
B) He had illness of worry and grief over their stubbornness.
C) He was pretending to avoid idol worship.
D) Both A and B as possible interpretations.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani defends Abraham’s infallibility by showing that his statements are not lies but rhetorical strategies or contextual expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been in a situation where telling the literal truth would have caused more harm than good? How can you, like Abraham, protect the truth while using wise speech? What specific step will you take this week to practice such discernment?
?
When you read the Qur'an or hear prophetic stories, do you immediately assume the best of the prophets, or are you quick to question their actions? What does your reaction reveal about your own heart's trust in Allah's choice of messengers?
?
This lesson shows that even the most seemingly clear 'lies' of Abraham have deeper explanations. Is there an area of your life where you judge others too quickly based on appearances? How will you train yourself to seek out possible good interpretations before condemning?

Jacob's Grief and Prophetic Infallibility
Taftazani defends Jacob's expression of grief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Jacob's intense grief for Joseph is not considered a sin against prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Natural human emotions are not disobedience — even for prophets.

This passage addresses a common doubt: did Prophet Jacob's excessive love and grief for Joseph contradict his infallibility? Taftazani provides a concise answer distinguishing between sinful acts and natural emotional inclinations. Let's read his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: objection followed by answer.
The passage is very concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The objection states that Jacob's love, grief, and weeping were excessive. The answer introduces the concept of 'mayl al-nafs' (inclination of the soul) and asserts there is no sin in it, especially for a good person like Jacob.
3
Understand 'mayl al-nafs'
This Arabic term means a natural pull or inclination of the soul, as opposed to a deliberate act of disobedience. This distinction is key to defending prophetic infallibility.
It is a natural emotion, not a sinful choice.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this answer preserves the doctrine of 'ismah (infallibility) while acknowledging the full humanity of prophets. They experience emotions but remain sinless in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة في قصة يوسف من جهة يعقوب (عليهما السلام) الإفراط في المحية والحزن والبكاء. والجواب: أنه لا معصية في ميل النفس سيما من يلوح عليه آثار الخير.
As for the doubt in the story of Joseph concerning Jacob (peace be upon both of them), it is the excess in love, grief, and weeping. And the answer: that there is no disobedience in the inclination of the soul, especially in one on whom traces of goodness appear.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This short passage addresses the objection that Jacob’s intense love for Joseph and his prolonged grief and weeping were excessive, possibly sinful. The author answers that there is no sin in a natural inclination of the soul (mayl al-nafs), especially for someone like Jacob, who was a prophet marked by goodness. Such feelings are not rebellious acts; they are human emotions that do not break infallibility as long as they do not involve disobedience.

Key Terms
إفراط
ميل النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jacob's excessive grief was a sin against his prophethood.
Correction: Natural emotional inclinations (mayl al-nafs) are not acts of disobedience; prophets are infallible only in actions, not feelings.

Checkpoint
Why is Jacob's excessive grief not considered a sin?
A) Because he repented afterwards.
B) Because the inclination of the soul is not disobedience, especially for the good.
C) Because crying is always permissible.
D) Because he did not actually commit any act.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility does not prohibit natural human emotions like love and grief; only deliberate disobedience is forbidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful loss and you weep or grieve deeply, do you ever feel guilty, thinking it shows weak faith or ingratitude? How does the example of Prophet Jacob (peace be upon him) free you from that false guilt? Will you now allow yourself to grieve without accusing yourself of sin?
?
Are you quick to judge others who express strong emotions in trials, labeling them as impatient or lacking trust in Allah? How will you change your reaction after learning that even a prophet grieved deeply and it was not disobedience?
?

The Shubha of Nuh and Its Answer
Sanusi lists the shubha against Nuh and gives three interpretative answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the integrity of prophetic statements by resolving an apparent contradiction in the Qur'anic narrative of Nuh.

Core Idea
Apparent contradictions in revelation are resolved through careful attention to linguistic context.

Sanusi now addresses a specific challenge posed against the prophet Nuh (peace be upon him). The Qur'an appears to contain a contradiction: Nuh says his son is from his family, while Allah says he is not. How can this be? Sanusi provides three interpretative answers that preserve the truthfulness of both Nuh and the divine speech. This lesson unpacks the objection and the nuanced response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. The objector claims that Allah's statement 'he is not of your family' contradicts Nuh's statement 'my son is of my family.' This seems to imply Nuh was mistaken.
Notice the objector takes both verses at face value without considering context.
2
Analyze the first answer
The first answer states that 'family' (ahl) in the divine promise refers specifically to righteous family members. Since Nuh's son was not righteous, he was not included in that promise. Thus, no contradiction.
This shows the importance of understanding the scope of terms in revelation.
3
Examine the second and third answers
The second answer interprets 'ahl' as people of the same religion. The third, based on narration, says Nuh's son was actually his stepson (from his wife) and only a stranger with good deeds is considered part of the Prophet's family.
These alternatives further illustrate how linguistic and historical context resolve apparent problems.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how Sanusi uses tafsir (exegesis) to defend the consistency of revelation. He does not deny the apparent contradiction but explains it through nuanced interpretation.
This method is common in kalam to handle shubuhāt (objections).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة في حق نوح (عليه السلام) فهو أن قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا نُوحُ إِنَّه لَيْسَ مِنْ أَهْلِكَ ﴾ (٢) تكذيب له في قوله: ﴿إِنَّ ابْنِي مِنْ أهلي (٣). والجواب أنه ليس للتكذيب بل للتنبيه على أن المراد بالأهل في الوعد هو الأهل الصالح، أو المعنى أنه ليس من أهل دينك، أو أنه أجنبي منك ، وإن أضفته إلى نفسك بأبنائك لما روي من أنه كان ابن امرأته والأجنبي إنما يعد من آل النبي إذا كان له عمل صالح
As for the objection concerning Nuh (peace be upon him): it is that His statement, the Exalted: 'O Nuh, indeed he is not of your family' (11:46) is a denial of his statement: 'Indeed my son is of my family' (11:45). The answer is that it is not for denial but for notification that what is meant by 'family' in the promise is the righteous family, or the meaning is that he is not of your religion, or that he is a stranger to you, even if you attributed him to yourself as a son due to what has been narrated that he was the son of his wife, and the stranger is only counted among the family of the Prophet if he has righteous deeds.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair concerning the prophet Nuh (peace be upon him). The objector claims that the verse 'O Nuh, indeed he is not of your family' contradicts Nuh's earlier statement 'Indeed my son is of my family', implying that Allah denied Nuh's claim. The author (Sanusi) provides three possible answers: first, the verse is not a denial but a clarification that 'family' in the promise refers to righteous family members; second, the meaning is that the son is not of Nuh's religion; third, the son is a stranger despite Nuh's calling him a son, because he was the son of his wife, and a stranger is only considered part of the Prophet's family if he has righteous deeds. Thus the apparent contradiction is resolved.

Key Terms
شبهة
جواب
نوح
أهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse directly denies Nuh, proving he was wrong.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the two verses use 'ahl' in different senses; the objection is resolved by recognizing that 'ahl' in the promise refers to righteous kin, not biological family.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the objection against Nuh?
A) The verse proves Nuh was lying.
B) 'Ahl' refers to righteous family, not biological kin.
C) Nuh's son was not really his son.
D) The verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi resolves the apparent contradiction by reinterpreting 'ahl' to mean righteous kin, not biological family, preserving both Nuh's truthfulness and Allah's speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever encountered a verse or hadith that seemed to contradict your understanding, causing you to doubt? How does this lesson challenge you to pause and seek deeper meaning rather than assume error?
?
The lesson shows that even a prophet's statement is refined by divine response. When you read the Qur'an, do you approach difficult passages with the humility that your initial understanding may need refinement? What specific step can you take this week to study a challenging verse with that mindset?
?
Allah's words are precise and context-driven. If you were in Nuh's position, would you have been upset by Allah's reply? What does this teach you about trusting Allah's wisdom even when His response differs from your expectations? How can you apply that trust in a current situation where God's decree seems to contradict your hopes?

The Shubha of Ibrahim and Its Answer
Sanusi enumerates three objections against Ibrahim and provides answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent lies of Prophet Ibrahim (peace be upon him) are defended by scholars through rhetorical and contextual explanations.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's 'lies' were rhetorical devices, not falsehoods.

The objection that Prophet Ibrahim (peace be upon him) uttered lies has troubled some. However, Muslim scholars have provided clear answers from the Quranic context. This lesson examines three statements of Ibrahim that appear problematic and the scholarly responses that vindicate his truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the three objections and the answers.
2
Identify the three objections
List the three statements of Ibrahim that are considered problematic by critics.
The statements are from Quran 6:46, 21:63, and 37:89.
3
Analyze the first answer
Focus on the explanation for Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' (6:46). Understand the concepts of hypothesis, interrogative, and pre-prophethood.
This answer is the most detailed.
4
Compare the second and third answers
See how the second and third statements are defended using allusion/mockery and illness of grief.
5
Reflect on the overall principle
Consider how these answers preserve Ibrahim's integrity and the principle that prophets are protected from lying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة في حق إبراهيم (عليه السلام) فهو أنه كذب في قوله تعالى : هَذَا رَبِّي (٤) وبَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبيرهُم ) (٥) وهو إِنِّي سَقِيم ) (٦) . والجواب : أن الأول على سبيل الفرض والتقدير، كما يوضع الحكم الذي يراد إبطاله أو على الاستفهام، أو على أنه كان في مقام النظر والاستدلال. وذلك قبل البعثة . والثاني على التعريض والاستهزاء. والثالث على أن به مرض الهم والحزن من عنادهم، أو الحمى على ما قيل .
As for the objection concerning Ibrahim (peace be upon him): it is that he lied in His statement the Exalted: 'This is my Lord' (6:46) and 'Rather, this large [idol] did it' (21:63) and 'Indeed I am sick' (37:89). The answer: the first is by way of hypothesis and supposition, just as a ruling is placed that is intended to be invalidated, or it is interrogative, or that he was in a position of consideration and reasoning, and that was before prophethood. The second is by way of allusion and mockery. The third is that he had an illness of grief and sorrow from their obstinacy, or fever as has been said.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the objection that the prophet Ibrahim (peace be upon him) made statements that appear to be lies: 'This is my Lord', 'Rather, the large one did it', and 'I am sick'. The author provides answers for each. The first statement is explained as a hypothetical premise to argue against the idolaters, like assuming something to then disprove it, or as a rhetorical question, or it occurred before his prophethood. The second statement is a form of allusion (ta'rid) and mockery (istihza') directed at their idols, not a literal claim. The third statement is interpreted as an illness of grief due to their obstinacy, or fever, which is a permissible excuse. Thus, these are not actual lies but contextually appropriate statements.

Key Terms

جواب
إبراهيم
فرض
تقدير
تعريض
استهزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibrahim outright lied.
Correction: Ibrahim's statements are explained as rhetorical devices or pre-prophethood, not actual lies.

Checkpoint
How is Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' explained in the answer?
A) It was a literal truth.
B) It was a hypothesis or rhetorical question.
C) It was a sin that he repented for.
D) It was said after prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Prophets are protected from lying; apparent falsehoods have valid interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that Ibrahim's apparent lies were actually permissible rhetoric. When you are in a debate or discussion about faith, do you ever use words that could be misunderstood as deceptive? How can you ensure your speech remains truthful while also being effective? Write down one specific conversation you had recently and evaluate your honesty.
?
Consider Ibrahim's statement about the idols: 'Rather, the large one did it.' He used mockery to expose the absurdity of idol worship. Have you ever used humor or sarcasm to correct someone's false belief? Was your intention pure, or did it come from a place of arrogance? Commit to examining your heart before engaging in such speech.
?
Ibrahim's 'illness' was grief over his people's obstinacy. When you see people rejecting the truth or persisting in sin, does it grieve you personally, or do you remain indifferent? Ask Allah to give you a heart that feels for others, and write one practical step you will take today to turn that grief into sincere dua for their guidance.

Defending Ya'qub's Grief
Sanusi defends Ya'qub against charges of excessive grief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ya'qub's grief for Yusuf is not considered sinful from an Aqeedah perspective.

Core Idea
Prophets feel natural emotions, not sinful ones.

This lesson defends the prophetic character of Ya'qub (AS) against the charge that his excessive grief for Yusuf (AS) was blameworthy. Sanusi exposes the objection and then provides a clear answer rooted in the permissibility of natural human emotions and righteous fears.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the concise objection and the two-part answer.
2
Identify the two main parts of the answer
The answer gives two reasons: (1) natural inclination of the soul is not a sin, especially for a righteous person; (2) complaining to Allah in calamities is permissible. Also note the additional point about fear for Yusuf's faith.
3
Reflect on prophetic infallibility
Consider why this defense is important for the doctrine of isma (prophetic infallibility). Natural emotions do not contradict sinlessness when they are controlled and directed properly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة في قصة يوسف من جهة يعقوب (عليهما السلام) الإفراط في المحبة والحزن والبكاء. والجواب : أنه لا معصية في ميل النفس سيما من يلوح عليه آثار الخير والصلاح ، وأنواع الكمال ، ولا في بث الشكوى والحزن إلى الله تعالى في مصائب يكون من جهة العباد سيما وقيل : إنه كان من خوف أن يموت يوسف (عليه السلام) على غير دين الإسلام
As for the objection concerning the story of Yusuf from the side of Ya'qub (peace be upon them): excessive love, grief, and weeping. The answer: there is no sin in the inclination of the soul, especially for one upon whom appear signs of goodness, righteousness, and types of perfection, nor in expressing complaint and sorrow to Allah the Exalted in calamities that come from the hands of people, especially since it is said: that he was afraid that Yusuf (peace be upon him) might die upon other than the religion of Islam.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer, this time concerning Ya'qub (peace be upon him). The objector says Ya'qub showed excessive love, grief, and weeping for Yusuf, which might imply a lack of trust in Allah. The answer is that there is no sin in natural human emotions, especially for a person of such high spiritual station. Moreover, expressing complaint and sorrow to Allah is permissible. Additionally, Ya'qub's fear was not just personal loss but concern that Yusuf might die upon other than Islam, which is a righteous concern. Thus, his actions are not blameworthy.

Misconception: Prophets must be devoid of all emotions to be sinless.
Correction: Prophets experience natural emotions like grief and love, but they are not sinful because they channel them righteously and do not act against divine commands.

Key Takeaway
Ya'qub's grief for Yusuf was not sinful because natural emotions are not blameworthy and his sorrow was directed to Allah, driven by a righteous fear for Yusuf's faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of someone you love deeply. Is your love for them tainted by worldly attachment, or is it anchored in righteous concern for their Hereafter—like Ya'qub's fear for Yusuf's faith? What would it take for your love to be purified into something Allah would not blame?
?
You have likely felt heartbreak or loss. In those moments, did you direct your sorrow to Allah in complaint, or did you numb it with distractions? Knowing that a prophet wept openly and was not sinful, what is stopping you from turning your rawest emotions into worship? Commit today to bring one specific grief to Allah in sincere du'a.

Refuting Objections on Yusuf's Story
Sanusi refutes objections regarding Yusuf's actions and his brothers' lies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes objections about the actions of Prophet Yusuf and his brothers using multiple interpretive strategies.

Core Idea
Apparent flaws in prophets' stories are resolved by context, interpretation, and distinguishing sin from nature.

Sanusi addresses common objections regarding the story of Yusuf in the Quran. Critics point to the brothers' lies, Yusuf's near-mistake, and his actions like placing the cup and accepting prostration. Sanusi provides careful explanations that preserve the integrity of all prophets involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the full translation. Let the author's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the structure: objections (shubha) followed by answers (jawab).
2
Identify the two main objections
Find where Sanusi discusses the brothers' side and Yusuf's side. Note that the brothers were not prophets, so their sins don't affect prophethood.
The brothers' lies are dismissed because they weren't prophets.
3
Analyze the answers for Yusuf's ham
Examine the three interpretations of Yusuf's 'ham': natural inclination, approximation, or conditional with 'if not for the proof'. Understand that none imply sin.
The key is that 'ham' does not mean determination to sin.
4
Understand the cup and prostration
Note that the cup placement was with Allah's permission, and prostration was a customary greeting, not worship.
Sanusi also criticizes the Hashwites for vulgar interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جهة الأخوة ما فعلوا بيوسف، وما قالوا من الكذب. والجواب : أنهم لم يكونوا أنبياء. ومن جهة يوسف الهم المشار إليه بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَلَقَدْ هَمَّتْ بِهِ وَهَمَّ بِهَا ﴾ (١) وجَعَلَ السِّقَايَةَ فِي رَحْلِ أَخِيهِ) (٢) . والرضا بسجود إخوته وأبويه له . والجواب : أن ذلك قبل البعثة أو المراد: ﴿وَهَمَّ بِهَا لَوْلَا أَنْ رَأَى بُرْهَانَ ربه (٣) على أن يكون الجواب المحذوف ما دل عليه الكلام السابق ويكون التقدير: لولا أن رأي برهان ربه لخالطها أو المراد الميلان المذكور في الطبيعة البشرية، لا الهم بالمعصية، والقصد إليها أو هو من باب المشارفة، أي شارف أن يهم بها. وبالجملة فلا دلالة ههنا على العزم والقصد إلى المعصية فضلاً عما يذكره الحشوية من الحشويات. ولهذا ورد في هذا المقام من الثناء على يوسف ما ورد من غير أن تنعى عليه زلة أو يذكر له استغفار وتوبة . وأما جعل السقاية في رحل أخيه ، فقد كان بإذنه ورضاه ، بل بإذن الله تعالى. ونسبة السرقة إلى الأخوة تورية عما كانوا فعلوا بيوسف مما يجري مجرى السرقة . أو هو قول المؤذن : والسجدة كانت عندهم تحية وتكرمة كالقيام والمصافحة. لو كانت مجرد انحناء وتواضع ، لا وضع جبهة .
And from the side of the brothers: what they did to Yusuf and what they said of lies. The answer: they were not prophets. And from the side of Yusuf: the desire indicated by His statement the Exalted: 'And she certainly desired him, and he would have desired her' (12:24), and his placing the drinking cup in his brother's bag (12:70), and his consent to the prostration of his brothers and his parents to him. The answer: that was before prophethood, or the meaning of 'and he would have desired her' (12:24) is 'if he had not seen the proof of his Lord' such that the omitted answer is indicated by the previous discourse, and the estimation is: if he had not seen the proof of his Lord, he would have mixed with her, or the meaning is the inclination mentioned in human nature, not the intention of sin and the resolve towards it, or it is from the category of approximation, i.e., he came close to desiring it. In summary, there is no indication here of determination and intent towards sin, let alone what the Hashwites mention of vulgarities. For this reason, in this context, there is praise of Yusuf without any slip being held against him or any seeking of forgiveness or repentance being mentioned for him. As for placing the cup in his brother's bag, it was with his permission and consent, rather with the permission of Allah the Exalted. And attributing theft to the brothers is a subterfuge (tawriya) for what they had done to Yusuf which is akin to theft. Or it is the statement of the caller. And the prostration was in their tradition a greeting and honor, like standing and shaking hands, if it was merely bowing and humility, not placing the forehead.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage covers multiple objections concerning Yusuf (peace be upon him) and his brothers. The first objection is about the brothers' lies and actions toward Yusuf; the answer is simply that they were not prophets, so their sins are not a problem. The second objection concerns Yusuf himself: the 'desire' (ham) mentioned in the Quran, his placing the cup in his brother's bag, and his acceptance of prostration from his brothers and parents. The answer distinguishes several explanations: these actions occurred before his prophethood; the 'desire' refers only to a natural inclination, not an intent to sin; the cup placement was with permission from Allah; the prostration was not an act of worship but a customary greeting. The author also criticizes the Hashwites (a group known for anthropomorphism or simplistic interpretations) for their coarse narratives about Yusuf. Thus, all apparent objections are resolved.

Key Terms
شبهة
جواب
يوسف
هم
سقاية
سجود
تورية
حشوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Yusuf intended to sin with the woman, and his actions (cup placement, prostration) were wrong.
Correction: The 'ham' is interpreted as natural inclination or approximation, not sinful intent. The cup placement was authorized by Allah, and prostration was a customary greeting.

Checkpoint
How does the answer explain Yusuf's 'ham' (desire) toward the woman?
A) It was a sin he repented for.
B) It was a natural inclination, not an intent to sin.
C) It was an actual sin.
D) It was a test from Allah.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that apparent moral difficulties in prophetic stories are resolved through careful interpretation, preserving prophetic impeccability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where your own desires conflict with what is right, do you honestly examine your intentions like the scholars examined Yusuf's, or do you justify your actions by blaming circumstances? What would it take for you to stop and see the 'proof of your Lord' before acting?
?
The brothers tried to cover their wrong with lies, but they were not prophets and faced consequences. How often do you deceive others or yourself to hide your mistakes, forgetting that Allah sees all? Write down one area where you need to stop covering up and start repenting.

The Shubha of Musa and Its Answer
Sanusi answers objections about Musa's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against Prophet Musa regarding the killing of the Coptic and his permission to the sorcerers is resolved in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Musa's actions were innocent mistakes or strategies, not sins.

Prophet Musa (AS) is sometimes questioned for killing a Coptic man and allowing the sorcerers to perform magic. These actions might seem contrary to prophethood, but Sanusi provides a clear answer: the killing was a mistake before revelation, and the permission was a divine strategy. Let's examine the passage and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the three elements mentioned: killing, repentance, and permission to sorcerers.
The passage is short but packed.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The objection is that Musa's actions seem blameworthy. The answer explains each one: killing was a mistake before prophethood, repentance is normal, and permission was strategic.
Sanusi's answer is concise.
3
Understand the theological principle
This shows that prophets are protected from major sins, and any apparent wrongdoing must be interpreted within the context of their mission.
This is part of isma (protection).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في قصة موسى فقتل القبطي وتوبته عنه ، واعترافه لكونه من عمل الشيطان محمول على أنه كان خطأ، وقبل البعثة، وإذنه للسحرة في إظهار السحر بقوله: (ألْقُوا مَا أَنتُمْ مُلقُونَ ) (٤) .
As for the story of Musa: the killing of the Coptic and his repentance from it, and his confession that it was from the work of Satan, is considered that it was a mistake and before prophethood, and his permission to the sorcerers to show their magic by his saying: 'Throw what you will throw' (10:80).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection against Musa (peace be upon him) regarding three matters: killing the Coptic, his repentance, and his permission to the sorcerers to display their magic. The answer is that the killing was a mistake (not intentional murder) and occurred before his prophethood, so it is not a blameworthy act. His repentance from it is consistent with prophetic behavior. As for giving permission to the sorcerers, it was a strategic move to demonstrate their weakness when Musa's miracle would overpower them. Thus, all these actions are exonerated.

Key Terms
شبهة
جواب
موسى
قتل
توبة
سحرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Prophet Musa committed a sin by killing the Coptic man.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it was a mistake before prophethood, and thus not a sin.

Checkpoint
Why is Musa's killing of the Coptic not considered a sin in the answer?
A) Because the Coptic was a disbeliever.
B) Because it was a mistake and before prophethood.
C) Because he repented immediately.
D) Because it was commanded by Allah.

Key Takeaway
Apparent faults of prophets are either mistakes before prophethood or strategic actions, not sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a mistake, do you immediately repent and humble yourself like Musa, or do you try to justify yourself? Reflect on your last mistake. Did you see it as from Satan and turn to Allah?
?
Musa allowed the sorcerers to show their magic before revealing the truth. In your life, do you sometimes allow falsehood to be displayed because you trust that truth will eventually prevail, or do you feel the need to suppress it out of fear? How can you emulate this trust?
?
The killing was before prophethood. Have you considered that your past sins or mistakes before guidance do not disqualify you from being used by Allah? How does this understanding change how you view your own past?

Defense of Prophet Joseph: Jacob's Fear and the Brothers' Sins
Defending Jacob's concern and the brothers' actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the actions of Jacob and Joseph's brothers by distinguishing their status as non-prophets.

Core Idea
Not all actions of prophets' families are prophetic flaws.

This passage addresses two preliminary objections: Jacob's worry about Joseph's faith and the brothers' deceit. The author clarifies that the brothers were not prophets, so their sins do not tarnish Joseph's prophethood. This sets the stage for a deeper defense of Joseph himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the two objections being raised.
Notice the concise style of the author.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage mentions Jacob's fear about Joseph's religion and the brothers' lies. Find these in the text.
3
Analyze the author's answer
The author quickly answers that the brothers were not prophets. Reflect on why this distinction matters for prophetic infallibility.
This shows that infallibility (isma) applies only to prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلاح ، وأنواع الكمال ، ولا في بث الشكوى والحزن إلى الله تعالى في مصائب يكون من جهة العباد سيما وقيل : إنه كان من خوف أن يموت يوسف (عليه السلام) على غير دين الإسلام ومن جهة الأخوة ما فعلوا بيوسف، وما قالوا من الكذب. والجواب : أنهم لم يكونوا أنبياء. ومن جهة يوسف الهم المشار إليه بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَلَقَدْ هَمَّتْ بِهِ وَهَمَّ بِهَا ﴾ (۱) وجَعَلَ السِّقَايَةَ فِي رَحْلِ أَخِيهِ) (٢) . والرضا بسجود إخوته وأبويه له . والجواب : أن ذلك قبل البعثة أو المراد: ﴿وَهَمَّ بِهَا لَوْلَا أَنْ رَأَى بُرْهَانَ ربه (۳) على أن يكون الجواب المحذوف ما دل عليه الكلام السابق ويكون التقدير: لولا أن رأي برهان ربه لخالطها أو المراد الميلان المذكور في الطبيعة البشرية، لا الهم بالمعصية، والقصد إليها أو هو من باب المشارفة، أي شارف أن يهم بها. وبالجملة فلا دلالة ههنا على العزم والقصد إلى المعصية فضلاً عما يذكره الحشوية من الحشويات. ولهذا ورد في هذا المقام من الثناء على يوسف ما ورد من غير أن تنعى عليه ،زلة أو يذكر له استغفار وتوبة . وأما جعل السقاية في رحل أخيه ، فقد كان بإذنه ورضاه ، بل بإذن الله تعالى. ونسبة السرقة إلى الأخوة تورية عما كانوا فعلوا بيوسف مما يجري مجرى السرقة . أو هو قول المؤذن : والسجدة كانت عندهم تحية وتكرمة كالقيام والمصافحة. لو كانت مجرد انحناء وتواضع ، ، لا وضع جبهة .
And righteousness, and types of perfection, and not in expressing complaint and grief to Allah in calamities that come from the direction of people, especially since it was said: He was afraid that Joseph (peace be upon him) would die on a religion other than Islam, and from the direction of the brothers, what they did to Joseph, and what they said of lies. The answer: They were not prophets. And from the direction of Joseph, the desire referred to in His statement the Exalted: 'And certainly she desired him, and he would have desired her' (12:24), and his placing the cup in the saddlebag of his brother (12:70). And his being pleased with the prostration of his brothers and parents to him. The answer: That was before the prophethood, or the meaning of 'and he would have desired her' is: 'He would have desired her had he not seen the proof of his Lord' (12:24), such that the omitted answer is what the previous discourse indicates, and the estimation is: Had he not seen the proof of his Lord, he would have mixed with her. Or the meaning is the inclination mentioned in human nature, not the intention of sin and the resolve to it. Or it is from the category of approximation, i.e., he was about to intend it. In summary, there is no indication here of determination and intention to sin, let alone what the Hashwiyya mention of absurdities. Therefore, in this context, there is praise of Joseph such that no slip is denounced against him nor is any repentance or seeking forgiveness mentioned for him. As for his placing the cup in his brother's saddlebag, it was with his permission and consent, indeed with Allah's permission. And attributing theft to the brothers is a dissimulation for what they did to Joseph which is akin to theft. Or it is the saying of the crier: And the prostration was among them a greeting and honor like standing and handshaking, even if it were mere bowing and humility, not placing the forehead.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defends Prophet Joseph against a series of objections. The author first addresses Jacob's fear that Joseph might die on a non-Islamic religion—but this is not a flaw. Then he addresses the brothers' actions: they lied and harmed Joseph, but they were not prophets, so their actions do not affect Joseph's infallibility. Regarding Joseph himself, three issues are raised: his 'desire' for Zulaykha (the 'ham'), the cup incident, and the prostration of the brothers and parents to him. For the 'desire', the author gives several interpretations: it was before prophethood, or the verse means he would have desired had he not seen the proof, or it was merely natural inclination not intended sin, or it was an approximation (he almost intended). In any case, it was not a resolve to sin, and no blame or repentance is recorded for Joseph in the Quran. The cup incident is justified because Joseph acted with permission from Allah and his brother, and the accusation of theft was a metaphor for what the brothers had done earlier. The prostration was a custom of greeting, not worship, so it was permissible. Thus all objections are answered.

Key Terms
العصمة
الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Jacob's fear indicates lack of trust in Allah.
Correction: Jacob's fear was for Joseph's faith, not a lack of trust.

Checkpoint
What is the author's answer regarding the brothers' lies?
A) They were forgiven.
B) They were not prophets.
C) They repented.
D) They were punished.

Key Takeaway
The brothers' sins do not affect Joseph's prophethood because they were not prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see flaws in the family members of a religious leader or prophet, do you let that shake your trust in the message? How can you separate the person from the message as this lesson teaches?
?
Jacob's primary grief was not for Joseph's absence but for his faith—do you grieve more for the worldly losses of your loved ones or for the state of their faith? What does that reveal about your priorities?
?
If you are a parent, would you fear more for your child's worldly success or for their commitment to Islam? Are you willing to be like Jacob, whose deepest concern was eternal salvation?

Interpreting Joseph's 'Desire' (Ham)
Interpreting Joseph's desire and its compatibility with infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various scholarly interpretations of Joseph's 'desire' (ham) toward Zulaykha and why it does not violate prophethood.

Core Idea
Joseph's 'desire' is not a sin but a natural inclination or a conditional intention.

The Quranic verse 'And certainly she desired him, and he would have desired her' has been a point of contention. The author presents multiple explanations to show that Joseph never actually intended to sin. These interpretations safeguard his infallibility while acknowledging his human nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the interpretations
Read the Arabic and translation, then identify each interpretation the author gives for Joseph's desire.
There are four main interpretations.
2
Understand the grammatical argument
Note the author's mention of the omitted answer in the verse and how it changes the meaning.
This is a subtle point about Arabic grammar.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that no blame is recorded for Joseph. Why is this significant?
It reinforces the doctrine of infallibility.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلاح ، وأنواع الكمال ، ولا في بث الشكوى والحزن إلى الله تعالى في مصائب يكون من جهة العباد سيما وقيل : إنه كان من خوف أن يموت يوسف (عليه السلام) على غير دين الإسلام ومن جهة الأخوة ما فعلوا بيوسف، وما قالوا من الكذب. والجواب : أنهم لم يكونوا أنبياء. ومن جهة يوسف الهم المشار إليه بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَلَقَدْ هَمَّتْ بِهِ وَهَمَّ بِهَا ﴾ (۱) وجَعَلَ السِّقَايَةَ فِي رَحْلِ أَخِيهِ) (٢) . والرضا بسجود إخوته وأبويه له . والجواب : أن ذلك قبل البعثة أو المراد: ﴿وَهَمَّ بِهَا لَوْلَا أَنْ رَأَى بُرْهَانَ ربه (۳) على أن يكون الجواب المحذوف ما دل عليه الكلام السابق ويكون التقدير: لولا أن رأي برهان ربه لخالطها أو المراد الميلان المذكور في الطبيعة البشرية، لا الهم بالمعصية، والقصد إليها أو هو من باب المشارفة، أي شارف أن يهم بها. وبالجملة فلا دلالة ههنا على العزم والقصد إلى المعصية فضلاً عما يذكره الحشوية من الحشويات. ولهذا ورد في هذا المقام من الثناء على يوسف ما ورد من غير أن تنعى عليه ،زلة أو يذكر له استغفار وتوبة . وأما جعل السقاية في رحل أخيه ، فقد كان بإذنه ورضاه ، بل بإذن الله تعالى. ونسبة السرقة إلى الأخوة تورية عما كانوا فعلوا بيوسف مما يجري مجرى السرقة . أو هو قول المؤذن : والسجدة كانت عندهم تحية وتكرمة كالقيام والمصافحة. لو كانت مجرد انحناء وتواضع ، ، لا وضع جبهة .
And righteousness, and types of perfection, and not in expressing complaint and grief to Allah in calamities that come from the direction of people, especially since it was said: He was afraid that Joseph (peace be upon him) would die on a religion other than Islam, and from the direction of the brothers, what they did to Joseph, and what they said of lies. The answer: They were not prophets. And from the direction of Joseph, the desire referred to in His statement the Exalted: 'And certainly she desired him, and he would have desired her' (12:24), and his placing the cup in the saddlebag of his brother (12:70). And his being pleased with the prostration of his brothers and parents to him. The answer: That was before the prophethood, or the meaning of 'and he would have desired her' is: 'He would have desired her had he not seen the proof of his Lord' (12:24), such that the omitted answer is what the previous discourse indicates, and the estimation is: Had he not seen the proof of his Lord, he would have mixed with her. Or the meaning is the inclination mentioned in human nature, not the intention of sin and the resolve to it. Or it is from the category of approximation, i.e., he was about to intend it. In summary, there is no indication here of determination and intention to sin, let alone what the Hashwiyya mention of absurdities. Therefore, in this context, there is praise of Joseph such that no slip is denounced against him nor is any repentance or seeking forgiveness mentioned for him. As for his placing the cup in his brother's saddlebag, it was with his permission and consent, indeed with Allah's permission. And attributing theft to the brothers is a dissimulation for what they did to Joseph which is akin to theft. Or it is the saying of the crier: And the prostration was among them a greeting and honor like standing and handshaking, even if it were mere bowing and humility, not placing the forehead.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defends Prophet Joseph against a series of objections. The author first addresses Jacob's fear that Joseph might die on a non-Islamic religion—but this is not a flaw. Then he addresses the brothers' actions: they lied and harmed Joseph, but they were not prophets, so their actions do not affect Joseph's infallibility. Regarding Joseph himself, three issues are raised: his 'desire' for Zulaykha (the 'ham'), the cup incident, and the prostration of the brothers and parents to him. For the 'desire', the author gives several interpretations: it was before prophethood, or the verse means he would have desired had he not seen the proof, or it was merely natural inclination not intended sin, or it was an approximation (he almost intended). In any case, it was not a resolve to sin, and no blame or repentance is recorded for Joseph in the Quran. The cup incident is justified because Joseph acted with permission from Allah and his brother, and the accusation of theft was a metaphor for what the brothers had done earlier. The prostration was a custom of greeting, not worship, so it was permissible. Thus all objections are answered.

Key Terms
الهم
العصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Joseph intended to commit sin with Zulaykha.
Correction: Scholars interpret the verse to show only a fleeting thought or conditional intention, not actual sin.

Checkpoint
Which interpretation suggests the verse means Joseph would have desired had he not seen the proof?
A) Before prophethood
B) Conditional meaning
C) Natural inclination
D) Approximation

Key Takeaway
Joseph's 'desire' is interpreted in ways that preserve his infallibility.

?

This lesson shows how scholars interpret ambiguous texts charitably. What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's choice of messengers? Will you commit to studying such issues with an open heart before forming opinions?
?

Will you adopt this same charitable hermeneutic in your daily judgments?
?
The author concludes that Joseph's praise without repentance proves his purity. Is there an area of your life where you are relying on lack of explicit criticism as proof of innocence, rather than actively seeking forgiveness? This lesson challenges you to examine whether your confidence is based on Allah's mercy or on your own rationalizations. Will you sincerely ask Allah to show you your true state?

The Cup Incident and the Accusation of Theft
Defending the cup incident and theft accusation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author justifies Joseph's placement of the cup in his brother's bag and the ensuing accusation of theft.

Core Idea
Joseph's actions in the cup incident were lawful and with divine permission.

The story of Joseph placing the cup in his brother's saddlebag raises questions about deception and false accusation. The author defends Joseph by stating he acted with permission, and the theft accusation was a metaphorical ruse. This lesson unpacks that defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key actions
Note the two points: Joseph placed the cup with permission, and the theft accusation was a dissimulation.
2
Understand the justification
The author says the placement was with Joseph's brother's consent and Allah's permission. Reflect on how this clears Joseph of wrongdoing.
This shows Joseph's authority and divine backing.
3
Analyze the 'dissimulation' (tawriyah)
The accusation of theft is a tawriyah (dissimulation) meaning it is not a lie but a metaphor for what the brothers had done earlier.
This avoids the charge of false accusation.
