The Prostration as a Greeting
Explaining prostration as a greeting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Joseph's acceptance of his brothers' and parents' prostration was not an act of worship but a customary greeting.

Core Idea
Prostration to Joseph was a cultural greeting, not an act of worship.

One of the objections against Joseph is that he allowed his brothers and parents to prostrate to him, which could be seen as accepting worship. The author explains that in their time and culture, prostration was a form of greeting and honor, similar to standing or handshaking. This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the key point
The author states that prostration was a greeting and honor, not worship.
2
Compare with modern customs
Reflect on how cultural practices like bowing can be respectful without being worshipful.
This helps understand the context.
3
Apply the principle
Consider why this distinction is important for upholding Joseph's prophethood.
It shows he did not accept worship.

Defending the Actions of Prophet Moses
Defense of Prophet Moses' alleged lapses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars defend Prophet Moses' apparent lapses while upholding his infallibility (عصمة).

Core Idea
Every action of a prophet is either sinless or divinely justified.

The doctrine of prophetic infallibility (عصمة) requires that prophets be free from sin after their call. But the Quran itself records incidents from the life of Prophet Moses that seem problematic: killing a Copt, allowing sorcerers to practice magic, throwing the tablets in anger, pulling Aaron's beard, and expressing shock at Khidr's actions. This passage from the commentary addresses each incident, showing that none actually violates Moses' prophetic infallibility. As you read, notice how the author provides rational explanations that align with both the text and theological principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's sequence of defenses come through.
Note that the passage covers five distinct incidents in order.
2
Identify each incident and its defense
Go through each of the five incidents: killing the Copt, sorcerers, tablets, Aaron's beard, and the comment to Khidr. For each, note the specific defense the author gives.
The author uses different strategies: pre‑prophethood, necessity, permissible, and misinterpretation.
3
Understand the underlying principles
Reflect on the two key principles used: (1) actions before prophethood are not subject to the same standard, and (2) actions after prophethood can be justified as non‑sinful or even necessary.
Also note the distinction between a sin and a mistake (خطأ).
4
Connect to the doctrine of 'isma
Consider how these explanations preserve Moses' infallibility. Why would a plain reading of the Quranic verses be insufficient without such commentary?
This shows the role of theological reasoning (kalam) in interpreting revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في قصة موسى فقتل القبطي وتوبته عنه ، واعترافه لكونه من عمل الشيطان محمول على أنه كان خطأ، وقبل البعثة، وإذنه للسحرة في إظهار السحر بقوله: (ألْقُوا مَا أَنتُمْ مُلقُونَ ) (٤) . ليس رضاء به. بل الغرض إظهار إيطاله، أو اظهار معجزته ، ولا يتم الا به وقيل لم يكن حراماً حينئذ، وإلقاء الألواح كان عن دهشة وتحير لشدة غضبه والأخذ برأس هارود، وجره إليه لم يكن على سبيل الإيذاء، بل كان يدنيه الى نفسه ليتفحص منه حقيقة الحال، فخاف هارون أن يحمله بنو إسرائيل على الإيذاء، ويفضي إلى شماتة الأعداء، فلم يثبت بذلك ذنب له ، ولا لهارون ، فإنه كان ينهاهم عن عبادة العجل ، وقوله للخضر: ﴿لَقَدْ جِئْتَ شَيْئاً نُكْرَاً ) (١) أي عجباً وما فعله الخضر كان بإذن الله تعالى.
As for the story of Moses, the killing of the Copt and his repentance for it and his admission that it was from the work of Satan are interpreted as having been a mistake and before the prophethood. And his permission to the sorcerers to show magic by his saying, 'Throw what you will throw' (10:80) was not approval of it. Rather, the purpose was to show its invalidation or to show his miracle, and it is not accomplished except by it. And it was said it was not forbidden at that time. And the throwing of the tablets was due to shock and confusion from the intensity of his anger. And his taking the head of Aaron and pulling him towards him was not for the sake of hurting, rather he was drawing him near to himself to investigate the truth of the matter from him. Aaron feared that the Children of Israel would incite him to hurt and that it would lead to the gloating of enemies. Therefore, no sin is established thereby for him nor for Aaron, for he was forbidding them from worshipping the calf. And his saying to al-Khidr (al-Khidr), 'You have certainly done a dreadful thing' (18:74) means something amazing, and what al-Khidr did was by Allah's permission.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defends Prophet Moses against several objections. First, the killing of the Copt: it is explained as a mistake that occurred before his prophethood, so it does not affect his infallibility. His repentance and admission that it was from Satan show he was not sinning knowingly as a prophet. Second, his permission to the sorcerers to show magic: this was not approval, but a setup to invalidate their magic and manifest his own miracle. It was necessary for the demonstration, and some say magic was not forbidden at that time. Third, throwing the tablets: this was due to shock and anger, not a deliberate act of disrespect. Fourth, seizing Aaron by the head and beard: this was not to hurt but to draw him close to ascertain the situation. Aaron feared being harmed by the Israelites, so no sin is proven for either of them. Fifth, his statement to Khidr, 'You have done a dreadful thing': this was an expression of amazement, not an accusation of sin, and Khidr's actions were by divine permission. Thus all apparent lapses are explained.

Key Terms
العصمة
الخطأ
قبل البعثة
السحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Moses committed a sin by killing the Copt or by his actions with Aaron.
Correction: The passage explains that the killing was a mistake before prophethood, and the actions with Aaron were not harmful but investigative; thus no sin occurred.

Checkpoint
How does the passage explain Moses' permission to the sorcerers to show magic?
A) He approved of magic.
B) It was to invalidate their magic and show his miracle.
C) He was forced by Pharaoh.
D) It was before prophethood.

Key Takeaway
All apparent lapses in Moses' behavior are explained as either pre-prophethood errors, necessary actions, or misinterpreted intentions, preserving his infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone wrongs you, do you immediately assume they intended harm? This lesson shows that even a prophet's apparent harshness (grabbing Aaron) had a good intention. How can you train yourself to give those who hurt you the benefit of the doubt—at least seventy excuses—before judging their heart?
?
You have likely made excuses for your own failures. But here, every action of a prophet is justified. Are you willing to hold yourself to a higher standard than you defend yourself? If a prophet's actions are not sins, what does that say about how you should evaluate your own excuses?
?
Moses threw the tablets in a moment of intense anger—an action that could seem disrespectful. Yet Allah did not rebuke him for it. Think of a time you lost your temper. Do you believe your anger was justified, or do you need to repent for how you expressed it? What would it mean for you to take full responsibility without making excuses?

Defense of Prophet David's Minor Slip
The author defends Prophet David's actions and explains the symbolic nature of the two litigants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic scholarly defense of Prophet David's actions in the story of Uriah's wife and the two litigants, distinguishing between a minor slip and a major sin.

Core Idea
Prophet David's error was a minor slip, not a grave sin, and the litigants were angels sent to alert him.

The story of Prophet David and Uriah's wife has been subject to misinterpretation by some groups. In this passage, the author clarifies the established facts: David proposed to a woman already proposed to by Uriah, which was a minor slip due to his high status. The two litigants were angels symbolically representing the issue to alert him. This lesson unpacks the key points of this defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the narrative and the author's argument.
Pay attention to phrases like 'لم يثبت سوى' and 'فكان زلة'.
2
Identify the established facts and the slip
Determine what is actually established about David's action and why it is considered a slip (zalla). The passage mentions only a proposal to a woman already proposed to by Uriah.
The slip is minor, not a major sin.
3
Understand the symbolic representation of the angels
The author explains that the two litigants were angels sent by Allah to alert David, and the story is a representation (tamthil), not a literal event. This refutes literalist claims.
This interpretation preserves David's innocence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في قصة داود عليه السلام، فلم يثبت سوى أنه خطب امرأة كان خطبها أوريا، فزوجها أولياؤها داود دون أوريا، أو سأل أن ينزل عنها فيطلقها، وكان ذلك عادة في عهده، فكان زلة منه لاستغنائه بتسعة وتسعين، والخصمان كانا ملكين أرسلهما الله تعالى إليه لينبهاه فلما تنبه استغفر ربه وخَرَّ راكعاً وأناب . وسياق الآيات يدل على كرامته عند الله تعالى، ونزاهته عما ينسبه إليه الحشوية، إلا أنه بالغ في التضرع والتحزن والبكاء والاستغفار استعظاماً للزلة بالنظر إلى ماله من رفي رفيع المنزلة، وتقرير الملكين تمثيل وتصوير للقصة لا إخبار بمضمون الكلام ليلزم الكذب، ويحتاج إلى ما قيل إن المتخاصمين كانا لصين دخلا عليه للسرقة، فلما رآهما اخترعا الدعوى أو كانا راعيي غنم ظلم أحدهما الآخر، والكلام على حقيقته .
As for the story of David (peace be upon him), nothing is established except that he proposed to a woman whom Uriah had proposed, but her guardians married her to David instead of Uriah, or he asked that she be released to him so that he could divorce her, and that was customary in his time. So it was a slip from him because he was satisfied with ninety-nine, and the two litigants were two angels whom Allah sent to him to alert him. When he became alert, he sought forgiveness from his Lord, fell down bowing, and repented. And the context of the verses indicates his honor with Allah and his innocence from what the Hashwiyya attribute to him, except that he was extreme in supplication, grief, weeping, and seeking forgiveness out of magnifying the slip considering his lofty station. And the report of the two angels is a representation and picture of the story, not a report of the actual speech that would entail lying. And it needs what was said that the two litigants were two thieves who entered upon him to steal, and when he saw them they fabricated the claim, or they were two shepherds, one of whom wronged the other, and the speech is according to its reality.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defends Prophet David against the accusation of grave sin. The only established incident is that he proposed to a woman who was already proposed to by Uriah, but due to custom, the woman's family chose David, or he asked that Uriah divorce her. This was a minor slip (zalla) because he already had ninety-nine wives but desired another. The two litigants who came to him were actually angels sent by Allah to alert him. Once David realized his error, he immediately repented. The verses show his high rank and exonerate him from the serious sins the Hashwiyya attribute to him. His extreme humility and weeping were because of his high station, making even a small slip seem great. The entire episode of the litigants is a symbolic representation, not a literal event, to avoid the need for lying. Some add that the litigants were actually thieves or quarreling shepherds, but the truth is the symbolic interpretation.

التمثيل

التوبة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet David committed adultery and murder to marry Uriah's wife.
Correction: The passage states only a minor slip in marriage proposal and that the litigants were angels sent to alert him, clearing him of major sins.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the nature of David's error?
A) He committed adultery with Bathsheba.
B) He had a minor slip (zalla) in a marriage proposal.

Key Takeaway
Prophet David's action was a minor slip, and the story of the litigants is a symbolic representation to teach repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Prophet Solomon's Heedlessness and Repentance
The author defends Prophet Solomon regarding his preoccupation with horses missing the afternoon prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Prophet Solomon's heedlessness regarding the afternoon prayer was a minor oversight, not a sin, and that his immediate repentance preserved his prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Prophets' minor oversights are instantly corrected by repentance, upholding their sinlessness.

This passage from the commentary addresses the incident of Prophet Solomon mentioned in Surah Sad. Critics might question how a prophet could be heedless of prayer. The text clarifies that Solomon's distraction was a minor neglect of a recommended act, and his immediate grief and action demonstrate his righteousness. Let's examine the passage to see how his response reaffirms prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2
Identify key actions

The hamstringing is a form of repentance and discipline.
3
Understand the doctrinal implication
Reflect on why this incident does not violate prophetic infallibility. The commentary explains it was a minor oversight of a recommended act, not a sin.
Prophets are protected from major sins but may have minor slips (zalla) which they immediately rectify.
4
Connect to broader belief
Consider how this story supports the doctrine of isma (infallibility) by showing immediate repentance as a sign of prophethood.

As for the story of Solomon, there are matters: one of them is what is alluded to by His statement: 'When there were shown to him in the afternoon the excellent steeds...' (38:31) etc. And that is that he occupied himself with reviewing the horses until the sun set, and he became heedless of the afternoon prayer or of a devotion he had at the afternoon time. So he grieved over that, and he called back the horses and hamstrung them.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage addresses the only major incident regarding Prophet Solomon: his preoccupation with the horses until he missed the 'asr prayer or a devotional act. The Quran alludes to this in Surah Sad. According to the author, Solomon was so engrossed in reviewing the horses that the sun set and he neglected his prayer. He immediately felt grief, called the horses back, and hamstrung them (i.e., disabled them) as a form of discipline and repentance. The story shows that Solomon's mistake was a minor oversight of a recommended act, not a sin, and he promptly repented, thus preserving his infallibility.

Defending Prophetic Infallibility: Moses and Aaron
Taftazani exculpates Moses and Aaron from accusations of sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defends the prophetic infallibility of Moses and Aaron by explaining apparent problematic actions as permissible or due to valid reasons.

Core Idea
A prophet's action always has a valid explanation; it is never a sin.

In this passage, Taftazani addresses several incidents from the lives of Moses and Aaron that might appear sinful. He provides reasons that show these actions were either permitted by Allah, necessary for a miracle, or due to a legitimate emotional reaction. Understanding this defense is crucial for affirming the doctrine of 'isma (prophetic infallibility).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the flow of arguments.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify each incident
There are three main incidents: the throwing of the tablets, Moses pulling Aaron's head, and Moses' rebuke to Khidr. Underline each.
Also note Aaron's fear of the Israelites.
3
Examine Taftazani's explanation for each
For each incident, note the specific reason Taftazani gives to show it was not a sin. Write a short note next to each.
E.g., throwing tablets was from bewilderment, not rebellion.
4
Synthesize the overall defense
Consider how all these explanations together establish that no sin is attributable to Moses or Aaron.
This reinforces the doctrine of 'isma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس رضاء به. بل الغرض إظهار إيطاله، أو اظهار معجزته ، ولا يتم الا به وقيل لم يكن حراماً حينئذ، وإلقاء الألواح كان عن دهشة وتحير لشدة غضبه والأخذ برأس هارون، وجره إليه لم يكن على سبيل الإيذاء، بل كان يدنيه الى نفسه ليتفحص منه حقيقة الحال، فخاف هارون أن يحمله بنو إسرائيل على الإيذاء، ويفضي إلى شماتة الأعداء، فلم يثبت بذلك ذنب له ، ولا لهارون ، فإنه كان ينهاهم عن عبادة العجل ، وقوله للخضر: ﴿لَقَدْ جِئْتَ شَيْئاً نُكْرَاً ) (١) أي عجباً وما فعله الخضر كان بإذن الله تعالى.
Not satisfaction with it. Rather the purpose is to show its establishment, or to show his miracle, and that is not possible except by it. And it was said it was not forbidden at that time. Throwing the tablets was due to bewilderment and confusion from the intensity of his anger. Taking hold of Aaron's head and pulling him to himself was not by way of harming, but he was drawing him near to himself to investigate from him the reality of the situation. Aaron feared that the Children of Israel would drive him to harm and that would lead to gloating of enemies. So no sin is established thereby for him nor for Aaron, for he was forbidding them from worshiping the calf. And his saying to Khidr: "You have done a strange thing" (Quran 18:74) meaning amazing. And what Khidr did was by the permission of Allah.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the prophets Moses and Aaron against accusations of sin. The author begins by stating that the purpose of certain actions was not mere satisfaction but either to establish something or to show a miracle. He addresses the incident of Moses throwing the tablets of the Law, explaining it was due to bewilderment and intense anger, not a sinful act. Then he discusses Moses grabbing Aaron by the head and pulling him; this was not to harm but to draw him close for an investigation. Aaron's fear was that the Israelites would harm him, not that Moses intended harm. Therefore, no sin is established for either prophet. Moses's rebuke to Khidr (in the Quranic story) is explained as expressing amazement, not blame. Finally, the author notes that Khidr's actions were permitted by Allah, implying no sin in Moses's objection.

النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophets commit sins like ordinary people.
Correction: Apparent sinful actions have valid explanations that preserve their infallibility.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Defense of Prophet David (AS) – A Slip, Not a Sin
Taftazani defends David from the story of Uriah's wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological defense of Prophet David regarding the incident of Uriah's wife, distinguishing between a minor slip and a major sin, and the symbolic nature of the two litigants.

Core Idea
Prophet David's action was a slip, not a sin, and the angels were sent to admonish him.

In this passage, Imam al-Taftazani addresses a controversial story about Prophet David. Many have misunderstood the Quranic account, accusing David of a grave sin. Taftazani clarifies that only a minor slip occurred, and the two litigants were angels sent by Allah to remind him. This lesson will walk through Taftazani's careful defense and the linguistic and theological principles he uses to uphold the prophet's innocence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice Taftazani's cautious wording.
The passage is a defense, so note the key claims.
2
Identify the two established facts

The slip is because David had ninety-nine wives.
3
Understand the explanation of the two litigants
Taftazani argues the litigants were angels sent to admonish David, and the account is a representation, not a literal conversation.
This refutes the Hashwiyya accusation of lying.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Note David's immediate repentance and Taftazani's emphasis on his high rank, which made a slip appear grave.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the story of David, peace be upon him, nothing is established except that he courted a woman whom Uriah had courted, so her guardians married her to David instead of Uriah, or he asked that she be divorced for him, and that was customary in his time. The matter is as it really is.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defends David against the accusation of committing adultery with Bathsheba. The author states that only two things are established: either David courted a woman that Uriah had courted and her guardians married her to David (which was customary), or he asked for her divorce. This was a slip because he already had ninety-nine wives. The two disputants were angels sent by God to admonish him. David immediately repented. The context of the Quranic verses shows his honor and innocence from what the 'Hashwiyya' (anthropomorphists) claim about him. The author explains that the account of the two litigants is a representation, not a literal report, so there is no lie. Alternative interpretations (thieves or shepherds) are mentioned but the reality is that it was a divine admonition.

Solomon's Preoccupation with Horses: Forgetfulness, Not Sin
Taftazani addresses Solomon's pre-occupation with horses and his response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defends prophetic infallibility in the story of Solomon's horses by interpreting the apparent lapse as forgetfulness and the subsequent act as penance and charity.

Core Idea
Prophets are infallible; apparent lapses are due to forgetfulness, not sin.

This lesson examines the story of Prophet Solomon's preoccupation with horses, which some might see as a lapse in devotion. Taftazani presents the incident and its defense, showing how scholars reconcile the apparent neglect with prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the content.
Notice the Quranic reference (38:31) and the sequence of events.
2
Identify the two parts: problem and answer
The passage first describes the incident (Solomon missed a prayer due to horses) and then gives the answer (it was forgetfulness; he showed remorse).
3
Understand the defense details
Focus on the key points: forgetfulness (سهو/نسيان), hamstringing as remorse and charity, and alternative interpretations (love for jihad, pronoun referring to horses, wiping as honor/testing/repair).
This shows how scholars protect prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في قصة سليمان فأمور: أحدها ما أشير إليه بقوله : إذ عُرِضَ عَلَيْهِ بِالْعَشِيِّ الصَّافِنَاتُ الجياد ...) (۲) الخ . وذلك انه اشتغل باستعراض الأفراس حتى غربت الشمس، وغفل عن العصر أو عن ورد كان له وقت العشي، فاغتم لذلك، واسترد الأفراس فعقرها . والجواب : أن ذلك كان على سبيل السهو والنسيان وعقر الجياد، وضرب أعناقها كان لإظهار الندم وقصد التقرب إلى الله تعالى والتصدق على الفقراء من أحب ماله على أن المفسرين من قال : المراد حبه للجهاد، وإعلاء كلمة الله . وضمير «توارت» للجياد لا للشمس، وإنما طفق مسحاً بالسوق والأعناق تشريفاً لها ، أو امتحاناً ، أو إظهاراً لإصلاح آلة الجهاد بنفسه .
As for the story of Solomon, there are matters: First, what is indicated by His saying: "When there were displayed before him in the afternoon the swift steeds" (Quran 38:31). That is, he busied himself reviewing the horses until the sun set, and he missed the afternoon prayer or a regular devotion he had in the evening. So he grieved for that, called back the horses, and hamstrung them. The answer: That was due to forgetfulness and oversight. Hamstringing the horses and striking their necks was to show remorse and to seek nearness to Allah and to give charity to the poor from what he loved most. Moreover, some commentators say that the intended meaning is his love for jihad and exalting the word of Allah. The pronoun 'hid' (vanished) refers to the horses, not the sun. He only began wiping their legs and necks honoring them, or testing them, or showing that he personally repaired the equipment of jihad.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage addresses the first matter concerning Solomon: the incident of the horses. The author quotes the Quranic verse showing horses were displayed to him in the afternoon. Solomon became so absorbed that he missed a prayer or devotion. He grieved and hamstrung the horses as a penance. The author then gives the answer: this was forgetfulness, not intentional sin. Hamstringing was an act of regret and charity. Some commentators reinterpret the verse as love for jihad. The pronoun in the verse refers to the horses, not the sun, and Solomon's wiping of the horses was either honoring them, testing them, or repairing them for jihad. Thus, no sin is involved.

Key Terms
سليمان
الصافنات
الجهاد
السهو
النسيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Solomon intentionally neglected his prayer, which would be a sin for a prophet.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that it was due to forgetfulness (سهو ونسيان), which is not a sin, and his subsequent actions were acts of remorse and charity.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why did Solomon hamstring the horses?
A) Out of anger
B) To show remorse and give charity
C) To prevent them from escaping
D) To punish them

Key Takeaway
Apparent prophetic lapses are excused as forgetfulness and reinterpreted as righteous acts, preserving infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you miss a religious obligation due to being absorbed in a permissible activity, do you follow Solomon's example by immediately feeling remorse and taking concrete action to make amends, or do you rationalize it away? What 'horses' in your life distract you from prayer, and what would it look like to 'hamstring' them — to sacrifice that distraction to draw closer to Allah?
?
The commentators reinterpreted Solomon's love of horses as love of jihad. In your own life, do you reinterpret your worldly pursuits as being for Allah's sake, or are they purely for your own enjoyment? When was the last time you turned a passion into a means of worship?
?
Solomon's forgetting was not a sin; but what about your own forgetfulness? Do you hold yourself accountable for missed acts of worship, or do you dismiss them as unintentional? What would genuine remorse and reform look like this week?

Solomon's Trial with His Son
Taftazani explains Solomon's trial with his son and his repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Solomon's trial with his son exemplifies 'leaving the better course' and that taking precautions does not contradict trust in Allah.

Core Idea
Even prophets can fall short of the best course; precautions are compatible with tawakkul.

This lesson explores the first narrated trial of Prophet Solomon: his attempt to protect his son by hiding him in a cloud, which resulted in the child appearing dead. Taftazani explains that this was not a sin but a departure from the more excellent reliance on Allah. We will see how the Prophet's own saying 'Tie it and trust' justifies taking means while still trusting in Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage describing Solomon's son and his reaction. Notice the key phrase 'ترك الأولى' (leaving the better course).
2
Understand 'ترك الأولى'
Learn that 'ترك الأولى' is a term used for a lesser choice that is not sinful but falls short of the ideal. Identify how Solomon's action fits this category.
This is important for understanding prophetic infallibility.
3
Connect to the hadith about tying and trusting
Reflect on the hadith 'اعقلها وتوكل' (Tie it and trust). How does this justify taking precautions while still trusting in Allah?
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this teaching applies to your own life: Can you take precautions while maintaining tawakkul? What does it mean to leave the better course?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها - ما أشير إليه بقوله : وَلَقَدْ فَتَنَّا سُلَيْمَانَ . . (١) الآية . فإن كان ذلك ما روي أنه ولذله ابن فكان يغذوه في السحابة خوفاً من أن تقتله الشياطين أو تخبله، مما دعاه ، إلى أن ألقي على كرسيه ميتاً ، فتنبه لخطئه في ترك التوكل، فاستغفر وأناب . فهذا مما لا بأس به، وغايته ترك الأولى، وليس في التحفظ، ومباشرة الأسباب ترك الامتثال لأمر التوكل على ما قال (عليه السلام) اعقلها وتوكل» (٢) . وكذا ما روي أنه قال: «الأطوفن الليلة على سبعين امرأة كل واحدة تأتي بفارس مجاهد في سبيل الله» ولم يقل : «إن شاء الله». فلم تحمل إلا امرأة واحدة، جاءت بشق ولد له عين واحدة ويد ،واحدة ورجل واحدة. فألقته القابلة على كرسيه . وأما ما روي عن حديث الخاتم والشيطان وعبادة الوثن في بيته، وجلوس الشيطان على كرسيه فعلى تقدير صحته يجوز أن يكون اتخاذ التماثيل غير محرم في شريعته وعبادة التماثيل في بيته غير معلوم له .
Second: What is indicated by His saying: "And We tried Solomon" (Quran 38:34). If that is what was narrated that he had a son and he fed him in a cloud for fear that the devils would kill him or harm him, which led to his being thrown onto his throne dead. So he realized his mistake in leaving trust in Allah, and he sought forgiveness and repented. This is fine, and its end is leaving the better course. Precautions and the use of means do not constitute abandoning the command of trust, as he (peace be upon him) said: 'Tie it and trust.' Similarly, what was narrated that he said: 'I will indeed go around to seventy women tonight, each bringing a horseman fighting in the path of Allah,' and he did not say 'If Allah wills.' So none became pregnant except one woman, who gave birth to a half-child with one eye, one hand, and one leg. The midwife threw it onto his throne. As for what was narrated about the ring, the devil, and the worship of an idol in his house, and the devil sitting on his throne, assuming its authenticity, it is possible that making statues was not forbidden in his law, and the worship of idols in his house was not known to him.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage covers the second trial of Solomon. The author discusses two narrations. First, the story of Solomon's son whom he tried to protect by hiding him in a cloud, but the child appeared dead on the throne. Solomon realized his error in neglecting reliance on Allah and repented. This is only 'leaving the better course' (ترك الأولى), not a sin. The author cites the hadith 'Tie it and trust' to show that taking precautions does not contradict trust. Second, Solomon's oath to visit seventy wives without saying 'if Allah wills,' resulting in only one child born deformed. This too is a lesson. The author then addresses the more serious stories about a ring, a devil, and idol worship in Solomon's house, stating that even if authentic, idol making might not have been forbidden in his law, and he was unaware of the idol worship.

Key Terms
سليمان
التوكل
ترك الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taking precautions means a lack of trust in Allah.
Correction: The hadith 'Tie it and trust' shows that using means while relying on Allah is the proper approach; trust does not preclude precautionary measures.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani classify Solomon's action in the story of his son?
A) A major sin
B) Leaving the better course (ترك الأولى)
C) Disbelief
D) Intentional disobedience

Key Takeaway
Solomon's trial teaches that while means are permissible, the ideal is to combine them with full reliance on Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own life, have you ever taken extreme precautions out of fear, only to later realize that your trust in Allah was lacking? What did you learn from that experience, and what will you do differently next time?
?
Solomon, a prophet, repented for merely leaving the better course. Does your heart feel the weight of your own shortcomings in trusting Allah, even when you are doing permissible things? Identify one specific area where you prioritize your planning over reliance on Allah, and commit to changing that starting today.
?
The Prophet said, 'Tie it and trust.' Are you truly tying your camel—taking legitimate means—while your heart is completely at peace with Allah's decree? Or do you find yourself anxious and overthinking even after taking precautions? What step can you take to align your heart's state with your actions?

Solomon's Oath Without 'Insha'Allah'
Taftazani narrates Solomon's oath and its result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of saying 'insha'Allah' when making future plans, as illustrated by Solomon's oath.

Core Idea
Saying 'insha'Allah' acknowledges that the future is entirely in Allah's hands.

This lesson examines the second narration of Solomon's trial: his oath to visit seventy wives without saying 'if Allah wills'. The result was only one child, born deformed. Taftazani presents this as a lesson in humility and the proper acknowledgment of Allah's sovereignty over all outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage about Solomon's oath and its outcome. Note the phrase 'لم يقل : إن شاء الله' (he did not say: if Allah wills).
2
Understand the significance of 'إن شاء الله'
Recognize that saying 'insha'Allah' is a Quranic command (Quran 18:23-24) and a sign of reliance on Allah. Reflect on why omitting it can lead to unintended consequences.
The story shows that even a prophet is not exempt from this etiquette.
3
Analyze the outcome
Consider why only one wife became pregnant and why the child was deformed. What does this teach about absolute divine will?
4
Apply the lesson to daily life
Think about how you can incorporate 'insha'Allah' into your own speech when making plans. How does this practice cultivate humility and trust?

Defending Solomon: Allegations of Idol Worship
Taftazani defends Solomon against claims of idol worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defends Prophet Solomon against serious allegations of idol worship by invoking legal possibility and lack of knowledge.

Core Idea
Even if reports about Solomon seem serious, they may be explained by differences in divine law or ignorance.

This lesson addresses the most controversial narratives about Solomon: that a devil tricked him, took his ring, and even worshipped an idol in his house. Taftazani offers a defense by suggesting that making statues might not have been forbidden in Solomon's law, and he was unaware of the idol worship occurring. This lesson shows how scholars protect prophetic infallibility through careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage where Taftazani discusses the ring, the devil, and idol worship. Note his conditional phrasing 'على تقدير صحته' (assuming its authenticity).
2
Understand the defensive strategy
Taftazani uses two arguments: (1) making statues may not have been forbidden in Solomon's shari'a, (2) the idol worship in his house was unknown to him. How do these preserve his innocence?
This shows the principle of giving the best possible interpretation to reports about prophets.
3
Compare with Islamic teachings on idolatry
Reflect that later Islamic law strictly prohibits idol-making and worship, but earlier prophets had different rulings. This highlights the concept of abrogation and diversity of divine laws.
4
Apply the principle of 'good assumption'
Consider how we should approach questionable reports about religious figures: we should seek possible excuses before condemning, as Taftazani does here.

Learning Objective

Solomon's prayer was for a prophetic miracle, not personal glory.

This lesson examines the third trial of Prophet Solomon (peace be upon him) as mentioned by Taftazani. The brief Quranic verse cited raises a question: was Solomon asking for excessive worldly power? We will see that his request was a prayer for a unique kingdom as a sign of prophethood.

Many think Solomon was being greedy, but scholars explain it was a prayer for a miracle.

Defense of Sulayman's Treatment of His Horses
The author answers an objection about Sulayman's actions with his horses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend Prophet Sulayman's actions with his horses, interpreting them as forgetfulness and acts of charity rather than wrongdoing.

Core Idea
Prophetic actions that appear problematic are explained by context and noble intentions.

This passage addresses a potential objection against Prophet Sulayman regarding his treatment of his horses. Critics might see his actions as harsh or misguided, but the author provides a nuanced defense rooted in the principles of prophetic infallibility and sincere repentance. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The objection is implied: Sulayman's hamstringing and striking horses seems wrong. The answer: it was due to forgetfulness (not intentional sin).
3
Understand the explanation for hamstringing and wiping
Hamstringing and striking were to show regret, draw near to Allah, and give charity. Wiping legs/necks was honor, test, or maintaining jihad tools.
Note the multiple interpretations offered.
4
Examine the pronoun clarification
The author clarifies that 'tawarat' refers to the horses, not the sun, to avoid a misreading.
5
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this defense upholds prophetic infallibility by reinterpreting actions as virtuous.
This connects to the broader topic of 'ismah.

والجواب : أن ذلك كان على سبيل السهو والنسيان وعقر الجياد، وضرب أعناقها كان لإظهار الندم وقصد التقرب إلى الله تعالى والتصدق على الفقراء من أحب ماله على أن المفسرين من قال : المراد حبه للجهاد، وإعلاء كلمة الله . وضمير «توارت» للجياد لا للشمس، وإنما طفق مسحاً بالسوق والأعناق تشريفاً لها ، أو امتحاناً ، أو إظهاراً لإصلاح آلة الجهاد بنفسه .
And the answer: that that was from forgetfulness and negligence, and hamstringing the horses and striking their necks was to show regret and the intention of drawing near to Allah Almighty and giving in charity to the poor of his most beloved wealth, and that some of the commentators said: the meaning is his love for jihad and elevating the word of Allah. And the pronoun of 'tawarat' refers to the horses, not the sun, and he began wiping their legs and necks as an honor for them, or as a test, or as a demonstration of repairing the tools of jihad himself.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection against Prophet Sulayman regarding his treatment of his horses. The author explains that Sulayman's actions were due to forgetfulness and not deliberate sin. Hamstringing the horses and striking their necks was actually a display of regret and repentance, intending to get closer to God and give charity from his most beloved wealth. Some commentators interpret that his love was for jihad, not the horses. The author clarifies that the pronoun 'tawarat' (covered) refers to the horses, not the sun, as some might think. Sulayman began wiping their legs and necks as an honor, or as a test, or to show that he personally maintained the tools of jihad.

Key Terms

عقر الجياد
إظهار الندم
التقرب إلى الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sulayman's hamstringing and wiping the horses' necks was a sign of cruelty or indulgence.
Correction: It was an act of charity and honor, motivated by regret and devotion to Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the main explanation given for Sulayman's actions with his horses?
A) He acted out of cruelty and anger.
B) It was due to forgetfulness and was a sign of regret and charity.
C) He was testing the horses' obedience.
D) The horses were to be sacrificed for a feast.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Sulayman's actions were not faults but were expressions of regret and charity, consistent with his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Prophet Sulayman used his love for horses to fuel jihad, but when it caused him to miss prayer, he acted decisively. What love in your life is threatening to distract you from your primary duties to Allah? What specific step can you take today to realign that love?
?
The author defends Sulayman by attributing his initial oversight to forgetfulness and showing that his later actions were acts of charity. Have you ever made an excuse for a mistake that was actually a cover for your own negligence? How can you imitate Sulayman's public regret and turn your error into an act of worship?

Prophet Sulayman's Trial: Trust vs. Precautions
The author relates the story of Sulayman's son and defends it as leaving the better option.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Prophet Sulayman's action of hiding his son was not sinful but merely leaving the better option, and how taking precautions is compatible with tawakkul.

Core Idea
Taking precautions does not contradict trust in Allah; leaving the better option is not a sin.

This lesson examines the first story in the second objection against Prophet Sulayman. Critics claimed his hiding his son showed lack of trust in Allah. The author clarifies that such caution is not blameworthy and that the Prophet's repentance was for leaving the more excellent practice, not for sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic allusion
The passage begins by referencing the Quranic verse 'And we certainly tried Sulayman...' Understand that this verse is the basis of the objection.
The verse is in Surah Sad.
2
Understand the story
The tradition says Sulayman fed his son in a cloud to protect him from devils, but the son died on the throne. Recognize that Sulayman's action was a precaution.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author says this is 'leaving the better option' (ترك الأولى), not a sin. Taking means and precautions is commanded, as the Prophet said: 'Tie it and rely.'
This reconciles human effort with reliance on Allah.
4
Reflect on the principle
Prophets are human and may choose a lesser good, but they do not sin. This helps defend prophethood against exaggerated expectations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها - ما أشير إليه بقوله : وَلَقَدْ فَتَنَّا سُلَيْمَانَ . . (١) الآية . فإن كان ذلك ما روي أنه ولذله ابن فكان يغذوه في السحابة خوفاً من أن تقتله الشياطين أو تخبله، مما دعاه ، إلى أن ألقي على كرسيه ميتاً ، فتنبه لخطئه في ترك التوكل، فاستغفر وأناب . فهذا مما لا بأس به، وغايته ترك الأولى، وليس في التحفظ، ومباشرة الأسباب ترك الامتثال لأمر التوكل على ما قال (عليه السلام) اعقلها وتوكل» (٢) . وكذا ما روي أنه قال: «الأطوفن الليلة على سبعين امرأة كل واحدة تأتي بفارس مجاهد في سبيل الله» ولم يقل : «إن شاء الله». فلم تحمل إلا امرأة واحدة، جاءت بشق ولد له عين واحدة ويد ،واحدة ورجل واحدة. فألقته القابلة على كرسيه . وأما ما روي عن حديث الخاتم والشيطان وعبادة الوثن في بيته، وجلوس الشيطان على كرسيه فعلى تقدير صحته يجوز أن يكون اتخاذ التماثيل غير محرم في شريعته وعبادة التماثيل في بيته غير معلوم له .
And the second: What is alluded to by His statement: 'And we certainly tried Sulayman...' the verse. If that is what was narrated that a son was born to him and he used to feed him in the cloud for fear that the devils would kill him or cause him confusion, which led him to being thrown dead onto his throne, so he became aware of his mistake in abandoning reliance (tawakkul), so he sought forgiveness and repented. So this is not blameworthy, and its utmost is leaving the better option, and there is no in caution and taking means leaving compliance with the command of reliance, as he (peace be upon him) said: 'Tie it and rely.' And likewise what was narrated that he said: 'I will certainly go around tonight to seventy women, each will come with a horseman fighting in the way of Allah,' and he did not say 'if Allah wills.' So only one woman conceived, and she gave birth to half a child having one eye, one hand, and one leg. The midwife threw it onto his throne. As for what was narrated about the hadith of the ring and the devil and the worship of the idol in his house, and the sitting of the devil on his throne, then assuming its authenticity, it is possible that making statues was not forbidden in his law, and the worship of idols in his house was not known to him.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against Prophet Sulayman and its answer. The objection is based on the Quranic verse about Sulayman's trial. The story goes that Sulayman had a son whom he hid in a cloud to protect him from devils, but the son died and was thrown dead onto his throne. Sulayman realized his mistake in abandoning tawakkul (reliance on God) and repented. The author argues that this is not a sin but merely leaving the better option. Caution and taking means do not negate tawakkul; in fact, the Prophet Muhammad commanded to tie one's camel and then rely on God. Another story: Sulayman vowed to visit seventy women in one night to produce warriors, but omitted 'if Allah wills,' so only one woman conceived, producing a deformed child. The author says this shows the importance of saying 'if Allah wills.' Then there is a story about the ring and the devil worshipping an idol in Sulayman's house. The author responds that even if these stories are authentic, it is possible that making statues was not forbidden in his law, and he was unaware of the idol worship in his house. Thus the prophet is not blameworthy.

Key Terms
الفتنة
التوكل
ترك الأولى
اعقلها وتوكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets must never take any precaution; absolute reliance means no human effort.
Correction: Prophets, like all humans, are encouraged to take rational means while trusting Allah. Sulayman's precaution was not a sin; he merely left the more excellent practice.

Checkpoint
How does the author categorize Sulayman's action of hiding his son?
A) A grave sin
B) Leaving the better option (ترك الأولى)
C) A recommended act
D) A neutral act

Key Takeaway
Prophet Sulayman's precaution was not a sin but merely 'ترك الأولى', demonstrating that taking means is compatible with trust in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life do you avoid taking practical steps (like Sulayman's precaution) out of a mistaken belief that it shows lack of tawakkul? What is one specific situation today where you will 'tie it and rely'?
?
Prophet Sulayman took extreme precautions, yet his son still died. How does this challenge your assumption that your careful planning can secure outcomes? Will you repent from over-reliance on your plans and surrender to Allah's decree?
?
The story shows that prophets sometimes choose the 'less excellent option.' What is one area where you consistently choose the mediocre permissible action over the excellent one? Will you commit to raising your intention and action for Allah's pleasure starting today?

Sulayman's Vow and the Importance of 'If Allah Wills'
The author narrates Sulayman's vow and the ring story, defending them as not sinful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of Sulayman's vow to visit seventy women demonstrates the importance of saying 'if Allah wills' and that apparent failures are not necessarily sins for prophets.

Core Idea
Forgetting to say 'if Allah wills' led to an imperfect outcome, but this is not a sin for a prophet.

The second part of the objection involves Sulayman's statement that he would visit seventy women to produce warriors, but he omitted 'if Allah wills.' Only one woman conceived, and the child was deformed. The author also addresses stories about a ring and devil worshipping an idol. This lesson clarifies that such events do not prove sinfulness but highlight the virtue of relying on Allah's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of the vow
Sulayman said he would go around seventy women, each to bear a horseman, but he didn't say 'if Allah wills.'
This is a well-known hadith story.
2
Understand the outcome
Only one woman conceived, and the child was deformed. The author uses this to show the necessity of adding 'if Allah wills' to one's plans.
The child was half-formed.
3
Analyze the author's defense
The author does not consider this a sin but rather a lesson. Prophets can make such imperfect choices.
4
Address the ring and devil story
Regarding the story of the ring and devil worshipping an idol, the author says even if authentic, it's possible that making statues wasn't forbidden in Sulayman's law, and he may not have known about the idol worship.
This preserves his prophetic innocence.

Sulayman's Request for a Unique Kingdom: Not Envy but Divine Purpose
The author explains why Sulayman asked for a kingdom no one else would have.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sulayman's request for a kingdom no one else would have was not an act of envy but a request for a miracle or religious purpose.

Core Idea
Sulayman's request was a miracle, not envy.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection against the prophet Sulayman: that his request for a unique kingdom stemmed from envy. By unpacking the verse and offering multiple interpretations, the author shows that Sulayman's intention was either to seek a miracle fitting his time, demonstrate the compatibility of great kingship with worship, or safeguard religious integrity. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow: the objection is stated, then the answer with multiple interpretations.
2
Identify the objection
Notice that the objection is based on the verse 'Grant me a kingdom that will not belong to anyone after me,' which some interpret as envy.
The author refutes this directly.
3
Analyze the primary answer
The author first states that it was a request for a miracle appropriate to his time, or a demonstration that great kingship does not hinder worship.
This is the main defense.
4
Examine additional interpretations
Several other explanations are given via 'وقيل' (and it was said). Note each: a religious kingdom, a kingdom not deposed, hidden kingdom (contentment), and fear of others' inability to maintain limits.
These further remove any notion of envy.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how all these interpretations show that Sulayman's request was virtuous and practical, not selfish.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها - ما يشعر به قوله تعالى: وَهَبْ لِي مُلْكَاً لَا يَنْبَغِي لأحَدٍ مِنْ بَعْدِي ) (۳). من الحسد، وعدم إرادة الخير للغير. والجواب : أن ذلك لم يكن حسداً، بل طلباً للمعجزة على وفق ما زمانه، ولاق بحاله فإنهم كانوا يفتخرون في ذلك العهد بالملك والجاه، وهو كان ناشئاً في بيت الملك والنبوة، ووارثاً لهما، أو إظهاراً لإمكان طاعة الله وعبادته مع هذا الملك العظيم . وقيل : أراد ملكاً لا يورث مني، وهو ملك الدين، لا الدنيا، أو ملكاً لا أسلبه، ولا يقوم فيه غيري مقامي ، كما وقع ذلك مرة . وقيل : ملكاً خفياً لا ينبغي للناس وهي القناعة . وقيل : كان ملكه عظيماً ، فخاف أن لا يقوم غيره بشكره ولا يحافظ فيه على حدود الله
And the third: What is suggested by His statement: 'Grant me a kingdom that will not belong to anyone after me.' (from envy and not desiring good for others). And the answer: That was not envy, but rather a request for a miracle appropriate to his time and fitting for his situation, for they used to boast in that era of kingship and status, and he was raised in the house of kingship and prophethood and inherited both, or a demonstration of the possibility of obedience and worship of God with this great kingship. And it was said: He intended a kingdom that would not be inherited from me, and that is the kingdom of religion, not the world, or a kingdom from which I will not be deposed, and no one else will take my place, as had happened once. And it was said: a hidden kingdom that does not befit people, and that is contentment. And it was said: His kingdom was great, and he feared that no one else would be able to thank for it or preserve the limits of God in it.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection against Sulayman, based on his request to God for a unique kingdom that no one else would have after him. The objection claims this stemmed from envy and not willing good for others. The author refutes this by offering several interpretations. First, Sulayman asked for a miracle suited to his time, as people then prided themselves on kingship and status, and he was raised in that environment. Alternatively, he wanted to demonstrate that great kingship does not prevent obedience to God. Other interpretations: he meant a religious kingdom that would not be inherited, or a kingdom where he would not be deposed (as had happened once before), or it refers to contentment (qana'a), or he feared that no one else could maintain the gratitude and limits of God. Thus, his request was not sinful but rather virtuous or practical.

Key Terms
الملک
الحسد
المعجزة
القناعة
حدود الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sulayman's request for a unique kingdom was due to envy and greed.
Correction: The scholars explain it was either a request for a miracle, a demonstration of religious devotion, or a desire to protect the kingdom's religious aspect.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is NOT a possible interpretation of Sulayman's request for a unique kingdom?
A) He wanted a miracle fitting for his time.
B) He feared others would not be able to handle such kingship.
C) He wanted to be the only king forever out of pride.
D) He wanted a kingdom of religion, not the world.

Key Takeaway
Sulayman's request for a unique kingdom was not from envy but from a desire for a miracle and to ensure the proper maintenance of God's limits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you desire something unique or special for yourself, is it driven by a desire to serve God or by envy of others? Examine your last ambition.
?
Sulayman feared that no one else could maintain gratitude and God's limits in his kingdom. Are you grateful for the blessings you have, and do you fear losing them because of your own shortcomings?
?
The author says Sulayman's request was a miracle to show that great power does not preclude worship. Do you use your own status or abilities as an excuse to neglect worship, or as a platform for obedience?

Defense of Prophet Yunus: Anger and Patience
Defense of Prophet Yunus: three objections answered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theological defense of Prophet Yunus reconciles apparent criticism in the Quran with his prophetic perfection.

Core Idea
Prophetic anger is directed at disbelief, not at God.

This passage addresses a potential objection regarding Prophet Yunus’s actions described in Surah al-Anbiya. Some might think the verses imply disobedience or doubt in God’s power. The author provides a careful interpretation that preserves Yunus’s status as a prophet while acknowledging a minor human imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how the author responds to each apparent criticism.
The passage uses tafsir of three Quranic verses.
2
Identify the three objections
The passage addresses: (1) Yunus going away in anger, (2) thinking God would not take him to task, (3) calling himself a wrongdoer.
Each objection is reinterpreted by the author.
3
Understand the linguistic reinterpretation
The phrase 'lā naqdira' means 'restrict', not 'have power over'. The author cites another Quranic usage as evidence.
This avoids claiming Yunus doubted God's power.
4
Grasp the concept of 'tark al-afḍal'
Yunus calling himself a wrongdoer means he left the better option (patience), not that he committed a sin.
This preserves prophetic infallibility regarding major sins.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Prophets can make minor mistakes of leaving the better option, but they repent. This defends Yunus's status.
The verse 'Be not like the companion of the fish' advises patience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في قصة يونس مما يشعر به قوله تعالى : ( وذا النون إذ ذهب مغاضباً فظن أن لن نقدر عليه) (۱) فالجواب أن المغاضبة على الكفار المعاندين، لا على الله تعالى . ومعنى: لن نقدر»، لن نضيق عليه كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ فقدر عليه رزقه (۲) فلا يوجب شكاً في القدرة. ومعنى الظلم في قوله : إني كنت من الظالمين ) (۳) ترك الأفضل وهو الصبر . وهذا معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تكن كصاحب الحوت ) (٤) أي في ترك الصبر على معاندة الكفار.
As for the story of Yunus, what is suggested by His statement: 'And the man of the fish, when he went away in anger and thought that We would not take him to task [or have power over him].' So the answer: his anger was against the stubborn disbelievers, not against Allah Almighty. And the meaning of 'We would not take him to task' is We would not be hard on him, as in His statement: 'And when He tests him by restricting his provision,' so it does not imply doubt in power. And the meaning of injustice in his words: 'I was among the wrongdoers' is leaving the better option, which is patience. And this is the meaning of His statement: 'And be not like the companion of the fish,' that is, in leaving patience against the stubbornness of the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defends Prophet Yunus from the apparent criticism in the Quranic verses. The objection is that Yunus went away in anger and thought God would not be able to punish him. The author clarifies that the anger was towards the disbelievers, not God. The phrase 'thought that We would not have power over him' (لن نقدر عليه) does not mean doubting God's power; rather, it means 'We would not be hard on him' (لن نضيق عليه), as in another verse. His statement 'I was among the wrongdoers' does not mean he committed a sin; rather, it means he left the better option, which was patience. The lesson is that even prophets can make minor mistakes of leaving the better option, but they repent.

Correction: His anger was against the disbelievers, not God; 'lā naqdira' means 'restrict', and 'wrongdoer' means leaving the better option, not sin.

Defending the Prophet's Infallibility: Interpreting Forgiveness and Guidance Verses
The author interprets verses about the Prophet's forgiveness and being 'lost' as referring to minor slips, leaving the better option, and ignorance of rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how verses that seem to impute sin or error to the Prophet Muhammad are interpreted by Sunni scholars to preserve his infallibility, referring instead to minor slips, leaving the better option, and pre-prophetic ignorance of rulings.

Core Idea
The Prophet's 'sins' are only minor oversights, not moral failures.

The Quran contains verses that appear to command the Prophet to seek forgiveness or state that he was 'lost' before guidance. A superficial reading might suggest the Prophet committed real sins or was spiritually wayward. In this passage, the author clarifies that these verses refer to minor slips (zalla), leaving the better option (tark al-afdal), and his state before receiving divine law—all of which are consistent with his infallibility from major sins and disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two types of verses being discussed: those about forgiveness and the one about being 'lost'.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to the terms 'zalla' and 'tark al-afdal'.
2
Identify the interpretations
The forgiveness verses are interpreted as referring to a slip (zalla) and leaving the better option (tark al-afdal). The 'lost' verse is interpreted as loss of divine laws and rulings, or literally getting lost as a child.
These interpretations prevent attributing real sins to the Prophet.
3
Understand the doctrinal purpose
Reflect on why these interpretations are necessary: they uphold the doctrine of 'isma (infallibility) of the Prophet from major sins and disbelief.
The author is defending the Prophet's perfect moral character.
4

Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you can identify the interpretations given in the passage.

Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defends the Prophet Muhammad from the implication that he had real sins. The verses that command him to seek forgiveness or state that he was forgiven are interpreted as referring to minor slips (zalla) and leaving the better option (tark al-afdal), not actual sins. Another interpretation is that it refers to his literal getting lost as a child in the mountain paths of Mecca. Thus, all such verses are explained in a way that preserves the Prophet's infallibility from major sins and disbelief.

Prophet Solomon's Request: Not Envy but Miracle
Response to accusation of envy against Solomon, explaining his request for a unique kingdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Prophet Solomon's request for a unique kingdom was not motivated by envy but was either a miracle appropriate to his time or a sign of his special status.

Core Idea
Solomon's request was a miracle, not envy.

This lesson addresses a common objection against Prophet Solomon: that his prayer for a unique kingdom (as mentioned in the Quran) stemmed from envy. The answer provides several interpretations that absolve him of this accusation. We will explore each explanation and understand how they align with the principles of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise structure: first the objection is stated, then the answer begins with 'والجواب'.
The objection claims Solomon's request was from envy.
2
Identify the primary answer
The first part of the answer states it was a request for a miracle (معجزة) suited to his time and status. Understand why this is significant in the context of prophetic signs.
3
Examine the alternative interpretations
The passage lists several interpretations introduced by 'وقيل' (and it was said). Each offers a different perspective on the nature of the kingdom Solomon sought. List them and note how each refutes envy.
There are at least four distinct interpretations.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider why someone might think Solomon was envious. The lesson shows that the request was either a miracle, a religious kingdom, a safeguard against deposition, or a sign of contentment—all noble motives.
The key is that prophets are protected from such vices.
5
Summarize the lesson's takeaway
Conclude that Solomon's request was divinely sanctioned and free from blame. All interpretations serve to uphold his prophetic integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الحسد، وعدم إرادة الخير للغير. والجواب : أن ذلك لم يكن حسداً، بل طلباً للمعجزة على وفق ما زمانه، ولاق بحاله فإنهم كانوا يفتخرون في ذلك العهد بالملك والجاه، وهو كان ناشئاً في بيت الملك والنبوة، ووارثاً لهما، أو إظهاراً لإمكان طاعة الله وعبادته مع هذا الملك العظيم . وقيل : أراد ملكاً لا يورث مني، وهو ملك الدين، لا الدنيا، أو ملكاً لا أسلبه، ولا يقوم فيه غيري مقامي ، كما وقع ذلك مرة . وقيل : ملكاً خفياً لا ينبغي للناس وهي القناعة . وقيل : كان ملكه عظيماً ، فخاف أن لا يقوم غيره بشكره ولا يحافظ فيه على حدود الله
From envy, and not desiring good for others. The answer: That was not envy, but rather a request for a miracle according to the custom of his time, and befitting his situation—for they used to boast in that era of kingship and status, while he grew up in the house of kingship and prophethood, inheriting both; or to show the possibility of obeying God and worshiping Him along with that great kingdom. And it was said: He desired a kingdom not to be inherited from him, which is the kingdom of religion, not the world; or a kingdom from which I am not deposed, and no one else takes my place, as had happened once. And it was said: A hidden kingdom not befitting people, which is contentment. And it was said: His kingdom was great, and he feared that no one else would give thanks for it or uphold the limits of God in it.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning Prophet Solomon. The objection states that Solomon's request for a unique kingdom (as mentioned in the Quran) stemmed from envy and a lack of goodwill towards others. The answer provides several explanations. First, it was not envy but a request for a miracle appropriate to his time, as people boasted of kingship and status, and he was born into a house of both kingship and prophethood. Alternatively, it was to show that one can obey God while possessing great power. Other interpretations: he sought a religious kingdom not inherited by others, or a kingdom from which he would not be deposed, or a hidden kingdom of contentment, or he feared that no one else could properly thank God for such a great kingdom. All these explanations absolve him of envy.

Misconception: Prophet Solomon's request was motivated by envy towards others who had worldly power.
Correction: The request was either a miracle, a religious kingdom, or a sign of contentment—all blameworthy motives are excluded.

Answering the Objection Regarding Prophet Jonah's Anger
Explanation of Jonah's 'going angrily' and the meaning of 'We would not constrain him'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent objection from Prophet Jonah's story is resolved by correctly interpreting key Quranic terms.

Core Idea
Jonah's anger was against disbelievers, not God; his 'wrongdoing' was leaving patience.

The story of Prophet Jonah contains verses that some might misunderstand as implying sin or doubt. This passage provides a careful theological clarification, showing that Jonah's actions were not sinful but merely leaving the more excellent patience. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author addresses each potential misunderstanding.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the three clarifications
The passage makes three distinct points: (1) Jonah's anger was directed at disbelievers, not God; (2) 'We would not constrain' means restrict, not doubt power; (3) 'wrongdoing' refers to leaving the better option of patience.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider why it is important to interpret ambiguous verses in a way that upholds the perfection of prophets and God's attributes.
This protects divine transcendence and prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في قصة يونس مما يشعر به قوله تعالى : ( وذا النون إذ ذهب مغاضباً فظن أن لن نقدر عليه) فالجواب أن المغاضبة على الكفار المعاندين، لا على الله تعالى . ومعنى: لن نقدر»، لن نضيق عليه كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ فقدر عليه رزقه ﴾ فلا يوجب شكاً في القدرة. ومعنى الظلم في قوله : إني كنت من الظالمين ) ترك الأفضل وهو الصبر . وهذا معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تكن كصاحب الحوت ) أي في ترك الصبر على معاندة الكفار.
And regarding the story of Jonah, which is suggested by His saying: 'And the man of the fish when he went away in anger and thought that We would not constrain him.' The answer: The anger was against the obstinate disbelievers, not against God, may He be exalted. And the meaning of 'We would not constrain' is 'We would not be strict with him' as in His saying: 'But He restricts his provision' so it does not imply doubt in [God's] power. And the meaning of wrongdoing in his saying: 'Indeed I was of the wrongdoers' is leaving the better, which is patience. And this is the meaning of His saying: 'And be not like the companion of the fish,' i.e., in leaving patience regarding the obstinacy of the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage answers an objection about Prophet Jonah (Yunus). The objection arises from the verse 'when he went away in anger and thought that We would not constrain him.' The answer clarifies that his anger was against the obstinate disbelievers, not against God. The phrase 'We would not constrain' is interpreted as 'We would not be strict with him,' using the same root as in 'He restricts his provision,' so it does not imply doubt in God's power. Jonah's statement 'I was of the wrongdoers' means he left the better option, which was patience. The Quranic warning 'be not like the companion of the fish' refers to leaving patience in the face of stubborn disbelief. Thus, Jonah did not commit sin but merely left the more excellent action.

Key Terms

نقدر (ضيق)
ظالم
ترك الأفضل
صبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Jonah doubted God's power ('thought that We would not constrain him') implies a flaw in his prophethood.
Correction: The word 'nuqaddir' means 'restrict' not 'be unable', so Jonah did not doubt God's power. His 'anger' was against disbelievers, and his 'wrongdoing' was leaving the better choice of patience.

Key Takeaway

Defending Prophet Muhammad: Understanding Apparent 'Sins' in the Quran
Defense of the Prophet against apparent sin verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars interpret Quranic verses that seem to attribute sins to the Prophet Muhammad, explaining them as slips or leaving the better.

Core Idea
Apparent 'sins' of the Prophet are actually 'tark al-afdal' — leaving the better, not disobedience.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a critical theological concern: several Quranic verses, if taken literally, might suggest that the Prophet Muhammad committed sins. The author systematically explains each verse, showing that they refer to minor slips (zallah) or choosing a less optimal course (tark al-afdal) rather than actual disobedience. Let's explore these defenses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the author's method of addressing each verse.
2
List the verses and their apparent implications
Identify each Quranic verse mentioned. What does each verse seem to imply about the Prophet?
Verses include: ask forgiveness, found you erring, removed your burden, etc.
3
Identify the author's uniform explanation
For each verse, note how the author interprets it. Look for key terms like 'zallah' (slip) and 'tark al-afdal' (leaving the better).
The author consistently avoids attributing sin.
4
Connect to prophetic infallibility (ismah)
Reflect on why this interpretation is necessary. The doctrine of ismah requires that prophets are protected from major sins.
5
Summarize the principle
In your own words, explain that prophets may commit slips or leave the better, but not sins of disobedience.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في حق نبينا فمثل : واسْتَغْفِرْ لِذَنْبِكَ (ه) ولقد تاب الله على النبي (٦) وليغفر لك الله ما تقدم من ذنبك ) (۷) . محمول على ما فرط منه من الزلة وترك الأفضل ، وقوله : ووجدك ضالاً فهدی (۸). معناه فقدان الشرائع والأحكام وقيل : إنه ضل في صباه في بعض شعاب مكة، فرده أبو جهل إلى عبد المطلب . وقيل : ضل في طريق الشام حين خرج به أبو طالب. وبالجملة لا دلالة على العصيان، والميل عن طريق الحق . ولذا قال : ما ضل صاحبكم وما غوى ) (۱) وقوله : ( ووضعنا عنك وزرك) (٢ ) . ثم لما كان يثقل عليه، ويغمه من فرطاته قبل النبوة، أو من جهله بالشرائع والأحكام، أو من تهالكه على إسلام أولي العناد وتلهفه . وقوله : عفا الله عنك لم أذنت لهم (۳) تلطف في الخطاب، وعتاب على ترك الأفضل، وإرشاد إلى الاحتياط في تدبيره الخيرات. قوله : ما كان أن يكون له أسرى .. إلى قوله : لولا كتاب من الله سبق لمسكم فيما أخذتم فيه عذاب عظيم . عتاب على ترك الأفضل، وهو أن لا يرضى باختيار الصحابة الفداء. وكذا الكلام في قوله : ولم تحرم ما أحل الله لك ) (٥) وقوله تعالى : عبس وتولى أن جاءه الأعمى ) (٦) .
And regarding our Prophet, for example: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin' and 'Indeed Allah has pardoned the Prophet' and 'That Allah may forgive you your preceding sin.' These are interpreted as what proceeded from him of a slip and leaving the better. And His saying: 'And He found you erring and guided.' Its meaning is the lack of the laws and judgments; and it was said that he got lost in his childhood in some valleys of Mecca, and Abu Jahl returned him to Abdul Muttalib; and it was said he got lost on the road to Syria when Abu Talib took him out. In summary, there is no indication of disobedience or deviating from the path of truth. Hence He said: 'Your companion has not strayed nor has he erred.' And His saying: 'And We removed from you your burden.' Then he was saddened and grieved by his slips before prophethood, or by his ignorance of the laws and judgments, or by his eagerness for the Islam of the obstinate and his longing. And His saying: 'May Allah pardon you, why did you give them permission?' is gentleness in address, reproof for leaving the better, and guidance to caution in managing good deeds. His saying: 'It is not for a Prophet to have captives... until His saying: 'Had it not been for a decree from Allah that preceded, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took.' This is reproof for leaving the better, which is that he was not pleased with the companions' choice of ransom. Similarly, the discussion applies to His saying: 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' and His saying: 'He frowned and turned away because the blind man came to him.'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a continuous defense of Prophet Muhammad against apparent implications of sin in several Quranic verses. The author lists verses and provides uniform explanations: they refer to 'zallah' (slip) or 'tark al-afdal' (leaving the better), not actual sin. For 'istaghfir li-dhanbika' (ask forgiveness for your sin), it is a slip or leaving the better. For 'found you erring and guided,' it means lacking knowledge of religious laws before prophethood, or a physical getting lost in childhood. For 'We removed your burden,' it refers to the weight of pre-prophethood slips or ignorance. For 'May Allah pardon you, why did you give them permission?' it is a gentle reproof for a less optimal decision. For the captives verse, it reproofs the choice of ransom over execution. For 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful?' and 'He frowned and turned away,' the same principle applies: reproof for leaving the better. All these are not sins but lesser choices.

ذنب
زلة
ترك الأفضل
ضال
عتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these Quranic verses prove that prophets can commit sins of disobedience.
Correction: The verses are interpreted as referring to minor slips (zallah) or leaving the better (tark al-afdal), not actual sins, preserving prophetic infallibility.

Checkpoint

D) The Prophet was ignorant of the laws and judgments.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verses that seem to accuse the Prophet of sin are consistently interpreted as minor slips or leaving the better, preserving his perfect obedience and prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Exonerating the Prophet from the Gharaniq Incident
Enumeration of exonerating explanations for the Satanic verses incident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various scholarly explanations that exonerate the Prophet from the alleged 'Satanic verses' incident.

Core Idea
The Prophet is protected from deliberate error; any report to the contrary is interpretively resolved.

The report of the Prophet reciting praise of idols (the 'Satanic verses') is a famous objection to his infallibility. This passage presents several answers that clarify what really happened, all of which affirm his prophetic protection. We will explore each explanation in turn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the multiple explanatory phrases beginning with 'وقيل' (and it was said).
The passage presents four distinct answers.
2
Identify the four answers
Underline each explanation: (1) Satanic casting, not intentional; (2) 'gharaniq' means angels; (3) it was abrogated Qur'an; (4) the 'wish' is inner speech and whispers were removed.
These are not mutually exclusive; they are alternative interpretations.
3
Understand the doctrinal principle
Ask yourself: What core belief about prophets is preserved by each of these answers? All maintain that the Prophet never intentionally spoke falsehood about Allah.
This connects to the broader doctrine of 'isma (prophetic infallibility).

Source Text

وما روي من أنه قرأ بعد قوله : أفرأيتم اللات والعزى ومناة الثالثة الأخرى (۷) تلك الغرانيق العلى وإن شفاعتها لترتجى» فلما أخبره جبريل بما منه، حزن وخاف خوفاً شديداً، فنزل قوله تعالى : ( وما أرسلنا من قبلك من رسول ولا نبي إلا إذا تمنى ألقى الشيطان في أمنيته (۸) . تسلية له . فالجواب : أنه كان من إلقاء الشيطان لا تعمداً منه . وقيل : بل الغرانيق هي الملائكة . وكان هذا قرآن فنسخ . وقيل : معنى تمنى النبي حديث النفس. وكان الشيطان يوسوس إليه غير الهدى، فينسخ الله وساوسه من نفسه، ويهديه إلى الصواب.
And what has been related that he recited after his saying: 'Have you considered al-Lat and al-Uzza and Manat, the third, the other?
