Commentary
This passage addresses the 'Satanic verses' incident, where the Prophet allegedly recited words praising pagan idols. The answer provides several exonerating explanations. First, it was an interpolation by Satan, not intentional by the Prophet. Second, 'gharaniq' refers to angels, not idols. Third, this was a valid Quranic recitation that was later abrogated. Fourth, the 'wish' means inner thought, and Satan's whisperings were removed by God. In all interpretations, the Prophet is free from any deliberate error or sin.

Prophetic Lapses as Gentle Reproach for Leaving the Better
The author interprets Quranic verses as عتاب for ترك الأولى.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses that appear to reproach the Prophet are interpreted as gentle reproach (عتاب) for not choosing the best course of action, rather than as indications of sin, in defense of prophetic sinlessness (عصمة).

Core Idea
Prophetic reproach is affectionate guidance, not punishment for sin.

When we read verses like 'May Allah forgive you' addressed to the Prophet, it may seem that he committed a sin. However, classical scholars interpret these as gentle reproach (عتاب) for leaving the better option (ترك الأولى). This lesson explores how several Quranic verses are understood in light of the doctrine of prophetic sinlessness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's method of interpreting each verse.
The passage references specific verses and stories.
2
Identify the key concept of عتاب
Understand that عتاب is a gentle reproach, not a rebuke for sin. The author uses this term to describe the divine address.
3
Examine each cited verse
The author mentions verses from Surah An-Najm, Al-Inshirah, At-Tawba, Al-Anfal, At-Tahrim, and Abasa. See how each is explained as reproach for leaving the better.
4
Distinguish between sin and leaving the better
Grasp the difference between a sinful act and a suboptimal choice. The author argues that the Prophet's actions were not sinful but less than perfect.
This preserves عصمة.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكة، فرده أبو جهل إلى عبد المطلب . وقيل : ضل في طريق الشام حين خرج به أبو طالب. وبالجملة لا دلالة على العصيان، والميل عن طريق الحق . ولذا قال : ما ضل صاحبكم وما غوى ) (۱) وقوله : ( ووضعنا عنك وزرك) (٢ ) .

ثم لما كان يثقل عليه، ويغمه من فرطاته قبل النبوة، أو من جهله بالشرائع والأحكام، أو من تهالكه على إسلام أولي العناد وتلهفه .

وقوله : عفا الله عنك لم أذنت لهم (۳) تلطف في الخطاب، وعتاب على ترك الأفضل، وإرشاد إلى الاحتياط في تدبيره الخيرات. قوله : ما كان أن يكون له أسرى .. إلى قوله : لولا كتاب من الله سبق لمسكم فيما أخذتم فيه عذاب عظيم . عتاب على ترك الأفضل، وهو أن لا يرضى باختيار الصحابة الفداء. وكذا الكلام في قوله : ولم تحرم ما أحل الله لك ) (٥)

وقوله تعالى : عبس وتولى أن جاءه الأعمى ) (٦) .
Makkah, Abu Jahl returned him to Abdul-Muttalib. And it is said: He got lost on the road to Sham when Abu Talib took him out. In summary, there is no indication of disobedience or deviation from the path of truth. Therefore He (Allah) said: 'Your companion has not strayed nor erred' (1) and His saying: 'And We removed from you your burden' (2).

Then because it weighed heavily upon him and grieved him due to his slips before prophethood, or due to his ignorance of the laws and rulings, or due to his intense eagerness for the conversion of the obstinate and his yearning, His saying: 'May Allah forgive you, why did you permit them?' (3) is gentleness in address, and a reproach for leaving the better, and guidance toward caution in managing good deeds. His saying: 'It is not for a prophet to have captives... until His saying: 'Had it not been for a previous decree from Allah, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took' (4) is a reproach for leaving the better, which is that he should not have accepted the Companions' choice of ransom. Similarly is the discussion concerning His saying: 'And why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' (5) and His saying, exalted: 'He frowned and turned away because the blind man came to him' (6).

Commentary
This passage is a theological defense of prophetic sinlessness (عصمة الأنبياء) by interpreting several Quranic verses that appear to blame the Prophet as actually being gentle reproach (عتاب) for leaving the better option (ترك الأولى), not for actual sin. The author begins by referencing a story about the Prophet's early life (being lost or returned by Abu Jahl) and emphasizes that such events do not indicate disobedience. He then cites verses from Surah An-Najm (53:2) and Surah Al-Inshirah (94:2) to show that the Prophet did not err. The author explains that the verses reproaching the Prophet (e.g., 'May Allah forgive you, why did you permit them?' from Surah At-Tawba 9:43, the captives verse from Surah Al-Anfal 8:68, the verse forbidding what is lawful from Surah At-Tahrim 66:1, and the 'frowned and turned away' verse from Surah Abasa 80:1-2) are all forms of عتاب, which is a gentle, affectionate reproach for not choosing the best course of action, not a rebuke for sin. This preserves the doctrine that prophets are protected from major and minor sins, while allowing for 'lapses' (زلات) in matters of preference.

The Incident of the غرانيق and Its Defenses
Recounts gharaniq story and provides four defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various scholarly defenses against the claim that the Prophet (ﷺ) accidentally praised pagan idols during the gharaniq incident.

Core Idea
The Prophet's infallibility is preserved through multiple plausible explanations that exonerate him.

This lesson examines a controversial incident reported in some historical sources, where the Prophet (ﷺ) is said to have recited words praising pagan deities. The passage provides several answers to defend the Prophet from any accusation of intentional sin. We will explore each defense and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Note the sequence: story, distress, revelation of the consolation verse, then multiple answers.
The passage is dense with multiple interpretations.
2
Identify the four defenses
The text gives four distinct answers. List them: 1) Satan's casting, 2) Gharaniq are angels, 3) It was abrogated Qur'an, 4) It was inner thought with whisper removed.
Each defense independently preserves the Prophet's sinlessness.
3
Understand the primary defense
The first and main answer is that it was from 'Iqaa' ash-Shaytan' (Satan's casting), not intentional on the Prophet's part. The verse of consolation supports this.
This aligns with Quranic principle that prophets are protected from deliberate error.
4
Consider the alternative interpretations
The other defenses provide additional layers: 'gharaniq' could mean angels (no idol praise), the words were originally revealed then abrogated, or 'tamanna' refers to self-talk cleansed by Allah.
These show the richness of scholarly interpretation.
5
Reflect on the significance
Why is it important to have these defenses? They affirm the doctrine of 'Isma (infallibility) and show that even controversial narratives can be reconciled with prophetic integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما روي من أنه قرأ بعد قوله : أفرأيتم اللات والعزى ومناة الثالثة الأخرى (۷) تلك الغرانيق العلى وإن شفاعتها لترتجى» فلما أخبره جبريل بما منه، حزن وخاف خوفاً شديداً، فنزل قوله تعالى : ( وما أرسلنا من قبلك من رسول ولا نبي إلا إذا تمنى ألقى الشيطان في أمنيته (۸) . تسلية له . فالجواب : أنه كان من إلقاء الشيطان لا تعمداً منه . وقيل : بل الغرانيق هي الملائكة . وكان هذا قرآن فنسخ . وقيل : معنى تمنى النبي حديث النفس. وكان الشيطان يوسوس إليه غير الهدى، فينسخ الله وساوسه من نفسه، ويهديه إلى الصواب.
And what is narrated that he recited after His saying: 'Have you considered al-Lat and al-Uzza and Manat, the third, the other?' (7) 'Those are the high cranes, and their intercession is to be hoped for.' Then when Gabriel informed him of what came from it, he became sorrowful and feared intensely, so the verse was revealed: 'And We did not send before you any messenger or prophet except that when he wished, Satan cast into his wish' (8) as a consolation for him. The answer is: it was from Satan's casting, not intentional on his part. And it is said: rather, the 'gharaniq' are the angels. And this was Qur'an that was abrogated. And it is said: the meaning of 'the prophet wished' is self-talk. And Satan would whisper to him other than guidance, so Allah abrogates his whisperings from his soul and guides him to correctness.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial incident known as the 'غرانيق' (satanic verses) story. The author recounts the narrative: the Prophet, while reciting Surah An-Najm, supposedly uttered praise of the pagan goddesses. The author immediately provides multiple answers to defend the Prophet from any accusation of intentional sin. First, the Prophet became distressed when Gabriel informed him, and the verse from Surah Al-Hajj (22:52) was revealed as consolation, explaining that such whispered interpolations happen to prophets. The answer (فالجواب) is that it was from Satan's casting (إلقاء الشيطان), not intentional. Second, an alternative interpretation is that the word 'غرانيق' actually refers to angels, not goddesses, so there was no praise of idols. Third, it is said that this was previously Qur'an that was later abrogated (meaning it was valid revelation originally, then abrogated). Fourth, another interpretation is that 'تمنى' means the Prophet's inner thought, and Satan would whisper to him, but Allah removed those whisperings. Each explanation preserves the Prophet's infallibility by denying that he deliberately uttered falsehood.

Key Takeaway
The gharaniq incident, despite controversial reports, does not compromise the Prophet's infallibility, as multiple scholarly explanations show it was either Satan's casting, allegorical, abrogated, or internal whisper.

?

Understanding Apparent Admonitions in the Quran
Author explains verses of apparent admonition and answers with logical principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why verses like 'O Prophet, fear Allah' do not imply that the Prophet committed a sin, and how commands, prohibitions, and conditions in the Quran do not necessitate prior action or omission.

Core Idea
Commands and prohibitions do not imply prior sin.

The Quran contains verses that, on the surface, seem to admonish or even scold the Prophet Muhammad. How can this be reconciled with the doctrine of prophetic infallibility? In this passage, the author explains that such verses are not evidence of sin, but rather serve other purposes. He provides a clear logical answer: a command does not mean the commanded thing was previously neglected, and a prohibition does not mean the forbidden act was previously done.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the Zainab verse
Start with the author's explanation of the verse about concealing the marriage to Zainab (Surah Al-Ahzab 33:37). Notice how he classifies this as a 'lapse' (زلة) and 'leaving the better' (ترك الأولى), not a sin.
This sets the stage for understanding the category of 'ترك الأولى'.
2
Identify the other apparent admonitions
The author lists several other verses: 'O Prophet, fear Allah', 'Do not drive away', 'Do not be among the doubters', 'If you associate...', 'If you are in doubt...'. Read each one and note how they might appear to imply prior sin.
These are classic proof texts for those who argue against infallibility.
3
Understand the author's general answer
The author states a general principle: commands do not necessitate prior omission, prohibitions do not necessitate prior commission, and conditional statements do not imply the condition will occur. This is a logical refutation of the claim that these verses prove sin.
This principle is key to defending prophetic infallibility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the permissibility of minor sins for prophets in matters of ijtihad is not decisively proven. This acknowledges scholarly difference while maintaining the general position of infallibility.
The author does not take a strong stance here, leaving room for ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وتخفي في نفسك ما الله مبديه وتخشى الناس والله أحق أن تخشاه (۱). عتاب على أنه أخفى في نفسه عزيمة تزوج زينب عند تطليق زيد إياها خوفا من طعن المنافقين، ولا خفاء في أن إخفاء أمر دنيوي خوفاً من طعن أعداء الدين ليس من الصغائر، فضلاً عن الكبائر، بل غايته زلة وترك الأولى. وكذا ميلان القلب لزينب، وأما مثل قوله : يا أيها النبي اتق الله (۲) ولا تطرد الذين يدعون ربهم (۳) فلا تكونن من الممترين (٤) ولئن أشركت ليحبطن عملك (٥) فإن كنت في شك مما أنزلنا إليك فاسأل الذين يقرأون الكتاب (٦) . والجواب : أن الأمر لا يقتضي سابقة تركه ولا النهي سابقة فعله، ولا الشرط وقوع مضمونه، وبالجملة فمسألة جواز الصغيرة عمداً على الأنبياء في معرص الاجتهاد لا قاطع فيها، لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً.

فإن قيل : ما بال زلة الأنبياء حكيت بحيث تقرأ بأعلى الصوت على وجه الزمان ، مع أن الله غفار ،ستار، وقد أمرنا بالستر على من ارتكب ذنباً؟ قلنا ليدل على صدق الأنبياء، وكون ما يبلغون الشيء بأمر من الله من غير إخفاء لشيء، أو ليكون امتحاناً للأمم كيف يفعلون بأنبيائهم بعد الاطلاع على زلالتهم، وليعلموا أن الأنبياء مع جلالة قدرهم وكثرة طاعاتهم كيف التجأوا إلى التضرع والاستغفار في أدنى زلة، وأن الصغيرة ليست مما يقدح في الولاية والإيمان البتة، أو تقع مكفرة لا محالة، بحيث لا عتاب عليها ولا عقاب .
And His saying: 'And you concealed within yourself what Allah would disclose, and you feared the people while Allah has more right that you fear Him' (1). [It is] a reproach for concealing in himself the determination to marry Zainab when Zaid divorced her, out of fear of the hypocrites' criticism. And it is no secret that concealing a worldly matter out of fear of the enemies of religion is not among minor sins, let alone major sins, but its utmost is a lapse and leaving the better. Similarly, the inclination of the heart towards Zainab. And as for verses like His saying: 'O Prophet, fear Allah' (2), and 'Do not drive away those who call upon their Lord' (3), 'Do not be among the doubters' (4), 'If you associate [others with Allah], your work would surely become worthless' (5), 'If you are in doubt about what We have revealed to you, then ask those who read the Book' (6). The answer: that a command does not necessitate a prior omission, nor does a prohibition necessitate a prior commission, nor does a condition entail the occurrence of its content. In summary, the issue of the permissibility of minor sins intentionally for prophets in the arena of ijtihad has no decisive evidence, neither for negation nor affirmation.

If it is said: Why are the lapses of the prophets narrated such that they are recited loudly throughout time, while Allah is Forgiving, Concealing, and we have been commanded to conceal the one who commits a sin? We say: to indicate the truthfulness of the prophets, and that what they convey is the thing by the command of Allah without concealing anything, or to be a test for the nations as to how they treat their prophets after learning of their lapses, and so that they know how the prophets, despite their great status and abundant obedience, resorted to supplication and seeking forgiveness for the slightest lapse, and that minor sins do not at all impair sainthood and faith, or that they occur inevitably expiated such that there is no reproach or punishment for them.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage continues the defense of prophetic infallibility by explaining several more Quranic verses and then addressing a potential objection. The author first explains the verse about concealing the marriage to Zainab (Surah Al-Ahzab 33:37) as a reproach (عتاب) for leaving the better, noting that hiding a worldly matter out of fear of enemies is not even a minor sin, but a 'lapse' (زلة). Then he lists several other verses that appear to command or forbid the Prophet in a way that might imply prior sin: 'O Prophet, fear Allah' (Al-Ahzab 33:1), 'Do not drive away' (Al-An'am 6:52), 'Do not be among the doubters' (Al-Baqarah 2:147), 'If you associate...' (Az-Zumar 39:65), and 'If you are in doubt...' (Yunus 10:94). The author gives a general answer (والجواب): commands do not imply that the commanded thing was previously abandoned, and prohibitions do not imply that the prohibited act was previously done, and conditional statements (like 'if you associate') do not imply that the condition will actually occur. Therefore, these verses do not prove that the Prophet committed any sin. The author then concludes that the permissibility of minor sins intentionally for prophets is a matter of ijtihad without decisive evidence.

Then a clear objection is raised: why are the lapses of prophets narrated publicly in the Qur'an, given that Allah is Concealing and commands concealment of sins? The answer provides multiple purposes: (1) to prove the truthfulness of the prophets, that they convey everything by God's command without hiding anything, (2) to test the nations how they treat their prophets after knowing their lapses, (3) to teach people that even the greatest prophets turn to repentance for the slightest lapse, and (4) to show that minor sins do not impair sainthood and faith, and that they may be inevitably expiated so that no reproach or punishment remains.

Key Terms
عتاب
زلة
ترك الأولى
صغيرة
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Quranic verses like 'O Prophet, fear Allah' prove that the Prophet committed sins.
Correction: Commands and prohibitions do not necessitate prior neglect or commission. The author shows that these verses are not evidence of sin but serve other rhetorical or legal purposes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the maximum degree of the reproach (عتاب) in the verse about Zainab?
A) A minor sin (صغيرة)
B) A major sin (كبيرة)
C) A lapse and leaving the better (زلة وترك الأولى)
D) A clear sin (ذنب)

Key Takeaway
Apparent Quranic admonitions of prophets are not evidence of sin; they can be explained as lapses or are subject to logical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'O Prophet, fear Allah,' do you ever feel a sense of superiority or judgment toward the Prophet? How does this lesson's explanation challenge that feeling, and what does it reveal about your own heart's assumptions?
?
The author says concealing a matter out of fear of people is at most a lapse, not a sin. Think of a time you hid the truth because you feared someone's opinion. Was that just a 'lapse' for you, or did it reflect a deeper compromise in your faith? What would true trust in Allah have looked like in that moment?
?
You've learned that a conditional warning (like 'If you associate...') does not imply the condition will happen. Are there verses of threat in the Quran that you instinctively apply more to yourself than to the Prophet? How does that affect your relationship with Allah? Will you now read such verses with trust rather than fear?

The Wisdom Behind Narrating Prophetic Lapses
Author answers why prophetic lapses are publicly narrated in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple wisdoms behind why the Quran narrates the lapses of prophets, and how this does not contradict Allah's attribute of concealment.

Core Idea
Prophetic lapses are narrated to prove truthfulness and teach repentance.

If Allah is Concealing and forgives sins, why does the Quran publicly narrate the mistakes of prophets? This is a powerful objection that the author anticipates and answers. In this passage, he provides several profound wisdoms, including demonstrating the prophets' complete honesty in conveying revelation and teaching believers that even the greatest prophets repent for the slightest lapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states: since Allah is Forgiving and Concealing, and we are commanded to conceal people's sins, why are the lapses of prophets narrated publicly forever? This is a critical question that challenges the wisdom of the Quran.
This objection is strong and requires a nuanced answer.
2
Identify the first purpose: truthfulness
The author answers that narrating lapses proves the prophets are truthful, that they convey everything from Allah without hiding anything. Their honesty is so complete that they even report their own lapses.
This emphasizes the integrity of prophetic transmission.
3
Identify the second purpose: test for nations
Another purpose is to test how the nations treat their prophets after learning of their lapses. Will they reject them or follow them despite their human imperfections?
This tests people's faith and loyalty.
4
Identify the third purpose: teaching repentance and the nature of minor sins
The final three purposes are: to show how prophets turn to Allah in repentance for even minor lapses, to teach that minor sins do not affect sainthood or faith, and to show that such lapses are expiated without reproach.
These purposes have pedagogical and theological benefits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وتخفي في نفسك ما الله مبديه وتخشى الناس والله أحق أن تخشاه (۱). عتاب على أنه أخفى في نفسه عزيمة تزوج زينب عند تطليق زيد إياها خوفا من طعن المنافقين، ولا خفاء في أن إخفاء أمر دنيوي خوفاً من طعن أعداء الدين ليس من الصغائر، فضلاً عن الكبائر، بل غايته زلة وترك الأولى. وكذا ميلان القلب لزينب، وأما مثل قوله : يا أيها النبي اتق الله (۲) ولا تطرد الذين يدعون ربهم (۳) فلا تكونن من الممترين (٤) ولئن أشركت ليحبطن عملك (٥) فإن كنت في شك مما أنزلنا إليك فاسأل الذين يقرأون الكتاب (٦) . والجواب : أن الأمر لا يقتضي سابقة تركه ولا النهي سابقة فعله، ولا الشرط وقوع مضمونه، وبالجملة فمسألة جواز الصغيرة عمداً على الأنبياء في معرص الاجتهاد لا قاطع فيها، لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً.

فإن قيل : ما بال زلة الأنبياء حكيت بحيث تقرأ بأعلى الصوت على وجه الزمان ، مع أن الله غفار ،ستار، وقد أمرنا بالستر على من ارتكب ذنباً؟ قلنا ليدل على صدق الأنبياء، وكون ما يبلغون الشيء بأمر من الله من غير إخفاء لشيء، أو ليكون امتحاناً للأمم كيف يفعلون بأنبيائهم بعد الاطلاع على زلالتهم، وليعلموا أن الأنبياء مع جلالة قدرهم وكثرة طاعاتهم كيف التجأوا إلى التضرع والاستغفار في أدنى زلة، وأن الصغيرة ليست مما يقدح في الولاية والإيمان البتة، أو تقع مكفرة لا محالة، بحيث لا عتاب عليها ولا عقاب .
And His saying: 'And you concealed within yourself what Allah would disclose, and you feared the people while Allah has more right that you fear Him' (1). [It is] a reproach for concealing in himself the determination to marry Zainab when Zaid divorced her, out of fear of the hypocrites' criticism. And it is no secret that concealing a worldly matter out of fear of the enemies of religion is not among minor sins, let alone major sins, but its utmost is a lapse and leaving the better. Similarly, the inclination of the heart towards Zainab. And as for verses like His saying: 'O Prophet, fear Allah' (2), and 'Do not drive away those who call upon their Lord' (3), 'Do not be among the doubters' (4), 'If you associate [others with Allah], your work would surely become worthless' (5), 'If you are in doubt about what We have revealed to you, then ask those who read the Book' (6). The answer: that a command does not necessitate a prior omission, nor does a prohibition necessitate a prior commission, nor does a condition entail the occurrence of its content. In summary, the issue of the permissibility of minor sins intentionally for prophets in the arena of ijtihad has no decisive evidence, neither for negation nor affirmation.

If it is said: Why are the lapses of the prophets narrated such that they are recited loudly throughout time, while Allah is Forgiving, Concealing, and we have been commanded to conceal the one who commits a sin? We say: to indicate the truthfulness of the prophets, and that what they convey is the thing by the command of Allah without concealing anything, or to be a test for the nations as to how they treat their prophets after learning of their lapses, and so that they know how the prophets, despite their great status and abundant obedience, resorted to supplication and seeking forgiveness for the slightest lapse, and that minor sins do not at all impair sainthood and faith, or that they occur inevitably expiated such that there is no reproach or punishment for them.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage continues the defense of prophetic infallibility by explaining several more Quranic verses and then addressing a potential objection. The author first explains the verse about concealing the marriage to Zainab (Surah Al-Ahzab 33:37) as a reproach (عتاب) for leaving the better, noting that hiding a worldly matter out of fear of enemies is not even a minor sin, but a 'lapse' (زلة). Then he lists several other verses that appear to command or forbid the Prophet in a way that might imply prior sin: 'O Prophet, fear Allah' (Al-Ahzab 33:1), 'Do not drive away' (Al-An'am 6:52), 'Do not be among the doubters' (Al-Baqarah 2:147), 'If you associate...' (Az-Zumar 39:65), and 'If you are in doubt...' (Yunus 10:94). The author gives a general answer (والجواب): commands do not imply that the commanded thing was previously abandoned, and prohibitions do not imply that the prohibited act was previously done, and conditional statements (like 'if you associate') do not imply that the condition will actually occur. Therefore, these verses do not prove that the Prophet committed any sin. The author then concludes that the permissibility of minor sins intentionally for prophets is a matter of ijtihad without decisive evidence.

Then a clear objection is raised: why are the lapses of prophets narrated publicly in the Qur'an, given that Allah is Concealing and commands concealment of sins? The answer provides multiple purposes: (1) to prove the truthfulness of the prophets, that they convey everything by God's command without hiding anything, (2) to test the nations how they treat their prophets after knowing their lapses, (3) to teach people that even the greatest prophets turn to repentance for the slightest lapse, and (4) to show that minor sins do not impair sainthood and faith, and that they may be inevitably expiated so that no reproach or punishment remains.

Key Terms
زلة
الستر
الصدق
تضرع
استغفار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that publicizing prophets' lapses contradicts Allah's attribute of concealment and is unnecessary.
Correction: The narration serves multiple wisdoms: proving truthfulness, testing nations, teaching repentance, and clarifying that minor sins do not affect faith. It does not contradict Allah's concealment because these lapses are not sins that harm their status.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one purpose of narrating prophetic lapses?
A) To show that prophets are sinful and need punishment.
B) To indicate the truthfulness of the prophets in conveying revelation.
C) To encourage people to commit minor sins.
D) To embarrass the prophets and lower their status.

Key Takeaway
The Quran narrates prophetic lapses for profound wisdoms, including proving their truthfulness, testing nations, and teaching repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about a prophet's lapse, does it humble you to see their immediate turn to Allah, or do you feel a sense of superiority? Which reaction truly reflects the test described in this lesson?
?
If you were one of the companions who heard a prophetic lapse narrated, how would you treat that prophet afterward? Would you still follow him wholeheartedly, or would you begin to doubt? What does your answer say about your faith?
?
The author says even the slightest lapse is met with supplication and seeking forgiveness from prophets. When you commit a minor sin, do you rush to Allah with the same urgency? Or do you take it lightly, assuming it's 'expiated anyway'? How does this lesson confront that assumption?

Commands and Prohibitions Do Not Imply Sin
Author defends prophets against Quranic reproaches using logical principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic commands and prohibitions addressed to prophets do not necessarily indicate that they committed a sin.

Core Idea
A command does not prove prior omission, nor does a prohibition prove prior commission.

This lesson examines the first part of the discussion on the permissibility of minor sins for prophets. The author cites several Quranic verses that seem to reproach or command the Prophet Muhammad. He explains that these do not imply actual sin, because a command does not require that the person had previously neglected the command, and a prohibition does not require that they had previously done the forbidden act. This is a key logical distinction in defending prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author begins by citing a verse about the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab and then lists other verses. Pay attention to how he distinguishes between a slip (zalla) and a minor sin.
The Arabic phrase 'ترك الأولى' means 'abandonment of the better' – not a sin.
2
Identify the author's main argument
The author's central claim is that commands and prohibitions do not logically imply prior sin. Find the sentence where he states: 'the command does not necessitate prior abandonment'. Understand why this is important for defending prophets.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the permissibility of minor sins for prophets is a matter of ijtihad with no decisive proof. Reflect on why he leaves the question open-ended.
This shows scholarly humility and the limits of rational proof in this area.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وتخفي في نفسك ما الله مبديه وتخشى الناس والله أحق أن تخشاه .
عتاب على أنه أخفى في نفسه عزيمة تزوج زينب عند تطليق زيد إياها خوفا من طعن المنافقين، ولا خفاء في أن إخفاء أمر دنيوي خوفاً من طعن أعداء الدين ليس من الصغائر، فضلاً عن الكبائر، بل غايته زلة وترك الأولى.
وكذا ميلان القلب لزينب، وأما مثل قوله : يا أيها النبي اتق الله ولا تطرد الذين يدعون ربهم فلا تكونن من الممترين ولئن أشركت ليحبطن عملك فإن كنت في شك مما أنزلنا إليك فاسأل الذين يقرأون الكتاب .
والجواب : أن الأمر لا يقتضي سابقة تركه ولا النهي سابقة فعله، ولا الشرط وقوع مضمونه، وبالجملة فمسألة جواز الصغيرة عمداً على الأنبياء في معرص الاجتهاد لا قاطع فيها، لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً.
فإن قيل : ما بال زلة الأنبياء حكيت بحيث تقرأ بأعلى الصوت على وجه الزمان ، مع أن الله غفار ،ستار، وقد أمرنا بالستر على من ارتكب ذنباً؟
قلنا ليدل على صدق الأنبياء، وكون ما يبلغون السىء بأمر من الله من غير إخفاء لشيء، أو ليكون امتحاناً للأمم كيف يفعلون بأنبيائهم بعد الاطلاع على زلالتهم، وليعلموا أن الأنبياء مع جلالة قدرهم وكثرة طاعاتهم كيف التجأوا إلى التضرع والاستغفار في أدنى زلة، وأن الصغيرة ليست مما يقدح في الولاية والإيمان البتة، أو تقع مكفرة لا محالة، بحيث لا عتاب عليها ولا عقاب .
And His saying: 'And you conceal within yourself what Allah would expose, and you fear the people, while Allah is more worthy that you fear Him.'
Rebuke that he concealed in himself the intention to marry Zaynab when Zayd divorced her, fearing the slander of the hypocrites. There is no concealment that hiding a worldly matter out of fear of the slander of the enemies of religion is not among minor sins, let alone major sins, but rather its utmost is a slip and abandonment of the better.
And likewise the inclination of the heart toward Zaynab. As for such as His saying: 'O Prophet, fear Allah' and 'Do not drive away those who call upon their Lord' and 'Do not be among the doubters' and 'If you associate [others with Allah], your work would surely become worthless' and 'If you are in doubt about what We have revealed to you, ask those who recite the Scripture.'
And the answer: That the command does not necessitate prior abandonment, nor does the prohibition necessitate prior commission, nor does the conditional clause necessitate the occurrence of its content. In summary, the issue of the permissibility of minor sins intentionally for prophets in the arena of ijtihad has no decisive proof, neither for negation nor affirmation.
For it is said: What is the matter that the slips of the prophets are narrated such that they are recited aloud throughout time, while Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Concealing, and we are commanded to conceal [the faults] of one who commits a sin?
We say: To indicate the truthfulness of the prophets, and that what they convey is by the command of Allah without concealing anything, or to be a trial for the nations as to how they act towards their prophets after learning of their slips, and that they may know that the prophets, despite their great status and abundant obedience, how they resorted to supplication and seeking forgiveness for the slightest slip, and that the minor sin does not detract from sainthood (wilāya) and faith at all, or it occurs expiated necessarily, such that there is no reproach nor punishment for it.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the view that prophets may commit minor slips (zallat) but not major sins. The author begins by citing the Quranic verse about the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab, which is presented as a reproach (ʿitāb). He explains that this reproach is not for a sin but for a 'slip and leaving the better' (zalla wa tark al-ūlā). Then he addresses several verses that seem to command or prohibit the Prophet (e.g., 'fear Allah', 'do not drive away', 'if you associate...'). The author's answer: a command does not imply that the commanded person had previously omitted the action, a prohibition does not imply prior commission, and a conditional statement does not imply that the condition actually occurred. Thus these verses do not prove actual sin. He concludes that the permissibility of minor sins for prophets before or after prophethood is a matter of ijtihad with no decisive textual proof either way.
Then comes an explicit objection: why are the slips of prophets publicly recited throughout time, given that Allah conceals and commands concealment? The answer provides multiple wisdoms: to affirm the truthfulness of prophets (they hide nothing), to test the nations, to teach humility and repentance, and to show that minor sins do not harm sainthood and faith and are expiated without reproach or punishment.

Key Terms
زلة (zalla)
صغيرة (ṣaghīra)
عتاب (ʿitāb)
الأمر لا يقتضي (al-amr lā yaqtaḍī)
ترك الأولى (tark al-ūlā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does a command (amr) not necessitate?
A) The commanded action is obligatory.
B) Prior abandonment of the commanded act.
C) That the person is a prophet.
D) That the command is from Allah.

Key Takeaway
Quranic commands and prohibitions addressed to prophets do not imply that the prophets had previously failed to obey them; thus they do not prove sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Prophet once feared people's criticism more than Allah's pleasure, what fear of people's opinions is currently holding you back from an act of obedience you know you should do? Will you confront that fear today?
?
Have you ever dismissed a 'small' slip in your own life as 'just leaving the better' when it was actually a disobedience? How can you develop the honesty to call it what it is?
?
The author says commands do not imply prior neglect. When your parents, spouse, or leader tells you to do something, do you immediately feel accused? How can you instead receive it as guidance without defensiveness?

Why Are the Slips of Prophets Publicized?
Author answers objection about publicizing prophetic slips with multiple wisdoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the wisdoms behind the public recitation of prophetic slips, as explained by the author in response to an objection.

Core Idea
Publicizing slips affirms truthfulness, tests nations, and teaches humility.

In this lesson, we examine an explicit objection raised against the view that prophets can commit minor sins: if Allah is concealing and commands concealment, why are the slips of prophets recited publicly throughout time? The author offers several wisdoms, including affirming the prophets' truthfulness, testing other nations, and demonstrating the importance of repentance. This shows that such narrations have beneficial purposes and do not detract from the prophets' status.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'فإن قيل' to the end. Notice the objection: why publicize slips if Allah conceals sins? Then read the answer, which lists multiple wisdoms.
The word 'السىء' in the Arabic may be a corruption; it likely means 'that which' or a similar word.
2
Identify the wisdoms
The author gives several reasons. List them: (1) to show the prophets' truthfulness and that they hide nothing, (2) as a trial for nations, (3) to teach humility and repentance, (4) to show that minor sins do not harm sainthood, (5) that such slips are expiated without reproach.
The reasons are connected by 'أو' (or) but are cumulative.
3
Reflect on the doctrinal implications
Consider how these wisdoms protect the concept of prophethood: publicizing slips does not reduce respect but rather increases it by showing the prophets' submission to Allah.
This answers the objection that publicizing would be shameful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وتخفي في نفسك ما الله مبديه وتخشى الناس والله أحق أن تخشاه .
عتاب على أنه أخفى في نفسه عزيمة تزوج زينب عند تطليق زيد إياها خوفا من طعن المنافقين، ولا خفاء في أن إخفاء أمر دنيوي خوفاً من طعن أعداء الدين ليس من الصغائر، فضلاً عن الكبائر، بل غايته زلة وترك الأولى.

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the view that prophets may commit minor slips (zallat) but not major sins. The author begins by citing the Quranic verse about the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab, which is presented as a reproach (ʿitāb). He explains that this reproach is not for a sin but for a 'slip and leaving the better' (zalla wa tark al-ūlā). Then he addresses several verses that seem to command or prohibit the Prophet (e.g., 'fear Allah', 'do not drive away', 'if you associate...'). The author's answer: a command does not imply that the commanded person had previously omitted the action, a prohibition does not imply prior commission, and a conditional statement does not imply that the condition actually occurred. He concludes that the permissibility of minor sins for prophets before or after prophethood is a matter of ijtihad with no decisive textual proof either way.

The answer provides multiple wisdoms: to affirm the truthfulness of prophets (they hide nothing), to test the nations, to teach humility and repentance, and to show that minor sins do not harm sainthood and faith and are expiated without reproach or punishment.

Misconception: Some think that publicizing prophetic slips contradicts Allah's attribute of concealment and shows disrespect to prophets.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one wisdom behind publicizing the slips of prophets?
A) To show that prophets are infallible in all matters.
B) To indicate the truthfulness of the prophets.
C) To encourage people to sin like prophets.
D) To prove that prophets committed major sins.

Key Takeaway
The public recitation of prophetic slips serves multiple beneficial purposes, including affirming truthfulness, testing nations, and teaching humility, without detracting from their status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a mistake of someone you respect, do you immediately judge them or do you pause to reflect on your own faults? This lesson shows that even prophets' slips are publicized to humble others. How can you change your reaction the next time you hear of someone's fault?
?
The passage teaches that prophets immediately turned to supplication and seeking forgiveness for the slightest slip. Think of a recent minor sin you committed. Did you rush to seek Allah's forgiveness with the same urgency? What holds you back from emulating their humility?
?
Allah reveals prophets' slips not to diminish them but to affirm their truthfulness and to test how nations respond. How do you respond when you learn of the shortcomings of religious figures? Are you quick to criticize or do you see it as a lesson in humility and a reminder of Allah's mercy?

Prophethood

Conditions of Prophethood
The author lists conditions required for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key conditions that must be present in a prophet according to Islamic theology, particularly the emphasis on moral and social perfection.

Core Idea
Prophethood requires not only spiritual but also moral and social perfection.

This passage from the Sharh al-Maqasid outlines the conditions (shurūṭ) that a prophet must fulfill. These conditions ensure that the prophet is free from any repulsive qualities that could hinder the acceptance of the divine message. The author lists both personal and lineage-based criteria.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without deep analysis. Then read the translation to get the full list of conditions.
Note that the passage has two parts: a concise list and an expanded explanation.
2
Identify each condition
List the conditions mentioned: masculinity, perfection of intellect, strength of opinion, and freedom from repulsive things. Also note the specific examples given.
Examples include leprosy, cupping, and ancestral shame.
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why each condition is necessary. For instance, why must a prophet be free from base occupations? How does this preserve the mission?
The wisdom (ḥikma) is to ensure the prophet is respected and the message accepted.
4
Apply to the concept of prophethood
Consider how these conditions differentiate prophets from ordinary people and why they are essential for the prophetic role.

قال : خاتمة
خاتمة ـ النبوة مشروطة بالذكورة، وكمال العقل، وقوة الرأي، والسلامة عن المنفرات كزنا الآباء، وعهر الأمهات والفظاظة، ومثل البرص، والجذام، والحرف الدنيئة، وكل ما يخل بالمروءة وحكمة البعثة، ونحو ذلك .
من شروط النبوة الذكورة، وكمال العقل والذكاء والفطنة، وقوة الرأي، ولو في الصبا كعيسى ويحيى ( عليهما السلام والسلامة عن كل ما ينفر عنه كزنا الآباء، وعهر الأمهات، والغلظة والفظاظة، والعيوب المنفرة كالبرص، والجذام، ونحو ذلك، والأمور المخلة بالمروءة كالأكل على الطريق، والحرف الدنيئة كالحجامة، وكل ما يخل بحكم البعثة من آداء الشرائع، وقبول الأمة.
He said: Conclusion
Conclusion — Prophethood is conditioned by masculinity, perfection of intellect, strength of opinion, and immunity from repulsive things such as the fornication of fathers, the promiscuity of mothers, harshness and roughness, and like leprosy, elephantiasis, and base occupations, and everything that contravenes chivalry (murū'a) and the wisdom of the mission, and the like.
Among the conditions of prophethood are masculinity, perfection of intellect, intelligence and acumen, strength of opinion, even in childhood like Jesus and John (peace be upon them), and immunity from everything repulsive such as fornication of fathers, promiscuity of mothers, harshness and roughness, and repulsive defects like leprosy, elephantiasis and the like, and matters that contravene chivalry such as eating on the road, and base occupations like cupping, and everything that contravenes the purpose of the mission of conveying the laws and acceptance by the community.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the conditions (shurūṭ) required for prophethood. The author first gives a concise list: masculinity (dhukūra), perfect intellect (kamāl al-ʿaql), strength of opinion (qūwat al-raʾy), and freedom from repulsive attributes (al-salāma ʿan al-munaffirāt) including ancestral shame (e.g., fornication of fathers, promiscuity of mothers), personal defects (harshness, leprosy, elephantiasis), base occupations (ḥiraf danī'a), and anything that violates murū'a (social dignity) and the wisdom (ḥikma) of the mission. Then he expands in more detail, adding 'intelligence and acumen' (al-dhakā' wa al-fiṭna), noting that these conditions apply even in childhood (e.g., Jesus and John). He also gives examples of base occupations like cupping (ḥijāma). The purpose is to ensure that the prophet is free from anything that would repel people from accepting the message, thus preserving the purpose of the mission (ḥukm al-biʿtha) which is to convey the laws and gain the community's acceptance.

ذكورة (dhukūra)
مروءة (murūʾa)
المنفرات (al-munaffirāt)
البرص (al-baraṣ)
الجذام (al-judhām)
الحرف الدنيئة (al-ḥiraf al-danī'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think prophethood can occur with repulsive defects or base lineage.
Correction: The author shows these are excluded to maintain respect and effectiveness.

Checkpoint

B) Perfection of intellect

Key Takeaway
The conditions of prophethood ensure that the prophet is a perfect example, free from any physical, moral, or social defects that could repel people from the message.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author states that a prophet must be free from anything repulsive, including physical defects and base lineage. Prophethood requires 'strength of opinion' and 'perfection of intellect,' as seen in even young prophets like Jesus and John. How can you seek to strengthen your understanding and conviction to be a more effective bearer of the message?

Why Specifying the Number of Prophets is Problematic
The author reports the hadith on number of prophets and cautions against specifying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic scholars caution against specifying the exact number of prophets and messengers, based on the limits of solitary hadith and Quranic principles.

Core Idea
Only Allah knows the full count of prophets; solitary reports cannot establish certainty in matters of belief.

This lesson examines a well-known hadith reporting the number of prophets and messengers. While the report is authentic, scholars advise against treating these numbers as definitive. They argue that such specification may lead to theological errors and contradicts the Quran's statement that many prophets are not mentioned. Let's delve into the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and author's citation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the numbers and the source (Abu Dharr al-Ghifari).
2
Understand the precaution
The author says it is preferable to refrain from specifying. Ask yourself why he says this despite the hadith being well-known.
The key term here is 'al-tanṣīṣ' (specification).
3
Identify the three main reasons
Look for reasons: (1) risk of false affirmation/negation, (2) contradiction with Quran 40:78, (3) epistemic insufficiency of khabar al-wāḥid.
These reasons are explicitly given in the passage.
4
Reflect on epistemic distinctions
Note the distinction between ẓann (probability) and certainty, and between ʿamaliyyāt (practical matters) and iʿtiqādāt (beliefs).
This is a core principle in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وقد ورد في الحديث أن عدد الأنبياء مائة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألفاً، وعدد الرسل ثلثمائة وثلاثة عشر ، لكن الأولى ترك التنصيص لأنه ربما يفضي إلى إثبات النبوة حيث ليس ونفيها حيث أيس، ويخالف ظاهر قوله تعالى منهم من قصصنا عليك ومنهم من لم نقصص عليك ) .

He said: And it has been reported

Meaning: It has been mentioned in some hadiths the clarification of the number of prophets and messengers according to what has been narrated from Abu Dharr al-Ghifari that he said: I said to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): How many prophets? He said: One hundred twenty-four thousand. I said: And how many messengers? He said: Three hundred thirteen, a great multitude. But some scholars mentioned that it is preferable that one should not specify their number because the solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid) even if it meets all conditions does not yield certainty (ẓann), and it is only considered in practical matters (ʿamaliyyāt) not in beliefs (iʿtiqādāt). And here, specifying their number contradicts the apparent meaning of His saying: 'Among them are those We have related to you and among them are those We have not related to you' and it also possibly contradicts reality, and affirming prophethood for one who is not a prophet if their number is...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of the reported numbers of prophets and messengers from a hadith. The author first states the hadith (attributed to Abu Dharr) giving numbers: 124,000 prophets and 313 messengers. However, he immediately cautions that it is better (al-ūlā) not to specify a precise number because doing so may lead to falsely affirming prophethood for someone who is not a prophet or denying it for someone who is a prophet (since we cannot be certain from a solitary report). Additionally, specifying a number contradicts the apparent meaning of Quran 40:78, which says that some prophets are mentioned and some are not (implying the total is known only to Allah). The commentary explains that solitary reports (khabar al-wāḥid) produce only probability (ẓann), which is sufficient for practical matters (ʿamaliyyāt) but not for matters of belief (iʿtiqādāt). Specifying a number could also contradict reality and lead to error. The passage ends mid-sentence, probably continuing on the next page.

عمليات (ʿamaliyyāt)
اعتقادات (iʿtiqādāt)
التنصيص (al-tanṣīṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some assume that a solitary hadith about numbers is definitive in matters of belief.
Correction: The author clarifies that khabar al-wāḥid yields only probability (ẓann) and should not be used for assertion in beliefs.

Key Takeaway
The number of prophets is known only to Allah; treating a solitary hadith as definitive on this matter can lead to error.

The Wisdom of Not Specifying the Number of Prophets
The author explains why it is better not to fix the number of prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical scholars preferred not to specify the exact number of prophets, based on the authority of khabar al-wahid and Quranic principles.

Core Idea
Fixing the number of prophets risks error and contradicts the Quran's silence on the matter.

This passage explores a subtle methodological issue: whether we should accept a well-known hadith giving exact numbers of prophets. The author argues that because the hadith is a single-narrator report (khabar al-wahid), it does not provide certainty and could conflict with Quranic indications, so it is better to leave the number unspecified.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith with numbers
Begin by reading the reported numbers of prophets (124,000) and messengers (313) from Abu Dharr's narration. Notice that the author presents this as a known report.
The numbers are large but not the main point.
2
Understand the nature of khabar al-wahid
Learn that khabar al-wahid yields only probability (zann), not certainty, and is only authoritative in practical matters (amaliyyat), not in core beliefs (i'tiqadat).
This is a key principle in Islamic jurisprudence.
3
Recognize the Quranic conflict
The Quran (40:78) states that some prophets are mentioned and some are not. Specifying a number would contradict this apparent meaning by implying a limit.
4
See the risk of error
If the actual number is different from the hadith, one might wrongly affirm prophethood for a non-prophet or deny it for a true prophet. This risk is unacceptable in matters of belief.
5
Conclude with the preferred approach
Thus, the better approach is to leave the number unspecified, avoiding potential error and adhering to the Quran's guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وقد ورد (وقد ورد في الحديث أن عدد الأنبياء مائة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألفاً، وعدد الرسل ثلثمائة وثلاثة عشر ، لكن الأولى ترك التنصيص لأنه ربما يفضي إلى إثبات النبوة حيث ليس ونفيها حيث أيس، ويخالف ظاهر قوله تعالى منهم من قصصنا عليك ومنهم من لم نقصص عليك ) (۱) . يعني قد ذكر في بعض الأحاديث بيان عدد الأنبياء والرسل على ما روي عن أبي ذر الغفاري أنه قال : قلت لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كم الأنبياء؟ فقال : مائة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألفاً . فقلت : وكم الرسل ؟ فقال ثلاثمائة وثلاثة عشر جماً غفيراً. لكن ذكر بعض العلماء أن الأولى أن لا يقتصر عددهم الواحد على تقدير اشتماله على جميع الشرائط لا يفيد إلا الظن، ولا يعتبر إلا في العمليات دون الاعتقادات وههنا حصر عددهم يخالف ظاهر قوله تعالى : ومنهم من قصصنا عليك ومنهم من لم نقصص) (۱) . لأن خبر الواحد لا يفيد إلا الظن، ولا يعتبر إلا في العمليات دون الاعتقادات، وههنا حصر عددهم يخالف ظاهر قوله تعالى : ومنهم من قصصنا عليك ومنهم من لم نقصص ويحتمل أيضاً مخالفة الواقع ، وإثبات نبوة من ليس بنبى إن كان عددهم في الواقع أقل مما ذكر. ونفي النبوة عمن هو نبي إن كان أكثر. فالأولى عدم التنصيص على عدد.
He said: 'And it has been reported.' (And it has been reported in the hadith that the number of prophets is one hundred and twenty-four thousand, and the number of messengers is three hundred and thirteen, but it is better to leave specification because perhaps it leads to affirming prophethood where it is not and denying it where it is realized, and it contradicts the apparent meaning of His statement: 'Of them are those We have related to you and of them are those We have not related to you' (40:78). That is, it has been mentioned in some hadiths the specification of the number of prophets and messengers according to what was narrated from Abu Dharr al-Ghifari that he said: I said to the Messenger of God, peace be upon him: How many prophets? He said: One hundred and twenty-four thousand. I said: And how many messengers? He said: Three hundred and thirteen, a large group. But some scholars said that it is better not to specify their number, because the report of a single narrator (khabar al-wahid) does not give certain knowledge, and it is only considered in practical matters, not in beliefs. And here specifying their number contradicts the apparent meaning of God's statement: 'Of them are those We have related to you and of them are those We have not related to you' (40:78). And it is also possible that it contradicts reality, affirming prophethood for one who is not a prophet if the actual number is less than mentioned, and denying prophethood for one who is a prophet if it is greater. So it is better not to specify the number.)
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage discusses the issue of specifying the number of prophets and messengers. It begins by mentioning a hadith from Abu Dharr reporting the numbers: 124,000 prophets and 313 messengers. It then explains the preferred position of avoiding specification. The reasoning is that the hadith is a khabar al-wahid (single-narrator report), which yields only probability (zann) and is only authoritative in practical matters, not in beliefs (i'tiqadat). Furthermore, specifying a number contradicts the Quranic verse which states that many prophets are not mentioned. It could also lead to error: if the actual number is different, one might wrongly affirm prophecy where it is not or deny it where it is. Therefore, it is better to leave the number unspecified.

Four Proofs of Adam's Superiority over Angels
The text enumerates four proofs of Adam's superiority over angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four scriptural and rational proofs that theologians use to argue for Adam's superiority over the angels.

Core Idea
Four proofs show that Adam's status exceeds that of angels.

This passage presents four distinct proofs – from the rebellion of Iblis, the teaching of the names, God's choice, and the value of perseverance – that together establish the superiority of Adam (and by extension, prophets) over angels. We'll examine each proof and see how they connect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's sequence of proofs come through.
The passage is enumerative; notice the numbering.
2
Identify each proof's basis
Find each of the four proofs. Note whether each proof is scriptural (Quran or hadith) or rational.
The first proof is drawn from the story of Iblis, the second from the teaching of names, the third from a Quranic verse, the fourth is rational.
3
Reflect on the first two proofs
Consider how the first proof (Iblis's arrogance) and the second (teaching names) directly point to Adam's honored position before God.
The first proof implies that the command to prostrate honored Adam; the second shows his knowledge.
4
Reflect on the last two proofs
Examine the third proof (God's choice of Adam and other prophets) and the fourth (persistence amid obstacles). How do they strengthen the argument?
The third proof is explicit in the Quran; the fourth is a rational appeal to the value of effort.
5
Summarize the collective argument
Write a short summary in your own words explaining how these four proofs together establish Adam's superiority over angels.
Consider the cumulative weight of scriptural and rational evidence.

زيارة وتحية، بدليل استكبار إبليس وتعليله بأنه خير منه لكونه من نار، وآدم من طين . الثاني - أمر آدم بتعليمهم الأسماء قصداً إلى إظهار فضله . الثالث - أن الله اصطفى آدم ونوحاً وآل إبراهيم وآل عمران على العالمين، الذين من جملتهم الملائكة . الرابع - ان المواظبة على الطاعات مع الشواغل واكتساب الكمال مع العوائق أدخل في استحقاق الثواب .
Visitation and greeting, by the evidence of Iblis's arrogance and his reasoning that he is better than him because he is from fire and Adam from clay. Second: God's command to Adam to teach them the names intending to show his virtue. Third: That God chose Adam, Noah, the family of Ibrahim and the family of Imran over the worlds, among whom are the angels. Fourth: That persistence in acts of obedience amid distractions and acquiring perfection amid obstacles is more conducive to deserving reward.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage enumerates four proofs from scripture and reasoning for Adam's superiority over the angels. The first proof points to Iblis's (Satan's) refusal to prostrate due to his claim of superiority, which implies that Adam was honored by the command to the angels to prostrate. The second proof is God teaching Adam the names, which demonstrated his knowledge and thus his excellence. The third proof is direct from the Quran: God chose Adam and other prophets over the worlds, which includes angels. The fourth proof is rational: persisting in obedience despite obstacles is more commendable and thus Adam, who had such trials, is more deserving of reward and hence superior.

Superiority of Prophets: First Proof from Prostration
The author states the Ash'ari view and gives the first rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof used by Ash'ari theologians to argue for the superiority of prophets over angels.

Core Idea
Angels commanded to prostrate to Adam proves his superiority.

The debate over whether prophets or angels are superior is a classic point of contention in Islamic theology. Here, the author presents the Ash'ari position and offers a rational argument rooted in the prostration of angels to Adam. This passage lays out both the parties involved and the logical core of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the author's concise style.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the translation completes the thought.
2
Identify the positions
Identify which groups affirm superiority of prophets (Ash'aris, Shia) and which oppose (Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, al-Halimi).
Also note the nuanced view: common believers vs. common angels.
3
Understand the proof structure
The proof has two premises: (1) God commanded angels to prostrate to Adam, (2) the Wise does not command a superior to prostrate to an inferior. Therefore Adam is superior.
4
Analyze Iblis's reaction
Iblis's refusal and his reasoning (he is from fire, Adam from clay) demonstrate that the prostration was one of honor, not worship.
This confirms that superiority was implied.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof establishes Adam's superiority over angels, and by extension, the superiority of prophets (since Adam is a prophet).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني فذهب جمهور أصحابنا والشيعة إلى أن الأنبياء أفضل الملائكة، فالمعتزلة والقاضي، وأبي عبدالله الحليمي منا. وصرح بعض أصحابنا بأنا عوام البشر من المؤمنين أفضل من عوام الملائكة، وخواص من الملائكة أفضل من عوام البشر، أي غير الأنبياء. لنا وجوه نقلية وعقلية : الأول - أن الله تعالى أمر الملائكة بالسجود لآدم، والحكيم لا يأمر بسجود الأفضل للأدنى . وإباء إبليس واستكباره والتعليل بأنه خير من آدم لكونه من نار وآدم من طين يدل على أن المأمور به كان سجود تكرمة وتعظيم، لا
As for the second station, the majority of our companions (Ash'aris) and the Shia hold that prophets are superior to angels. The Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, and Abu 'Abdullah al-Halimi from among us (Ash'aris) (disagree). Some of our companions explicitly stated that the common believers among humans are superior to common angels, while the elite of angels are superior to common humans, i.e., other than prophets. We have transmitted and rational proofs: First, God commanded the angels to prostrate to Adam, and the Wise does not command prostration of the superior to the inferior. Iblis's refusal and arrogance, and his reasoning that he is better than Adam because he is from fire and Adam from clay, indicates that the commanded prostration was one of honor and veneration, not of worship.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage begins a new discussion on the relative superiority of prophets and angels. The author states the view of the majority of Ash'aris and Shia that prophets are superior to angels, while noting that some (Mu'tazila, Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, and al-Halimi) disagreed. He then presents the first rational proof: God commanded the angels to prostrate to Adam, and the Wise would not command a superior to prostrate to an inferior, so Adam must be superior. The behavior of Iblis confirms that the prostration was one of honor.

Misconception: The prostration of angels to Adam was worship.
Correction: The author clarifies that it was prostration of honor (sujud takrimah) and veneration, not worship (ibadah).

A) God taught Adam all names.

C) Prophets receive revelation.
D) Angels are created from light.

Key Takeaway
The command for angels to prostrate to Adam is a rational proof that prophets (like Adam) are superior to angels.

Angels' Objection and Prophetic Infallibility
Explains the meaning of the angels' objection and defends prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological interpretation of the angels' objection and how prophetic infallibility is preserved despite apparent errors.

Core Idea
A prophet's minor slip does not break infallibility; God's wisdom often lies beyond our understanding.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection: if the angels questioned God's decision to appoint a successor, does that reflect negatively on the prophets? The author clarifies the meaning of the angels' statement and shows that any apparent error is not a sin but part of God's hidden wisdom. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and reaffirms the doctrine of infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Notice the footnote markers (1) and (2) referencing Quranic verses.
2
Understand the context of the angels' question
Recognize that the angels' wonder was about the attributes of the caliph, not about God's truthfulness. Their statement 'if you are truthful' expresses astonishment.
3
Analyze God's response
God's answer 'I know what you do not know' points to hidden wisdoms and benefits in the appointment of Adam.
This shows that human understanding is limited.
4
Tackle the objection against infallibility
Consider the claim that this story seems to imply a lie or error, which would negate prophetic infallibility. The author raises this objection to answer it.
The objection is a common misunderstanding.
5
Learn the answer: minor error vs. sin
The author states that a small amount of error and forgetfulness does not negate infallibility and does not necessitate sin. Prophets are protected from major sins but may have minor unintended slips.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعنى إن كنتم صادقين) (۱) في أني أستخلف من يتصف بذلك من غير حكم ، ومصالح ، وصفات تلائم الاستخلاف، إذ التعجب إنما يكون عند ذلك. ولذا قال في الرد عليهم إني أعلم ما لا تعلمون) (٢) . إشارة إلى تلك الحكم والمصالح . لا يقال ففيه دلالة على نفي العصمة بإثبات الكذب من الجملة لأنا نقول: هذا القدر من الخطأ والسهو لا ينافي العصمة، ولا يوجب المعصية
The meaning: 'if you are truthful' (that) I will appoint as successor one who is described with that [quality] without judgment, and benefits, and attributes suitable for the caliphate, for wonder occurs only at that. Therefore He said in response to them: 'I know what you do not know', indicating those wisdoms and benefits. It is not said that there is in this a proof of negating infallibility by establishing lying in general, for we say: This amount of error and forgetfulness does not negate infallibility and does not necessitate disobedience.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer pair. It begins by explaining the meaning of the angels' statement 'if you are truthful' from the Quran when they questioned Adam's appointment as caliph. The author states that their wonder was about whether God would appoint a successor without judgment and other attributes fitting for the caliphate. God's response 'I know what you do not know' points to the hidden wisdoms and benefits. Then a second objection is raised: that this story would negate prophetic infallibility because it implies that God's statement contains a lie? Or that the angels' accusation of God? Actually the objection is that the narrative seems to attribute lying to God or the prophets? The author clarifies: this amount of error and forgetfulness does not negate infallibility and does not necessitate sin. The answer distinguishes minor faults from major sins, affirming that prophets are infallible in matters of religion but may have minor slips.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any error by a prophet negates their infallibility.
Correction: This passage teaches that minor errors or forgetfulness do not negate infallibility and are not sins.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility allows for minor unintended slips but not major sins, as exemplified in the story of Adam's caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The angels questioned God's wisdom in appointing Adam. Do you ever question God's choices in your own life—your circumstances, trials, or the people He puts in authority? What does this lesson teach you about trusting His knowledge over your own, and what situation in your life right now demands that trust?
?
If even a prophet could have a minor slip of forgetfulness, how does that influence your view of your own imperfections? Are you too harsh on yourself or too lenient? What commitment can you make to avoid overreacting to minor mistakes in yourself and others, while still holding yourself accountable for major sins?

Harut and Marut: A Trial, Not a Sin
Refutes the claim that angels Harut and Marut sinned by sorcery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of Harut and Marut is interpreted to uphold prophetic infallibility, showing that the angels were a trial, not sinners.

Core Idea
Harut and Marut were a divine trial, not sinful angels.

The story of Harut and Marut is often cited as a challenge to prophetic infallibility, implying that even angels can sin. The Sunni response is careful: the angels did not practice sorcery; rather, sorcery was revealed as a test, and the angels warned people against it. Their punishment was a rebuke, not for sin, but for a minor lapse, just as prophets are rebuked for forgetfulness. This lesson unpacks the passage and clarifies the doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to understand the objection and the answer presented.
Notice the structure: objection first, then the response.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between learning sorcery to practice it (which is disbelief) and learning it to avoid it (which is faith). Locate this in the text.
This distinction is crucial for the defense.
3
Understand the nature of punishment
The passage says the angels' punishment was a 'rebuke' (mu'ataba), not for a major sin. Compare this to how prophets are rebuked for minor errors.
This preserves the doctrine of angelic and prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - قصة هاروت وماروت ملكين ببابل يعذبان لارتكابهما السحر. (۳) والجواب منع ارتكابهما العمل بالسحر، واعتقاد تأثيره، بل أنزل الله تعالى عليهما السحر ابتلاء للناس، فمن تعلمه و عمل به فكافر. ومن تجنبه أو تعلمه ليتوقاه ولا يغتر به فهو مؤمن وهما كانا يعظان الناس ويقولان : إنما نحن فتنة (۳) للناس وابتلاء، فلا تكفروا أي لا تعتقدوا ولا تعملوا فإن ذلك كفر. وتعذيبهما إنما هو على وجه المعاتبة كما تعاتب الأنبياء على السهو والزلة من غير ارتكاب منهما لكبيرة فضلاً عن كفر ، واعتقاد سحر أو عمل به . واليهود هم الذين يدعون أن الواحد من الملك قد يرتكب الكبيرة فيعاقبه الله بالمسخ .
Third: The story of Harut and Marut, two angels in Babylon being punished for committing sorcery. The answer is to deny their committing the practice of sorcery and belief in its effect. Rather, God revealed sorcery to them as a trial for people. So whoever learns it and practices it is a disbeliever, and whoever avoids it or learns it to beware of it and not be deceived by it is a believer. They were warning people and saying: 'We are but a trial for people and a test, so do not disbelieve', i.e., do not believe [in sorcery] and do not practice it, for that is disbelief. Their punishment is only by way of rebuke, just as the prophets are rebuked for forgetfulness and slipping without their committing a major sin, let alone disbelief, belief in sorcery, or acting by it. The Jews are the ones who claim that one of the angels might commit a major sin and God would punish him by transformation.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents an objection about the angels Harut and Marut being punished for sorcery, then gives the answer. The author denies that they actually practiced sorcery or believed in its efficacy. Rather, God sent down sorcery to them as a trial for people. Learning and practicing sorcery is disbelief, but learning it to avoid it is faith. The angels themselves warned people not to disbelieve. Their punishment is not for actual sin but is a rebuke, similar to how prophets are rebuked for minor forgetfulness without major sin. The author notes that it is the Jews who claim that an angel could commit a major sin and be punished by metamorphosis, implying that Muslims do not hold such a view. Thus the story does not impugn the prophets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the angels Harut and Marut actually committed sin by practicing sorcery and were justly punished for it.
Correction: The passage explains they did not practice sorcery; they were a test for people, and their punishment was a rebuke for a minor lapse, not for a major sin.

Checkpoint

A) For practicing sorcery.
B) For teaching sorcery to people.
C) As a rebuke for a minor error, not for actual sin.
D) For disbelieving.

Key Takeaway
The story of Harut and Marut does not contradict prophetic infallibility; they were a divine trial, not sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a temptation that is clearly forbidden, do you actively warn yourself and others like Harut and Marut did, or do you make excuses to indulge? What does your response reveal about the depth of your faith?
?
The angels said, 'We are but a trial, so do not disbelieve.' In what area of your life are you currently being tested? Are you treating that test as a divine provision to strengthen your faith, or are you allowing it to pull you away from Allah?
?
If a prophet can be rebuked for a minor slip, how does that humble you when you consider your own mistakes? Do you accept Allah's rebuke with gratitude, or do you rationalize your disobedience?

Prophet Superiority over Angels: The Prostration Proof
States the thesis and gives the first proof from the prostration of angels to Adam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first transmitted proof for the superiority of prophets over angels, based on the prostration of angels to Adam.

Core Idea
The prostration of angels to Adam proves Adam's superiority over them.

This lesson examines a central theological argument: if God commanded angels to prostrate to Adam, then Adam must be superior, for a wise being does not command the superior to honor an inferior. The passage presents the majority Ash'ari and Shia view, notes the opposition, and refines the discussion by distinguishing between common and elite angels and humans. The proof from the prostration event is unpacked, and the rebellion of Iblis is used to reinforce the nature of the prostration as one of honor.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Try to grasp the main claim and the proof before diving into commentary.
Notice the structure: thesis, opposition, distinction, then proof.
2
Identify the three positions mentioned
Look for: (a) the majority view (Ash'aris and Shia) that prophets are superior to angels, (b) the opposing view (Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, al-Halimi), and (c) the intermediate distinction (common believers vs. common angels, elite angels vs. common humans).
This distinction is important for nuance.
3
Analyze the first rational proof
The proof uses the command for angels to prostrate to Adam. Ask yourself: Why does this command imply Adam's superiority? The commentary explains the logical inference.
Pay attention to how Iblis's refusal supports the interpretation.
4
Reflect on the nature of the prostration
The passage clarifies that the prostration was one of honor (takrima), not worship or greeting. This is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
Iblis's reasoning (fire vs. clay) shows he understood it as an act of honoring Adam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني فذهب جمهور أصحابنا والشيعة إلى أن الأنبياء أفضل الملائكة، فالمعتزلة والقاضي، وأبي عبدالله الحليمي منا. وصرح بعض أصحابنا بأنا عوام البشر من المؤمنين أفضل من عوام الملائكة، وخواص من الملائكة أفضل من عوام البشر، أي غير الأنبياء. لنا وجوه نقلية وعقلية : الأول - أن الله تعالى أمر الملائكة بالسجود لآدم، والحكيم لا يأمر بسجود الأفضل للأدنى . وإباء إبليس واستكباره والتعليل بأنه خير من آدم لكونه من نار وآدم من طين يدل على أن المأمور به كان سجود تكرمة وتعظيم، لا سجود تحية وزيارة ولا سجود الأعلى للأدنى إعظاماً له، ورفعاً لمنزلته وهضماً لنفوس الساجدين .
As for the second station, the majority of our scholars and the Shia hold that the prophets are better than the angels. The Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, and Abu Abdullah al-Halimi from us [hold otherwise]. Some of our scholars explicitly stated that the common believers among mankind are better than the common angels, and the elite among the angels are better than the common humans, i.e., other than the prophets. We have transmitted and rational proofs: First, God Almighty commanded the angels to prostrate to Adam, and the wise does not command the prostration of the superior to the inferior. The refusal of Iblis and his arrogance and his reasoning that he is better than Adam because he is from fire and Adam from clay indicates that the commanded act was a prostration of honor and veneration, not a prostration of greeting and visitation, nor a prostration of the higher to the lower, but [a prostration] honoring him, elevating his rank, and humbling the souls of the prostrators.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage begins the second station of debate: the superiority of prophets over angels. The author states the view of the majority of Ash'aris and Shia that prophets are better than angels, while the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, and Abu Abdullah al-Halimi among the Ash'aris disagree. Some Ash'aris further distinguish: common believers are better than common angels, but the elite angels are better than common humans (i.e., non-prophets). Then the first proof is given: God commanded the angels to prostrate to Adam. A wise being does not command the superior to prostrate to an inferior. Iblis's refusal and his justification show that the prostration was one of honor, not worship. Therefore, Adam is superior to the angels who prostrated, and by extension all prophets share this superiority since Adam is a prophet.

Correction: The prostration was one of honor (takrima), not worship. It specifically proves Adam's superiority over those angels, not that angels are inferior in every aspect.
