Second Proof: Adam Taught the Angels the Names
Sanusi presents the second proof of Adam's superiority and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Adam's teaching of the names serves as a proof of his superiority over the angels, and how the objection of angelic knowledge is refuted.

Core Idea
Teaching the names proves Adam's superiority over the angels.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the second proof that Adam's superiority over the angels is established by his role as teacher. He then addresses a potential objection: that the angels had vast knowledge from the Preserved Tablet. The refutation shows that Adam's knowledge was unique and divinely given, as indicated by God's statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: proof, objection, refutation.
Notice the Quranic verse quoted.
2
Identify the main argument
The argument is that the teacher is better than the learner. Since Adam taught the angels, he is superior.
This is a simple logical deduction.
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims that angels had immense knowledge from the Preserved Tablet and long experience, which could surpass Adam's knowledge.
This objection challenges the proof.
4
See the refutation
God's statement 'Did I not tell you that I know the unseen of the heavens and the earth?' shows that Adam's knowledge was part of the unseen that God knew and revealed to him, proving his special status.
The refutation relies on divine authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن آدم أنبأهم بالأسماء وبما علم الله من الخصائص والمعلم أفضل من المتعلم، وسوق الآية ينادي على أن الغرض إظهار ما خفي عليهم من أفضلية آدم، ودفع ما توهموا فيه من النقصان. ولذا قال الله تعالى: ( ألم اقل لكم إني أعلم غيب السموات والأرض) (۱) وبهذا يندفع ما يقال: إن لهم أيضاً علوماً جمة أضعاف العلم بالأسماء لما شاهدوا من اللوح وحصلوا في الأزمنة المتطاولة بالتجارب والأنظار المتوالية .
Second: That Adam informed them of the names and what God taught of the characteristics, and the teacher is better than the learner. The context of the verse calls out that the purpose was to show what was hidden from them of Adam's superiority and to refute what they supposed of deficiency. Therefore God said: 'Did I not tell you that I know the unseen of the heavens and the earth?' And by this is repelled what is said: that they also have vast knowledge, many times the knowledge of names, because they observed the Tablet and obtained [knowledge] over long periods through successive experiments and considerations.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. The argument is that Adam taught the angels the names of things, and being a teacher indicates superiority over the learners. The Quranic verse quoted ('Did I not tell you that I know the unseen...') shows that God's purpose was to reveal Adam's superiority and remove any angelic misconception of deficiency. An objection is raised that the angels had immense knowledge from the Preserved Tablet and long experience. This is repelled: the knowledge of names was a special knowledge given to Adam that they did not have, proving his distinct superiority.

Third Proof: God's Selection Includes Angels
Cites the Quranic verse of divine selection and argues it includes angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse of divine selection (God choosing Adam, Noah, and the families of Abraham and Imran) establishes the superiority of all prophets over angels.

Core Idea
No specifier excludes angels from 'worlds', so prophets are superior to angels.

This lesson presents the third rational proof for the superiority of prophets over angels, based on a clear Quranic verse. The verse states that God chose certain individuals and families 'over the worlds'. The key move is to show that 'worlds' must include angels, and that prophets are included in that choice. The reasoning is tight and relies on a foundational principle of usul: the absence of a specifying evidence (mukhassis) leaves the general term intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic of Quran 3:33 and its literal translation. Notice the phrase 'over the worlds' (على العالمين). This is the starting point of the proof.
This verse is the foundation of the argument.
2
Understand the specification of non-prophets
The author points out that by consensus (ijma), the families of Abraham and Imran include non-prophets. This shows that the verse is not limited to prophets only, but the context here is about prophets being chosen.
The consensus is a key supporting evidence.
3
Identify the conclusion
Adam, Noah, and all prophets are chosen over the worlds. Since 'worlds' includes angels, prophets are superior to angels.
4
Reflect on the absence of a specifier
The author argues there is no mukhassis (specifier) to exclude angels from 'worlds', and no basis to interpret it as only the majority of creatures. This strengthens the claim that angels are included.
This is a key logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - قوله تعالى : إن الله اصطفى آدم ونوحاً وآل إبراهيم وآل عمران على العالمين) (۲). وقد خص من آل إبراهيم وآل عمران غير الأنبياء بدليل الإجماع . فيكون آدم ونوح ، وجميع الأنبياء مصطفين على العالمين الذين منهم الملائكة، إذ لا مخصص للملائكة عن العالمين، ولا جهة لتفسيره بالكثير من المخلوقات .
Third: God's saying: 'Indeed God chose Adam and Noah and the family of Abraham and the family of Imran over the worlds.' He has specified from the family of Abraham and Imran other than the prophets by evidence of consensus. So Adam and Noah and all the prophets are chosen over the worlds, among which are the angels, for there is no specification of the angels from the worlds, nor any basis to interpret it as [only] the majority of creatures.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof: the Quranic verse stating that God chose Adam, Noah, and the families of Abraham and Imran over the worlds. The author notes that by consensus, the families of Abraham and Imran include non-prophets. Therefore, the prophets among them, including Adam and Noah, are chosen over all the worlds. Since there is no evidence to exclude angels from the term 'worlds', the verse establishes the superiority of all prophets over angels.

Key Terms
اصطفاء
آل إبراهيم
آل عمران
عالمين
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'worlds' in the verse refers only to humans or jinn, excluding angels.
Correction: The author argues that there is no evidence to exclude angels from the general term 'worlds', so angels are included by default.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how do we know the verse of divine selection includes angels?
A) The verse explicitly mentions angels.
B) There is no specifier (mukhassis) to exclude angels from 'worlds'.
C) Angels are not part of the worlds.
D) The verse only refers to human families.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse 'God chose Adam and Noah... over the worlds' establishes the superiority of all prophets over angels because there is no evidence to exclude angels from 'worlds'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
You now know that prophets are superior to angels. Angels worship God without fail, yet prophets are higher. How does this affect your hope for your own salvation? Will you turn more earnestly to following the prophets, knowing their status with God?

Struggle in Worship and the Objection of Angelic Worship
Proof and objection regarding worship merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that human obstacles make worship more rewardable and the objection based on angels' superior worship.

Core Idea
Difficulty increases merit, but quantity and quality also matter.

The author presents a key argument for the superiority of human worship: overcoming worldly distractions makes our worship more difficult and thus more rewarding. However, an important objection is raised: angelic worship, being constant and pure, may surpass ours despite their lack of struggles. This lesson unpacks the proof and the objection, noting that the answer is incomplete in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof about human obstacles
Read the first part of the passage describing human preoccupations like desire and anger, and how constancy in worship despite these obstacles increases merit.
Notice that the proof relies on the principle that difficulty enhances reward.
2
Identify the objection
Read the objection starting with 'لا يقال'. It grants that angels lack such obstacles but questions whether difficulty alone makes worship better if quantity and quality differ.
The objection points out that angels worship more constantly and with greater sincerity, certainty, and piety.
3
Analyze the incomplete answer
The answer begins with 'li-anna' (because) but cuts off. Consider what the full answer might be: perhaps that human effort against obstacles adds a dimension of merit that angelic worship lacks, despite their higher quantity.
This is a common scholarly debate; the missing answer suggests the author may have elaborated further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن للبشر شواغل عن الطاعات العلمية والعملية كالشهوة، والغضب، وسائر الحاجات الشاغلة ، والموانع الخارجة والداخلة . فالمواظبة على العبادات، وتحصيل الكمالات بالقهر والغلبة على ما يضاد القوة العاقلة يكون أشق وأفضل وأبلغ في استحقاق الثواب ولا معنى للأفضلية سوى زيادة استحقاق الثواب والكرامة لا يقال لو سلم انتفاء الشهوة والغضب وسائر الشواغل في حق الملائكة ، فالعبادة مع كثرة المتاعب والشواغل إنما تكون أشق وأفضل من الأخرى إذا استويا في المقدار وباقي الصفات وعبادة الملائكة أكثر وأدوم ، فإنهم يسبحون الليل والنهار لا يفترون والإخلاص الذي به القوام والنظام واليقين الذي هو الأساس والتقوى التي هي الثمرة فيهم أقوى وأقوم لأن
Fourth: That humans have preoccupations from intellectual and practical acts of obedience, such as desire, anger, and other distracting needs, and external and internal obstacles. So constancy in worship and achieving perfections by overcoming and prevailing over that which opposes the rational faculty is more difficult, better, and more deserving of reward. And there is no meaning of superiority other than increase in desert of reward and honor. It is not said: if it is granted that the absence of desire, anger, and other distractions in the case of angels, then worship with many troubles and distractions is only more difficult and better than the other if they are equal in quantity and remaining qualities. And the worship of angels is more and more constant, for they glorify night and day without tiring. And the sincerity which is the support and order, the certainty which is the foundation, and the piety which is the fruit are stronger and more upright in them, because...
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof and then an objection with an incomplete answer. The proof argues that humans face many distractions (desire, anger, etc.) and external or internal obstacles. Persisting in worship by overcoming these obstacles is harder and thus more virtuous and deserving of reward. Superiority is defined as increase in reward. Then an objection is raised: even if angels lack these distractions, worship with obstacles is only more difficult and better if the quantity and other qualities are equal. But angels' worship is more constant (night and day), and their sincerity, certainty, and piety are stronger. Therefore, the objection implies that angelic worship may be more meritorious. The answer begins with 'li-anna' (because), but the text cuts off. The full answer would likely argue that despite quantity, the effort expended in overcoming human obstacles adds merit that angelic worship lacks.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any worship with difficulty is automatically better than any worship without difficulty, regardless of quantity or other qualities.
Correction: The objection clarifies that difficulty only enhances merit when other factors (quantity, sincerity, etc.) are equal. Otherwise, a constant and pure worship may be superior.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Second Argument: Adam's Superiority Through Teaching Names
Presents the second argument that Adam teaching names proves his superiority over angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for Adam's superiority, based on the principle that the teacher is superior to the learner, and how the objection about the angels' knowledge is refuted.

Core Idea
The teacher is superior to the learner: Adam taught the angels, so he is superior.

Sanusi presents the second proof for Adam's superiority, drawing on the verse where Adam names all things. The argument rests on a simple logical principle: the teacher is superior to the student. Watch also how he anticipates and refutes a possible objection: the angels' vast knowledge from the Preserved Tablet could not surpass Adam's directly taught knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the passage and note the key principle: al-mu'allim afḍal min al-muta'allim.
2
Identify the verse cited
Look for the Quranic quotation and understand its role in the argument.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
Notice the objection about the angels' knowledge from the Preserved Tablet and how the refutation uses the superiority of directly taught knowledge.
The refutation shows that even with extensive knowledge, the angels are still learners compared to Adam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن آدم أنبأهم بالأسماء وبما علم الله من الخصائص والمعلم أفضل من المتعلم، وسوق الآية ينادي على أن الغرض إظهار ما خفي عليهم من أفضلية آدم، ودفع ما توهموا فيه من النقصان. ولذا قال الله تعالى: ألم اقل لكم إني أعلم غيب السموات والأرض وبهذا يندفع ما يقال: إن لهم أيضاً علوماً جمة أضعاف العلم بالأسماء لما شاهدوا من اللوح وحصلوا في الأزمنة المتطاولة بالتجارب والأنظار المتوالية.
The second: Adam informed them of the names and of what God taught of the characteristics, and the teacher is superior to the learner. The flow of the verse calls out that the purpose was to show what was hidden from them of Adam's superiority, and to rebut what they supposed of deficiency. Hence God said: 'Did I not tell you that I know the unseen of the heavens and the earth?' And by this is repelled what is said: that they also have vast sciences, many times greater than the knowledge of the names, because they observed the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ) and acquired over extended periods through experiments and successive reflections.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for Adam's superiority over the angels. It begins by stating that Adam informed the angels of the names and divinely taught characteristics, and the principle that the teacher is superior to the learner. The verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:33) is cited to show that God's statement was intended to reveal to the angels Adam's superiority and to rebut any notion of his deficiency. Then it refutes a possible objection that the angels also possess vast knowledge, because they saw the Preserved Tablet and accumulated knowledge through long experience. The argument is that despite that, Adam's knowledge was directly from God, making him superior.

الأفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the angels' extensive knowledge from the Preserved Tablet makes them superior to Adam.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Adam's knowledge is directly taught by God, and the teacher is superior to the learner. The angels' knowledge, however vast, is acquired through means, whereas Adam's is divinely gifted, making him superior.

Checkpoint

A) The learner is superior to the teacher.
B) The teacher is superior to the learner.
C) Knowledge from experience is superior.
D) Knowledge from the Preserved Tablet is superior.

Key Takeaway
Adam's superiority over the angels is established by the principle that the teacher is superior to the learner, and the angels' vast knowledge does not surpass Adam's direct divine instruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divine Selection and Prophetic Superiority over Angels
Uses Quran 3:33 to argue prophets' superiority over all worlds including angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse of divine selection (Quran 3:33) is used to prove that prophets are chosen over all worlds, including angels.

Core Idea
Prophets are chosen over all creation, including angels, by divine selection.

This lesson presents the third argument from the verse of divine selection. The author argues that the verse explicitly mentions Adam and Noah, and by consensus includes all prophets from the families of Abraham and Imran. Since 'the worlds' encompasses all beings, including angels, and there is no evidence to exclude angels, the prophets' superiority is established.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic text carefully. Note the key terms: اصطفى, العالمين, الملائكة.
The passage is about the third argument.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage claims that the verse proves prophets are chosen over all worlds, including angels. Find the evidence given.
Consensus is used to specify the families.
3
Understand the reasoning
Follow the logic: the verse says 'over the worlds' which is universal; no exception for angels is given; so prophets are superior to angels.
This refutes those who limit 'worlds' to humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - قوله تعالى: إن الله اصطفى آدم ونوحاً وآل إبراهيم وآل عمران على العالمين. وقد خص من آل إبراهيم وآل عمران غير الأنبياء بدليل الإجماع. فيكون آدم ونوح وجميع الأنبياء مصطفين على العالمين الذين منهم الملائكة، إذ لا مخصص للملائكة عن العالمين، ولا جهة لتفسيره بالكثير من المخلوقات.
The third: His statement, may He be exalted: 'Indeed Allah chose Adam and Noah and the family of Abraham and the family of Imran over the worlds.' And it is specified from the family of Abraham and the family of Imran other than the prophets by evidence of consensus. So Adam, Noah, and all the prophets are chosen over the worlds, which include the angels, since there is no specification excluding the angels from the worlds, nor any basis for interpreting it as 'many of the creations'.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument from the verse of divine selection (Quran 3:33). It argues that the verse mentions Adam and Noah specifically, and then the families of Abraham and Imran, but the intended meaning includes all prophets from those families based on consensus. Therefore, the prophets are chosen over 'the worlds' (al-ʿālamīn), which includes the angels. Since there is no evidence to exclude angels from 'the worlds', and the verse does not limit it to 'many of the creations', the argument stands that prophets are superior to angels.

Correction: The passage argues that 'the worlds' includes all beings, including angels, based on the general nature of the term and lack of specification.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The verse of divine selection (Quran 3:33) proves that prophets are chosen over all worlds, which includes angels, because no exception is made for angels in the term 'al-ʿālamīn'.

Fourth Argument: Human Distractions Increase Worship Merit
Argues that human distractions increase worship's merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how human struggles against distractions make their worship more meritorious than that of angels, and the logical defense against the objection that angels' worship is superior.

Core Idea
Overcoming distractions makes human worship more difficult and therefore more rewarding.

In this passage, the author presents a classic argument for the superiority of human worship over that of angels. He argues that the numerous distractions humans face make their worship more difficult and thus more deserving of reward. He anticipates an objection based on the quantity and quality of angelic worship and provides a reasoned response, emphasizing that hardship only has meaning when there is opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the passage once to grasp the claim: human distractions (desire, anger, etc.) make worship harder and therefore better.
Notice the list of distractions: شهوة, غضب, etc.
2
Examine the objection
The objector says that angels have no distractions, their worship is more abundant and constant, and their sincerity, certainty, and piety are stronger because they see directly.
The objection is introduced by 'لا يقال'.
3
Study the answer
The author replies that the absence of distractions is granted, but hardship without opposition is inconceivable. He also rejects the claim that prophets' qualities are weaker.
The answer begins with 'لأنا نقول'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن للبشر شواغل عن الطاعات العلمية والعملية كالشهوة والغضب وسائر الحاجات الشاغلة والموانع الخارجة والداخلة. فالمواظبة على العبادات وتحصيل الكمالات بالقهر والغلبة على ما يضاد القوة العاقلة يكون أشق وأفضل وأبلغ في استحقاق الثواب ولا معنى للأفضلية سوى زيادة استحقاق الثواب والكرامة لا يقال لو سلم انتفاء الشهوة والغضب وسائر الشواغل في حق الملائكة، فالعبادة مع كثرة المتاعب والشواغل إنما تكون أشق وأفضل من الأخرى إذا استويا في المقدار وباقي الصفات وعبادة الملائكة أكثر وأدوم، فإنهم يسبحون الليل والنهار لا يفترون والإخلاص الذي به القوام والنظام واليقين الذي هو الأساس والتقوى التي هي الثمرة فيهم أقوى وأقوم لأن طريقهم العيان لا البيان والمشاهدة لا المراسلة، لأنا نقول: انتفاء الشواغل في حقهم مما لم ينازع فيه أحد. ووجود المشقة والألم في العبادة والعمل عند عدم المنافي والمضاد مما لا يعقل قلت أو كثرت. وكون باقي الصفات في حق الأنبياء أضعف وأدنى مما لا يسمع ولا يقبل.
The fourth: Humans have distractions from both theoretical and practical acts of obedience, such as desire, anger, and all other distracting needs, and external and internal obstacles. So perseverance in worship and acquisition of perfections by suppressing and overcoming that which opposes the rational faculty is more difficult, better, and more deserving of reward, and there is no meaning to superiority except an increase in the deserving of reward and honor. It should not be said: If we grant the absence of desire, anger, and all distractions in the case of the angels, then worship in the face of numerous hardships and distractions is only more difficult and better if they are equal in quantity and other qualities, and the angels' worship is more abundant and enduring, for they glorify night and day without flagging, and the sincerity upon which everything depends, the certainty which is the foundation, and the piety which is the fruit are stronger and more upright in them, because their way is direct vision not exposition, and witnessing not correspondence. For we say: The absence of distractions in their case is not disputed by anyone. And the existence of hardship and pain in worship and action in the absence of what opposes and contradicts is inconceivable, whether little or much. And that the other qualities in the case of the prophets are weaker and lower is something not to be heard nor accepted.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is the fourth argument, structured as a claim followed by an objection and answer. The argument states that humans have numerous distractions (shahwa, ghadab, etc.) that make their worship more difficult, and therefore more virtuous. Superiority is essentially greater deserving of reward. The objector claims that if angels lack distractions, then their worship is more abundant and constant, and their sincerity (ikhlāṣ), certainty (yaqīn), and piety (taqwā) are stronger because they see directly. The author answers that the absence of distractions is conceded, but hardship without opposition is meaningless. Furthermore, the notion that the prophets' qualities are weaker is rejected outright. The answer uses a logical argument: hardship only exists when there is opposition; without opposition, effort is not felt.

Key Terms
شواغل
شهوة
غضب
مشقة
ثواب
إخلاص
يقين
تقوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: More worship automatically means more reward.
Correction: This argument shows that difficulty and overcoming opposition increase merit, not just quantity.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection against this argument?
A) Angels have more distractions.
B) Angels' worship is less sincere.
C) Angels' worship is more abundant and their qualities stronger.
D) Humans cannot achieve sincerity.

Key Takeaway
Human worship is more meritorious because it involves overcoming distractions, and hardship only exists where there is opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You face daily distractions like desire and anger. How often do you consciously overcome them to perform worship? Will you commit to tracking one act of worship this week that you perform despite a strong distraction, and reflect on the increased reward?

Argument from Intellect and Desire: The Human Median
Argument using the median nature of humans between angels and beasts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the combination of intellect and desire in humans positions them potentially above angels or below beasts, and how this supports the prophethood argument about choosing perfection despite the ability to choose deficiency.

Core Idea
Overcoming desire elevates humans above angels who lack desire.

This lesson presents an argument based on the three categories of beings: angels (intellect only), beasts (desire only), and humans (both). The author argues that when a human's intellect overcomes desire, he surpasses angels, while when desire dominates, he falls below beasts. This argument is then linked to a previous discussion about choosing perfection despite the ability to choose deficiency, showing that the believer who overcomes desire is more excellent than angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the concise style and the Quranic citation.
2
Understand the three categories
Identify the three types of beings: angels (intellect without desire), beasts (desire without intellect), and humans (both). This sets up the comparison.
3
Analyze the two conditionals
See how the outcome changes based on which faculty dominates: if desire wins, human is lower than beasts; if intellect wins, human is higher than angels.
The Quranic verse supports the lower status.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Understand the link to the previous point: the disbeliever chose deficiency despite being able to choose perfection, making his act worse. Conversely, choosing perfection despite ability for deficiency is more excellent, so the believer who overcomes desire is superior to angels.
This reinforces the prophethood argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتمسك بأن للملائكة عقلاً بلا شهوة وللبهائم شهوة بلا عقل، وللإنسان كليهما. فإذا ترجح شهوته على عقله يكون أدنى من البهائم، لقوله تعالى: ﴿بل هم أضل﴾. فإذا ترجح عقله على شهوته يجب أن يكون أعلى من الملائكة، وهذا عائد إلى ما سبق، لأن تمام تقريره هو أن الكافر آثر النقصان مع التمكن من الكمال، وكل من المضاد فعل كذا فهو أضل وأرذل ممن آثره بدونه لأن إيثار الشيء مع وجود المنافي أرجح وأبلغ من إيثاره بدونه فيلزم ان يكون من آثر الكمال مع التمكن من النقصان أفضل وأكمل ممن آثره بدونه.
And it may be argued that the angels have intellect without desire, and beasts have desire without intellect, and humans have both. So if his desire overcomes his intellect, he is lower than the beasts, as per His statement: 'Nay, they are even more astray.' So if his intellect overcomes his desire, he must be higher than the angels. And this returns to what preceded, because its complete formulation is that the disbeliever chose deficiency despite being able to choose perfection, and whoever does such a contrary act is more astray and viler than the one who does it without that ability, because the choice of a thing in the presence of its opposite is more weighty and emphatic than the choice of it without that opposite. Hence it follows that the one who chooses perfection despite being able to choose deficiency is better and more perfect than the one who chooses it without that ability.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents another argument based on the combination of intellect and desire. It classifies angels as having intellect without desire, beasts as having desire without intellect, and humans as having both. If a human's desire overcomes intellect, he becomes lower than beasts (citing Quran 7:179). Conversely, if his intellect overcomes desire, he surpasses angels. The reasoning is then related to a previous point: choosing perfection despite the ability to choose deficiency is more meritorious than choosing perfection without such an ability. So the believer who overcomes desire is superior to the angel who has no desire to overcome.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans are inherently inferior to angels because they have desire.
Correction: This argument shows that overcoming desire elevates humans above angels, so desire is not an inherent flaw but an opportunity for merit.

Checkpoint

A) When he has no desire.
B) When his desire overcomes intellect.

Key Takeaway
The believer who overcomes desire is superior even to angels, because he chooses perfection despite the ability to choose deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when have you allowed your desires to overcome your intellect, causing you to act in a way that is lower than a beast? How does this passage make you feel about that moment, and what can you do today to choose intellect over desire?
?
This argument states that if your intellect overcomes your desire, you become higher than the angels. But are you living as if that is true? Consider one area where you are settling for spiritual deficiency when you could choose perfection. What would change if you took that step?
?
The passage says the disbeliever chose deficiency despite being able to choose perfection. Is there any area in your life where you are knowingly choosing the lesser option (e.g., a small sin, laziness, neglect) even though you have the ability to choose better? What is holding you back, and how can you start choosing perfection today?

Does 'Honor' Imply Superiority Over Angels?
Rebuttal of the argument from the verse about honoring Adam's children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Indeed We honored the children of Adam' does not prove that humans are superior to angels.

Core Idea
Honor does not automatically imply superiority over all.

Many argue that because Allah honored humans in the Quran, humans must be superior to angels. However, a careful reading of the same verse and other verses shows that honor is not exclusive and does not entail absolute preference. This lesson examines the rebuttal provided by the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the author's argument in his own words.
Notice the structure: the author first states the opposing argument, then provides two counterpoints.
2
Identify the two counterpoints
Look for the two reasons the author gives for why the honor verse does not prove superiority: (1) honor does not entail preference, (2) preference is limited to 'many' not all.
The second point is supported by another verse: 'And We preferred them over many of those We created'.
3
Consider the additional evidence
The author adds that angels are also described as 'honored servants' in the Quran, showing that honor is shared.
This further weakens the argument from honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التمسك بقوله تعالى: ولقد كرمنا بني آدم والتكريم المطلق لأحد الأجناس يشعر بفضله على غيره، فضعيف. لأن التكريم لا يوجب التفضيل سيما مع قوله تعالى وفضلناهم على كثير ممن خلقنا تفضيلا فإنه يشعر بعدم التفضيل على القليل وليس غير الملائكة بالإجماع، كيف وقد وصف الملائكة أيضاً بأنهم عباد مكرمون.
As for the argument from His statement: 'And indeed We have honored the children of Adam' – the absolute honor of one species suggests its superiority over others, but that is weak. Because honor does not entail preference, especially with His statement: 'And We preferred them over many of those We created, a great preference' which suggests they are not preferred over the few, and there is nothing other than angels by consensus. Moreover, angels are also described as 'honored servants' (ʿibād mukramūn).
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage rebuts the argument that the Quranic verse 'ولقد كرمنا بني آدم' (We have honored the children of Adam) proves human superiority over angels. The author states that while general honor may imply superiority, it is not decisive, especially when another verse says 'فضلناهم على كثير' (preferred them over many), implying not over all. Since angels are a few (or a special class), the verse does not include them. Moreover, angels themselves are called 'عباد مكرمون' (honored servants) in the Quran (21:26), so honor is common.

Key Terms
تكريم
تفضيل
بنو آدم
عباد مكرمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Indeed We honored the children of Adam' proves that humans are superior to all creatures, including angels.
Correction: The verse does not prove superiority over angels because honor does not imply preference, and the same verse later limits preference to 'many' not all, thereby excluding angels. Moreover, angels are themselves described as honored.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the argument from this verse weak?
A) Because the verse is not authentic.
B) Because honor does not necessarily imply preference, and another verse limits preference to 'many' not all.
C) Because angels are not included in 'children of Adam'.
D) Because the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The verse honoring Adam's children does not establish human superiority over angels because honor does not imply preference and the preference is limited to 'many' not all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The verse limits preference to 'many,' not all. Who are the 'few'? Are you comfortable with the idea that some creation may be preferred over you? How does this reshape your humility before Allah?
?
Angels are also 'honored servants.' Does this teaching humble you? Commit to reciting the verse about honored angels (21:26) with new understanding, and ask yourself: Do I honor Allah's creation as He does?

Introduces opponents' first argument from verses about angels.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Opponents cite angels' honor and unwavering obedience as evidence for their superiority.

This lesson introduces the first argument from opponents of the mainstream view that prophets are superior to angels. The Mu'tazilites, Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, and al-Halimi point to Quranic verses that describe angels as honored, close to God, and eternally obedient without pride. This argument sets the stage for understanding a key theological debate about relative merit.

Refuting Angelic Superiority: Rational and Scriptural Arguments
Author refutes claims of angelic superiority using logic and scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and scriptural refutation of the claim that angels are superior to prophets.

Core Idea
Angels' lack of desire is not a virtue if unchallenged; honor does not imply superiority.

The author systematically dismantles two key arguments for angelic superiority: the claim that prophets face distractions and the claim that angels' pure intellect makes them superior. He also shows why the verse about honoring Adam does not prove superiority over angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Observe the structure: first the rational argument, then the scriptural one.
Note the phrase 'طريقهم العيان لا البيان' as a key starting point.
2
Analyze the rational argument
Focus on the part about intellect and desire. The author argues that choosing perfection despite obstacles is more virtuous than having no obstacles.
This refutation uses the same logic the opponent used, but reversed.
3
Examine the scriptural refutation
Note how the author uses the verse 'وَفَضَّلْنَاهُمْ عَلَىٰ كَثِيرٍ' to show that the honoring does not cover angels, and that angels are also honored.
The key point: honoring does not necessitate superiority (تفضيل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طريقهم العيان لا البيان والمشاهدة لا المراسلة، لأنا نقول : انتفاء الشواغل في حقهم مما لم ينازع فيه أحد. ووجود المشقة والألم في العبادة والعمل عند عدم المنافي والمضاد مما لا يعقل قلت أو كثرت . وكون باقي الصفات في حق الأنبياء أضعف وأدنى مما لا يسمع ولا يقبل. وقد يتمسك بأن للملائكة عقلاً بلا شهوة وللبهائم شهوة بلا عقل، وللإنسان كليهما . فإذا ترجح شهوته على عقله يكون أدنى من البهائم، لقوله تعالى: ﴿بل هم أضل﴾ (۱) . فإذا ترجح عقله على شهوته يجب أن يكون أعلى من الملائكة، وهذا عائد إلى ما سبق، لأن تمام تقريره هو أن الكافر آثر النقصان مع التمكن من الكمال، وكل من المضاد فعل كذا فهو أضل وأرذل ممن آثره بدونه لأن إيثار الشيء مع وجود المنافي أرجح وأبلغ من إيثاره بدونه فيلزم ان يكون من آثر الكمال مع التمكن من النقصان أفضل وأكمل ممن آثره بدونه . وأما التمسك بقوله تعالى : ( ولقد كَرَّمْنَا بني آدم (۲) والتكريم المطلق لأحد الأجناس يشعر بفضله على غيره، فضعيف. لأن التكريم لا يوجب التفضيل سيما مع قوله تعالى وفضلناهم على كثير ممن خلقنا تفضيلا (۳) فإنه يشعر بعدم التفضيل على القليل وليس غير الملائكة بالإجماع، كيف وقد وصف الملائكة أيضاً بأنهم عباد مكرمون )
Their way is direct vision not statement, and observation not correspondence, for we say: The absence of distractions in their case is something that no one has disputed. And the existence of hardship and pain in worship and action when there is no obstacle or contrary is something that is not rational, whether little or much. And that the remaining attributes in the case of the prophets are weaker and lower is something that is not heard nor accepted. And it may be argued that angels have intellect without desire, and beasts have desire without intellect, and humans have both. So if his desire overcomes his intellect, he becomes lower than beasts, due to His saying: 'Nay, they are more astray' (1). So if his intellect overcomes his desire, he must be higher than angels, and this returns to what preceded, because the full establishment of it is that the disbeliever chose deficiency while being capable of perfection, and whoever [does] such a thing is more astray and viler than one who chose it without it, because choosing a thing with the presence of the contrary is more preferable and effective than choosing it without it. So it follows that whoever chooses perfection while being capable of deficiency is better and more perfect than one who chooses it without it. As for clinging to His saying: 'And indeed We have honored the children of Adam' (2), and absolute honoring of one species indicates its superiority over others, it is weak. Because honoring does not necessitate superiority, especially with His saying: 'And We have favored them over many of those We created, a favor' (3), for it indicates the lack of favor over the few, and it is not other than angels by consensus, especially since He also described angels as honored servants.),
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that claims angels are superior to prophets. The author begins by stating that the prophets' way is direct vision and observation, not indirect statement or correspondence. He then argues that the absence of distractions in the case of prophets is undisputed, and the presence of hardship and pain in their worship, despite no obstacles, is beyond rational comprehension. The author dismisses the claim that the remaining attributes of prophets are weaker and lower as unacceptable. Next, he addresses a common argument: angels have intellect without desire, beasts have desire without intellect, and humans have both. If human desire overcomes intellect, they are lower than beasts, as per the Qur'anic verse 'Nay, they are more astray' (7:179). If intellect overcomes desire, they must be higher than angels. This argument, however, is said to return to a previous point: the disbeliever chooses deficiency despite capability for perfection, and choosing a thing despite obstacles is more significant. Therefore, choosing perfection despite the possibility of deficiency is better than choosing it without that possibility. The author then refutes the use of the verse 'We have honored the children of Adam' (17:70) as proof of human superiority over angels, because honoring does not entail superiority, and the subsequent verse 'We have favored them over many of those We created' (17:70) implies that they are not favored over the few, which are angels by consensus. Moreover, angels are also described as honored servants.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'We have honored the children of Adam' proves that humans are superior to angels.
Correction: The author argues that honoring does not necessitate superiority, and the subsequent verse limits favor to 'many' not 'all', indicating angels are excluded. Additionally, angels are also described as honored.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the verse 'وَلَقَدْ كَرَّمْنَا بَنِي آدَمَ' not prove human superiority over angels?
A) Because the verse refers only to prophets, not all humans.
B) Because honoring does not necessitate superiority, and another verse limits favor to many but not all.
C) Because angels are also honored, so it is equal.
D) Because the verse was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Neither the nature of angels nor the verse of honor definitively proves their superiority over prophets; the prophet's merit lies in overcoming obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you chose to obey Allah despite strong desires pulling you away. Did that act feel ordinary or did it have a special weight? Now imagine a being that never feels those desires at all—like an angel. According to this lesson, why is your struggle more valuable than the angel's effortless obedience? How does that change the way you view your own daily battles with temptation? What can you do today to intentionally choose difficulty in worship, knowing it elevates you above even the angels?
?
The author argues that choosing perfection while capable of deficiency is more virtuous. Consider your own life: are there areas where you choose the easy path of habit over the hard path of growth? In what specific act of worship or character development are you currently settling for 'good enough' when you could strive for excellence? What would it look like to push past your comfort zone—not in a showy way, but sincerely for Allah—starting this week?
?
Angels are described as 'honored servants' (عباد مكرمون). You too are a servant of Allah. But He called them mukhribun (honored). Does the fact that Allah honors angels despite their lack of struggle make you jealous? Or does it remind you that honor is not based on struggle but on Allah's choice? This lesson says that not even the verse 'We have honored the children of Adam' proves superiority. How does that affect your self-image as a Muslim? Are you trying to prove your worth through achievements, or trusting that Allah's honoring of you is sufficient?

First Objection: Verses on Angels' Honor Do Not Prove Superiority
Author presents the first objection and its answer regarding angelic honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by those who claim angelic superiority over prophets, and why it fails to demonstrate superiority.

Core Idea
Honorific verses do not automatically establish superiority over others.

In this passage, the author identifies the first of several objections from opponents (the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, and al-Halimi) who argue that angels are superior to prophets. They cite Quranic verses praising angels' honor, proximity, and obedience. The author gives a concise answer: these verses do not prove superiority. Let's examine the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents and their claim
Who are the opponents mentioned? What is their overall stance? Notice they include figures from within the author's own school.
The opponents are identified as the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, and al-Halimi.
2
Examine the first objection from verses
What qualities of angels do the opponents highlight from the verses? List them: honor, proximity, dignity, persistence in obedience, abandonment of arrogance.
These are five attributes cited from the Quran.
3
Understand the answer
The author responds that these verses do not indicate superiority (afdaliyya). Why might that be? Because describing someone's honor does not compare them to others.
The answer is brief but decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وتمسك المخالفون والمخالفون وهم المعتزلة والقاضي والحليمي منا بوجوه : الأول - الآيات الدالة على شرفهم وقربهم وكرامتهم ومواظبتهم على الطاعة وترك الاستكبار. وأجيب بأنها لا تفيد الأفضلية .
He said: And the opponents clung to proofs, and they are the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, and al-Halimi from us, with faces: The first: The verses indicating their honor, proximity, dignity, persistence in obedience, and abandonment of arrogance. And it was answered that they do not indicate superiority.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage introduces a series of objections from the opponents (identified as the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi, and al-Halimi from our school) who argue that angels are superior to prophets. The first objection is based on verses that describe angels' honor, proximity to God, dignity, continuous obedience, and lack of arrogance. The answer given is that these verses do not prove superiority (afdaliyya) of angels over prophets; they only describe their qualities without comparison.

Second Objection: Prophet’s Denial of Being an Angel
Author presents the second objection from the verse 'Say: I do not say I am an angel', and gives the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur’anic verse ‘Say: I do not say I am an angel’ is interpreted to refute the claim that angels are superior to prophets.

Core Idea
Prophets are not inferior to angels; their denial is specific, not absolute.

Opponents cite the Prophet’s own words to argue that angels are superior because he denies being one. But the answer reveals a deeper meaning: the Prophet is not an angel in the sense of having certain divine powers. This lesson unpacks that response and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the verse cited by opponents: ‘Say: I do not say that I possess the treasures of Allah, nor do I know the unseen, nor do I say that I am an angel.’
This is Quran 6:50.
2
Understand the opponents' inference
Opponents argue that the Prophet denies being an angel, implying that angels are superior and therefore prophets cannot be better.
3
Examine the answer
The answer interprets the verse contextually: the Prophet denies being an angel only in terms of having power to inflict punishment or direct knowledge without revelation.
The answer limits the scope of the denial.
4
Appreciate the distinction
Recognize that the denial does not negate all angelic qualities but only specific powers. This refutes the claim of general inferiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - قوله تعالى : قل لا أقول لكم عندي خزائن الله ولا أعلم الغيب ولا أقول إني ملك) (۱) وأجيب بأن المعنى لست بملك حتى يكون لي القوة والقدرة على إنزال العذاب بإذن الله كما كان بجبريل ، أو يكون لي العلم بذلك بإخبار الله تعالى بلا واسطة .
The second: His saying: 'Say: I do not say to you that I possess the treasures of Allah, nor do I know the unseen, nor do I say that I am an angel' (1). And it was answered that the meaning is: I am not an angel such that I would have the strength and power to inflict punishment by Allah's permission as Gabriel did, or that I would have knowledge of that through Allah's informing without intermediary.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the opponents: the Qur'anic verse (6:50) where the Prophet says he is not an angel, implying that angels are superior. The answer interprets the verse contextually: the Prophet denies being an angel in the sense of having the power to bring down punishment as Gabriel did, or having direct knowledge from God without an intermediary. Thus, it does not imply general superiority.

Key Terms
قل لا أقول
خزائن الله
الغيب
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse proves prophets are inferior to angels because the Prophet denies being an angel.
Correction: The verse is interpreted as denying only specific angelic powers (like inflicting punishment or direct knowledge), not all aspects of angelic nature.

Checkpoint
How is the verse 'قل لا أقول لكم عندي خزائن الله...' interpreted in this passage?
A) It proves that prophets are inferior to angels.
B) It means the Prophet does not have the power of an angel to inflict punishment or direct knowledge.
C) It negates all angelic qualities from the Prophet.
D) It is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the Prophet's denial of being an angel is limited to specific powers, not to the honor of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Prophet say 'I am not an angel', do you ever secretly assume that your own shortcomings are proof that you cannot achieve high spiritual rank? How does the answer—that the denial is about specific powers, not honor—challenge you to stop using humility as an excuse for mediocrity? What is one specific 'angelic quality' (like perfect obedience or pure devotion) that you need to strive for today, because the Prophet still called you to it?
?
Have you ever dismissed your own worth because you didn't possess a certain talent or ability that others had? The Prophet's denial is precise: he didn't claim to be an angel in certain ways, but he didn't deny the glory of prophethood. Where in your life have you made a blanket denial of your value based on one limitation? Will you now embrace the specific role Allah has given you, without comparing yourself to others?

Third Objection: The Tree Incident and Angelic Superiority
Author presents the third objection from the tree verse and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the superiority of prophets over angels based on the tree verse, and the classical response that limits its scope to before Adam's prophethood.

Core Idea
The tree verse only shows angelic superiority before Adam became a prophet.

The third objection draws from Satan's words in the Quran to argue that angels are superior to Adam. However, scholars respond that this is a deceptive suggestion and, even if considered, only applies to the period before Adam's prophethood. This lesson examines the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note that the verse referenced is from Surah al-A'raf (7:20).
The objection is presented as the third argument against the superiority of prophets.
2
Analyze the two-part answer
Identify the two components: (1) the statement is a deceptive suggestion from Satan, and (2) even taken at face value, it only indicates superiority before Adam's prophethood (ba'tha).
The term 'takhyeel' means a deceptive imagining or suggestion.
3
Reflect on the scope of the response
Consider why the qualification 'before the mission' is crucial. It preserves the possibility that prophets are superior after prophethood, which is the core doctrine.
This reasoning aligns with the broader principle that prophets are superior to angels after their prophethood.

Fourth objection: Gabriel as teacher does not imply superiority
Objection and answer regarding Gabriel as teacher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that Gabriel's teaching implies his superiority over the Prophet is refuted by distinguishing the conveyor from the true source of teaching.

Core Idea
A conveyor does not become superior to the one who receives the message.

This passage presents the fourth objection against the Prophet's superiority: the verse describes Gabriel as 'intense in power' and as the one who taught the Prophet, implying that the teacher (Gabriel) is better than the student (the Prophet). The answer clarifies that Gabriel is only a messenger conveying revelation; the true teaching comes from Allah alone. This distinction resolves the apparent implication of superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
The verse reference (3) is to Surah an-Najm 53:5.
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues: Gabriel (Jibril) is called 'intense in power' and is described as having taught the Prophet (sallallahu 'alayhi wa sallam). Since a teacher is better than a student, Gabriel would be superior to the Prophet.
The key phrase is 'al-mu'allimu afḍal' (the teacher is better).
3
Understand the answer
The answer refutes by saying Gabriel is merely a muballigh (conveyor). The true teaching (ta'līm) is from Allah alone. Thus, the verse does not attribute superiority to Gabriel; he is just a medium.
The answer uses 'innamā' to restrict teaching to Allah.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the general principle: the one who transmits knowledge is not necessarily superior to the recipient. The source of knowledge is what matters. This protects the belief in the Prophet's superiority over all creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - علمه شديد القوى ) (۳) يعني جبريل، والمعلم أفضل . وأجيب بأنه مبلغ، وإنما التعليم من الله .
The fourth: 'Taught him one intense in power' (3), meaning Gabriel, and the teacher is better. And it was answered that he is a conveyor, and teaching is only from Allah.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: the verse (53:5) describes Gabriel as 'intense in power' and the one who taught the Prophet, implying that the teacher (Gabriel) is superior to the student (Prophet). The answer differentiates: Gabriel is merely a conveyor (muballigh) of revelation; the true teaching originates from God alone. Thus, the verse does not imply Gabriel's superiority over the Prophet.

Misconception: The teacher is always superior to the student, so Gabriel is superior to the Prophet because he taught him.
Correction: A teacher who only conveys a message (muballigh) is not necessarily superior to the recipient of the message. The true source of knowledge is Allah, and the Prophet's rank is higher because he receives directly from God.

The Linguistic Nuance in the Verse on Messiah and Angels
Objection and answer regarding angels' status in verse 4:172

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic argument used in the fifth objection regarding verse 4:172 and how the author refutes it by reinterpreting the comparison.

Core Idea
Mentioning angels after Jesus does not prove general superiority, only specific qualities.

This lesson examines the fifth objection against prophethood, where opponents use Qur'an 4:172 to claim angels are superior to prophets because of a linguistic construction. We will see how the author answers by reinterpreting the comparison to focus on specific reasons for not disdaining worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the linguistic rule the opponents invoke: in Arabic, when saying 'neither X nor Y', Y is usually higher in rank. They apply this to Jesus and angels in the verse.
Think of examples like 'the prince and those above him'.
3
Analyze the author's response
Notice how the author redefines the basis of comparison. The 'above' in the verse refers to having greater reasons not to disdain, such as being born without parents or possessing greater powers.
This shifts the argument from general rank to specific attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - لن يستنكف المسيح أن يكون عبد ا لله ولا الملائكة المقربون فإنه يقال : لا يترفع عن هذا الأمر الأمير ولا من فوقه. ولا يقال : ولا من هو دونه . وأجيب بأن مثله إنما يفيد الزيادة فيما جعل سبباً للترفع والاستنكاف، ككون عيسى (عليه السلام) ولد بلا أب وأبرأ الأكمه والأبرص. فالمعنى ولا من هو فوقه الملائكة الذين لا أب لهم ولا أم، ويقدرون على ما لا يقدر عليه عيسى (عليه السلام) .
The fifth: 'The Messiah will not disdain to be a servant of Allah, nor the angels nearest' (4:172). For it is said: The prince and those above him do not refrain from this matter, and it is not said: nor those below him. And it was answered that such an expression only indicates increase in that which is made a cause for disdain and refraining, such as Jesus (peace be upon him) being born without a father and healing the blind and the leper. So the meaning is: nor those above him, the angels who have no father or mother, and are able to do what Jesus cannot do.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection: the verse (4:172) states that neither the Messiah nor the angels nearest disdain to worship Allah. The opponents argue that in Arabic, when saying 'neither X nor Y', Y is usually higher in rank. For example, one says 'the prince does not refrain, nor those above him', not 'nor those below him'. Thus, angels are mentioned after the Messiah in this context, indicating they are higher. The answer explains that the expression is used to indicate an increase in the specific reason for not disdaining. In the case of Jesus, his reasons are unique birth and miracles; for angels, they lack any earthly means of pride (no parents) and possess powers Jesus does not. So the meaning is that even those above Jesus in these aspects (the angels) do not disdain, but this does not prove general superiority.

Key Terms

المسيح
الملائكة المقربون
لا يترفع
الأمير
من فوقه
سبب
الترفع
الاستنكاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mentioning angels after Jesus in the verse automatically proves their superiority in all respects.
Correction: The verse only indicates superiority in the specific cause for not disdaining worship (such as being without parents), not in overall rank.

Checkpoint
What is the linguistic argument made by the opponents in the fifth objection?
A) That Jesus is mentioned before angels, so he is superior.
B) That in such constructions, the second mentioned is usually higher in rank.
C) That the verse denies any disdain, so all are equal.
D) That angels are not mentioned explicitly.

Key Takeaway
The verse does not establish a general hierarchy but highlights specific reasons for not disdaining worship, with angels having even greater reasons than Jesus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect on one specific act of worship you have been neglecting, and tonight, perform it with full consciousness that you are emulating the Messiah and the angels.
?
The opponents used a linguistic argument to claim superiority. But the answer shows that even with greater causes, they did not disdain. Is there an act of worship that you have been avoiding because you feel it is beneath your dignity or level of faith? How can you change that mindset starting today, knowing that the highest of creation did not disdain servitude?

Why Angels Are Mentioned Before Prophets
Author presents the sixth objection that angels are always mentioned first, and answers with reasons of temporal priority or faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why angels are consistently mentioned before prophets in the Qur'an and the two rational explanations given by theologians.

Core Idea
Mentioning angels first does not prove superiority—it may indicate temporal priority or the need for greater faith.

This lesson addresses a common objection: if prophets are superior, why are angels always mentioned first? Sanusi presents the objection and gives two nuanced answers that show precedence in mention does not automatically imply superiority. This teaches us to distinguish between textual patterns and theological doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style: the objection is stated, then answered with two possibilities.
2
Identify the two reasons for angelic precedence
The answer gives two distinct reasons: temporal priority in existence, and the strength of faith required because angels are hidden from sight. List them in your own words.
Temporal priority means angels were created before prophets.
3
Reflect on the logic of the argument
Ask yourself: why would 'hiddenness' increase the strength of faith needed? How does this relate to the broader question of superiority?
The objection assumes that textual order implies rank, but the answer shows other valid explanations.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can distinguish between the given reasons and a false one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس - اطراد تقديم ذكرهم على ذكر الأنبياء. وأجيب بأنه لتقدمهم في الوجود، او في قوة الإيمان بهم لخفاء امرهم .
The sixth: The consistent precedence of their mention over the mention of the prophets. And it was answered that it is due to their priority in existence, or in the strength of belief in them due to the hiddenness of their matter.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection: angels are consistently mentioned before prophets in the Qur'anic text, suggesting their superiority. The answer offers two plausible reasons: (1) temporal priority – angels were created before prophets; (2) the strength of belief in them is greater because their nature is hidden (khafa' amrihim), meaning that since humans cannot see angels, they require stronger faith to believe in them, so they are mentioned first to emphasize their status.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that whenever angels are mentioned before prophets in the Qur'an, it proves angels are superior to prophets.
Correction: The passage shows that the consistent precedence can be explained by temporal priority (angels created first) or by the stronger faith needed for hidden beings, not by superiority.

Checkpoint

B) Stronger faith is required for belief in angels because they are hidden.
C) Angels are more virtuous than prophets.
D) Both A and B are mentioned.

Key Takeaway
Angels are mentioned first because they existed first or because believing in them demands stronger faith, not because they are superior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Eighth Argument and Its Response
Taftazani presents and counters the eighth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth argument for angelic superiority based on their deeds and knowledge, and the foundational response that prophets' struggles make them more deserving of reward.

Core Idea
Prophets' hardships make their deeds more meritorious than angels' effortless obedience.

Taftazani begins his refutation by addressing one of the most common arguments for angelic superiority: the quantity and quality of their worship. He then provides a concise response that shifts the criterion from mere quantity to the context of struggle. This lesson presents that argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage stating that angels' deeds are more numerous, enduring, and upright, and their knowledge more complete.
2
Understand the response
Notice that the response argues that deeds accompanied by subduing the contrary and bearing hardships are more deserving of reward, implying prophets face greater trials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن - أن أعمالهم أكثر وأدوم وأقوم، وعلومهم أكمل وأكثر. وأجيب بأن المقرون بقهر المضاد وتحمل المشاق أدخل في استحقاق الثواب . ) . أيضاً بوجوه نقلية وعقلية، أما النقليات فمنها قوله تعالى: ﴿ والله يسجد ما في السموات وما في الأرض من دابة والملائكة وهم لايستكبرون . يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون (1) خصصهم بالتواضع، وترك الاستكبار في السجود. وفيه إشارة إلى أن غيرهم ليس كذلك، وأن أسباب التكبر والتعظم حاصلة لهم، ووصفهم باستمرار الخوف وامتثال الأوامر ومن جملتها اجتناب المنهيات . ومنها قوله تعالى : " ومن عنده لا يستكبرون عن عبادته ولايستحسرون . يسبحون الليل والنهار لا يفترون) (۲) وصفهم بالقرب والشرف عنده وبالتواضع والمواظبة على الطاعة والتسبيح . ومنها قوله تعالى : بل عباد مكرمون لا يسبقونه بالقول وهم بأمره يعملون ) (۳) .. إلى أن قال: وهم من خشيته مشفقون. وخصهم بالكرامة المطلقة والامتثال والخشية وهذه الأمور أساس كافة الخيرات . والجواب أن جميع ذلك إنما يدل على فضيلتهم، لا أفضليتهم، سيما على الأنبياء، ومنها قوله تعالى: قل لا أقول لكم عندي خزائن الله ولا أعلم الغيب ولا أقول إني ملك (١) فإن مثل هذا الكلام إنما يحسن إذا كان الملك أفضل والجواب أنه إنما قال حين استعجله قريش العذاب الذي أوعدوا به بقوله تعالى : والذين كذبوا بآياتنا يمسهم العذاب بما كانوا يفسقون) (۲) والمعنى أني لست بملك حتى يكون لي القوة والقدرة على إنزال العذاب بإذن الله كما كان لجبرئيل (عليه السلام)، أو يكون لي العلم بذلك بإخبار من الله بلا واسطة ومنها قوله تعالى : ( ما نهاكما ربكما عن هذه الشجرة إلا أن تكونا ملكين ) (۳). أي إلا كراهة أن تكونا ملكين. يعني أن الملكية بالمرتبة العليا، وفي الأكل من الشجرة ارتقاء إليها . والجواب أن ذلك تمويه من الشيطان وتخييل أن ما يشاهد في الملك من حسن الصورة، وعظم الخلق ، وكمال القوة يحصل بأكل الشجرة. ولوسلم فغاية التفضيل على آدم (عليه السلام) قبل النبوة. ( ومنها قوله تعالى : عَلَّمه شديد القوى ) ( ٤ ) . يعني جبرئيل (عليه السلام والمعلم أفضل من المتعلم . والجواب ان ذلك بطريق التبليغ، وإنما التعليم من الله تعالى. ومنها قوله تعالى: لن يستنكف المسيح أن يكون عبداً لله ولا الملائكة المقربون (٥) .
Eighth: That their deeds are more numerous, more enduring, and more upright, and their knowledge is more complete and abundant. And it was answered that what is accompanied by subduing the contrary and bearing hardships is more deserving of reward. Also by transmitted and rational proofs. As for transmitted proofs, among them is His saying, Exalted is He: 'And to Allah prostrates whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth of creatures, and the angels, and they are not arrogant. They fear their Lord above them and do what they are commanded' (1). He distinguished them with humility and abandonment of arrogance in prostration. And in it is an indication that others are not like that, and that the causes of pride and self-magnification are present for them. And He described them with continuous fear and compliance with commands, among which is avoidance of prohibitions. And among them is His saying, Exalted is He: 'And those who are with Him are not arrogant toward His worship, nor do they become weary. They glorify [Him] night and day, never flagging' (2). He described them with proximity and honor with Him, and with humility and persistence in obedience and glorification. And among them is His saying, Exalted is He: 'Rather, they are honored servants. They do not precede Him in speech, and they act by His command' (3)... until He said: 'And they are, from fear of Him, apprehensive.' And He singled them out with absolute honor, compliance, and fear, and these matters are the foundation of all good. The answer is that all of that only indicates their virtue (fadilah), not their superiority (afdaliyyah), especially over the prophets. And among them is His saying, Exalted is He: 'Say, I do not say to you that I possess the treasures of Allah, nor do I know the unseen, nor do I say to you that I am an angel' (1). For such speech is only appropriate if the angel is superior. And the answer is that he only said that when the Quraysh hastened him regarding the punishment that they were threatened with, by His saying, Exalted is He: 'And those who deny Our signs – the punishment will touch them because they used to defy' (2). The meaning is that I am not an angel so that I would have strength and power to send down punishment with Allah's permission, as was the case for Jibril (peace be upon him), or that I would have knowledge of that through a report from Allah without intermediary. And among them is His saying, Exalted is He: 'Your Lord did not forbid you this tree except that you become angels' (3). That is, except out of dislike that you become angels, meaning that angelhood is the supreme rank, and in eating from the tree there is ascension to it. The answer is that that is a sophistry from Satan and a suggestion that what is observed in the angel of beauty of form, greatness of creation, and perfection of power is obtained by eating the tree. And if it were granted, the utmost is superiority over Adam (peace be upon him) before prophethood. And among them is His saying, Exalted is He: 'Taught by one severe in power' (4), meaning Jibril (peace be upon him), and the teacher is better than the student. The answer is that that is by way of delivering, and teaching is only from Allah, Exalted is He. And among them is His saying, Exalted is He: 'The Messiah would never disdain to be a servant of Allah, nor would the angels brought near [to Him]' (5).
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers regarding the superiority of angels over prophets. It begins with the eighth objection: that angels' deeds are more numerous, enduring, and upright, and their knowledge is more complete. The response states that deeds accompanied by overcoming opposition and bearing hardships are more deserving of reward—implying that prophets, who face greater trials, are more meritorious.

Then the author introduces transmitted and rational proofs for angelic superiority. The first transmitted proof is the verse from Surah an-Nahl (16:49-50), where angels prostrate humbly and obey constantly. The author interprets this as indicating their virtue but not superiority. Next is the verse from al-Anbiya (21:19-20) describing angels' continuous worship, again interpreted as virtue. Then the verse from al-Anbiya (21:26-28) calling them honored servants. The author concludes that all these verses only prove their virtue (fadilah), not absolute superiority (afdaliyyah), especially over prophets.

The author then addresses specific verses that might imply angelic superiority. The verse from al-An'am (6:50) where the Prophet (PBUH) says he does not claim to have treasures or be an angel is explained: it was said when the Quraysh demanded immediate punishment, meaning he lacked the angelic power to deliver it. Next, the verse from al-A'raf (7:20) where Iblees tempts Adam by saying the tree would make them angels is dismissed as a deception of Satan. Even if granted, it would only show angels' superiority over Adam before prophethood. The verse from an-Najm (53:5) that Jibril taught the Prophet is answered by noting that teaching is only from Allah, and Jibril is a messenger. Finally, the verse from an-Nisa (4:172) about the Messiah and angels not disdaining worship is likely presented as a proof of angelic piety, but the text cuts off before the response, implying a continuation.

The passage follows a systematic pattern: each proof for angelic superiority is presented and then refuted, either by reinterpreting the verse or by contextualizing its revelation.

Key Terms
ملائكة
أنبياء
فضيلة
أفضلية
استحقاق الثواب
تمويه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key factor that makes deeds more deserving of reward?
A) Numerousness and endurance
B) Being accompanied by subduing the contrary and bearing hardships
C) Being performed by angels
D) Having complete knowledge

Key Takeaway
The eighth argument is refuted by pointing out that prophets' deeds are more meritorious due to the hardships they endure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face opposition to your faith—whether from family, society, or your own desires—do you see it as a burden to be avoided, or as the very thing that could elevate your deeds? How might you shift your perspective based on this lesson?
?
Think of a recent act of worship that required genuine struggle. Did you consider that struggle as diminishing your worship, or did you recognize it as increasing its value? What would change if you embraced hardship as a gift?
?
